O, sons, since I have completed for you this book, which is the culmination of the science of medicine, by its completion, its consequences, and its explanations, it seems to me that I should fulfill my duty to you with this treatise, which is a part of manual operation, that is, surgery.
Because manual operation is almost completely lost in our region and in our time, to the point that its science is in great peril, and its practice is obscured; and there remain of it only some small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which are mostly corrupted and lead to errors and hesitation, so that their intentions are unclear and their power and art are weakened.
And it seems to me that I should revive it with the ordering of this treatise, according to the way of exposition, explanation, and abbreviation; and so that it may come with the forms of the cautery instruments and the other instruments of the work, as it should be by the addition of explanation, and by its preparation, which is a necessary cause.
But the reason why a skilled artisan is not found in our time is that the art of medicine is long, and that its practitioner must first be exercised in the science of anatomy, which Gualia has recognized, so that he knows the arrangement of the members and their forms, and their connection and separation; and he must have knowledge of the bones, nerves, and ligaments, and their number, and their arrangement; and of the pulsating and quiet veins, and the places of their emergence.
And for this reason Hippocrates says that with one name much is said, but little in the work, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already said this at the beginning of this book; for whoever is not learned in what we have said about anatomy is not exempt from falling into error, by which men will be killed, just as I have seen many of them, because some are trained in this science and undertake it without knowledge and experience.
For I saw a foolish and inexperienced physician incise a scrofulous abscess in the neck of a woman, and he incised some arteries of the neck, so that much blood flowed until the woman died in his hands.
And I saw another physician who undertook to remove a stone from a man who had reached old age, and the stone was large, and he came to him and extracted that stone along with a piece of the bladder, so that that man died on the third day.
And I was called to extract it, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, I knew that he would die.
And I saw another physician who received a stipend from a duke of our land for surgery, and a black eunuch came to him who had a fracture in his thigh; and the physician, through his ignorance, extracted the fracture, over the wound, with cushions and splints with strong compression, and did not allow the wound to breathe.
And then he unbound it according to his wishes, and then he bound it again, and then he left it like that for some days and ordered that we not unbind the bandage; and it remained so until the thigh and foot became abscessed, and it resulted in loss (of the limb/life).
And I was called to him, and I prepared to unbind the bandage, and calmness was achieved, and thus a lessening of his pains.
But however, corruption was already confirmed in the limb, so that I could not restrain it, and the corruption did not cease to spread in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who perforated a cancerous abscess, and it was ulcerated after some days, until its malignancy was magnified by its master (i.e., the disease worsened under his care).
The reason is that the hot cancer is pure melancholic humor, therefore it is not fitting that it be treated with iron at all, but rather it should be in a limb which suffers so that it may be entirely contained (or stopped).
And therefore, sons, it is necessary for you that the operation with the hand be divided into two parts: that is, into an operation which is associated with health, and into a work with which there is peril in the greatest dispositions (or circumstances).
And I have already explained this in every part of this book where operations occur which involve error and fear; therefore it is necessary for you to observe this and leave that, so that fools find no way to speak and to dishonor you.
Therefore, take up your instruments with diligence and with God's protection, and treat your patients with happiness and firmness.
And use the best method, by the grace of God, leading to health and praiseworthy success, and leave the terrible illnesses of difficult healing, and keep your instruments from that which makes you fear, so that hesitation in your faith and in your name may not overcome you: for it is more enduring to your glory, and higher in the world and behind your holiness.
Galen already said in some of his admonitions: Do not treat a bad illness, lest you be called a bad physician".
And I have divided this book according to three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter deals with the head down to the feet, the forms of instruments and the iron cauteries, and what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of incisions and perforations, and of cupping glasses, and of wounds, and of the extraction of arrows and similar things, all chaptered and ordered according to the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, of restoration and dislocation, and of the treatment of sprains and similar things; a chapter ordered from head to foot, and of the forms of the instruments.
It is necessary that I say the quality of the benefits of that cautery and its harms, and in what constitution cautery is prohibited.
Therefore I say that the discourse on the quality of the benefit of cautery and its harm is a long and prolix discourse, and subtle in science, and a hidden secret; and many wise men have already spoken of it, and they disagree about it, and a little of their discourses suffices for me, fearing prolixity.
And therefore I say that cautery benefits all complexions universally, which are with matter or without matter, except two complexions, which are hot complexions without matter.
In the hot and dry complexion with matter, there is disagreement; for some say that cautery is helpful for that, and others say the contrary, that cautery is not convenient in an illness which is by quality and by dryness, because the nature of fire is quality and dryness, and it is inconvenient that a hot and dry illness should be healed with that which is hot and dry.
And I contradict those who say the contrary, that benefit is done by cautery in every hot and dry illness that comes to the bodies of men, because when you compare the body of man and its humors to the complexion of fire, you find the body of man cold.
And I say, concerning the discourse of that, that experience has revealed this to me several times.
However, it is not fitting that this should come to someone who is already experienced and skilled in the chapter on cautery through prior practice, and who has known the diversity of the complexions of men and the disposition of illnesses in themselves, and the causes of those, and their accidents, and the span of time of those.
But in other complexions, there is no fear for you, and especially in cold and humid illnesses, because all physicians are agreed on those, and are not disagreed on the benefit with cautery in those.
And know, sons, that the secret of healing by cautery with fire, and of its goodness, is superior to cautery with burning medicine, because fire is simple, the action of which does not reach except the member which is cauterized and does not harm the other member next to it except with little harm.
But the action of burning medicine reaches to those members which are distant from it, and quickly brings to the member an illness of difficult healing and quickly kills.
But fire, by its sublimity and the goodness of its substance, does not do this, except to surpass it. And it is known to us through experiment and through long service of study with the art on the truth of the causes; and for that I am excused from the length of speech. And if it were not that it is not written in my book in this place, a secret hidden from you with fire, and the quality of its operation in bodies, and its expulsion by which it expels illnesses, with philosophical demonstrative speech, which would be acute in your thoughts.
And know, sons, that they disagree on the time in which it is convenient to perform cautery, and they place the best time in springtime.
And I say that cautery is convenient at all times, because the harm due to accidental time is submerged in the benefit of that which is obtained with cautery, and especially if the cautery is for pains, or necessary, strong and urgent pains, which do not tolerate delay, because it is feared that from those a malignancy will come, which is greater than the small harm entering through time.
And this does not enter your thoughts, O sons, this is only esteemed by the vulgar people and foolish doctors, that is, that to that illness which is healed by cautery, there is no remission at all times, and they posit that consequence.
And the cause is not at all as they think for that reason, for cautery is only in place of medicine which alters the complexion and dries the humors which are the cause of the pains.
However, cautery surpasses medicine in the speed of its effectiveness and virtue of its operation and greatness of its power.
And it is possible that his illness will return at some time, according to the complexion of the patient and the location of his illness, and his strength, and that which is prepared in his body from the aggregation of superfluities in him, and that which has come to him in his acquisition of food and similar causes; unless the illness in which cautery is administered is a subtle illness in a member with few superfluities and humors, such as cautery of a tooth for pain and similar to that, it is possible that that pain will not return to him, but this happens in small things.
And a more common saying is that cautery is the last resort of medicine, and it is a true saying, not in the way they understand it, for they believe that it is a cure that does not benefit with medicine or with any other thing besides cautery.
And the reason is contrary to that; and the intention that cautery is the last resort of medicine is only when we administer methods of cure in certain illnesses, and those medicines are not effective, and then at the end of the process, one administers cautery, and it is beneficial for that. And therefore, from this it is understood that cautery is the last resort of medicine, not according to the understanding of the common people and most foolish doctors.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except for the equality of gold and the sublimity of its substance.
And they said that the cauterized area does not scar, although this is not entirely true, for I have already tested this and found that it does not do so except in some bodies and not others, and cautery with gold is good and better than with iron as they said; however, when you cauterize with fire with gold, it is not clearly shown to you, for it is consumed according to the amount you desire due to the gold's softness, and because it readily absorbs heat; and if you apply too much heat to it, the gold melts from the fire, and some laminas fall into the fire so that the artisan is distracted by this.
And therefore, cautery with iron is done more easily and more appropriately for the correct operation.
And I have already ordered this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and I have ordered them from the head to the feet, so that I might readily give the petitioner what he desires.
The first chapter.
This cauterization counteracts the humidity and coldness on the brain, the accidents of which are of two causes: that is, the cause of excessive and copious rheumy flux, called coryza, flowing from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears, and excessive sleep, and toothache and throat pain, and universally of every illness that comes from coldness, such as paralysis, sudden stroke and apoplexy, and similar illnesses.
The form of this cauterization is this: firstly, order the patient to be evacuated with purgative medicine, purifying the head, for three or four nights, according to the strength of the patient, his age, and his constitution.
And then order him to shave his head with a razor, and then have him sit between your hands on a low bench and place his hands on his feet.
And then place the root of your palm near the hollow above the root of the nose between his eyes. However, when your middle finger has reached that place, mark that spot with a pen, and then ignite the olive-shaped cautery, of which this is the form:
And then place that [cautery] on the spot marked with the pen, in such a way that it descends, with which your hand should be gradually tightened, and you should rotate it.
And then lift your hand lightly and observe the area, and if you see some quantity of the bone's head uncovered, like the amount of a grain of barley, then raise your hand, and if not, return your hand with that iron, or with another one if this one is cooled, until that which I have told you is seen of the bone.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it on the area and leave it for three days.
And then place on it cotton soaked in vinegar and leave it there until the fire scar is destroyed. And then treat the area with tetrafarmacon ointment until it is healed.
And it is said that the longer the wound remains open, the better it throws off the arm, because it is better and more beneficial.
And some of them say that the fistula should be cauterized to the bone, and the cautery should be held there until some part of the bone's surface is hardened/burned.
And then scrape away what is burned from the bone, and then it should be treated/cured.
And others say that with the cautery, one should reach the bottom, until it firmly penetrates the bone, until the bone bleeds, and this is the form of a small abscess. And they believe that vapors from the head exhale from that place.
And the wound should be kept open for a long time, and then it should be healed until it is consolidated (healed completely).
But I have not seen these two types of cautery at all, except in some men as a way of fear; and leave that, it seems to me, better is health, when it is; because the head is weakened by the solution of its continuity, as we have testified in other members, and especially when the patient's head is of a cold nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better, in my opinion; and I use that and work with that for health.
Chapter Two.
Concerning the cautery of the head.
When chronic and prolonged pain occurs in the entire head, and this is prolonged in the patient, and he uses leeches and cupping and purgatives and plasters and potions and poultices and ointments and similar things, and especially if he has already been cauterized with a cautery which we have recounted and none of these things benefit him, then consider whether the patient's head is of strong constitution by nature; and if it is not cold and finds great cold, then cauterize that with another cautery a little below that one. And then cauterize that above the horn of his head with a cautery until the scab is parted. Incise the scab and let the bone be uncovered to the extent that we have recounted, and cauterize that with a cautery in the rear part of the head in the place which is called the vas – and this is the place where a cupping glass is placed. And lighten your hand in this, and do not uncover the bone, for the patient thereby finds much greater pain, different from the pain of all other cauteries of the head.
And I say this cautery in its place, for it is necessary that the cautery with which the horns of the head and the occiput are cauterized is a more subtle cautery than that with which the middle of the head is cauterized; and this is its form.
Chapter Three.
On the cauterization of recent (or new) migraine.
When a sudden, sharp pain occurs in the head, or a spreading pain within the eye, let the head be evacuated with purifying medicines, and let the other treatment be administered, which I have described in the descriptions of diseases; and if nothing helps, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine, or with iron.
But cauterization with a burning medicine is that you take a clove of garlic, and peel it and cut off its extremities from both ends. Then incise the location of the pain in the temple with a lancet, until a place is made under the skin, until it is hidden.
Then bind it tightly with a compress for continuous constriction, and leave it for the space of three hours; and then untie it and remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, and then place on it cotton soaked in boiling water until the area becomes inflamed.
And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines which I have written about in the twelfth treatise on burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron in this manner:  ignite the cautery, of which this is the form:
and it is called a nail or peg, because its head is like the shape of a clove, in which there is a certain curvature, and in the middle of it is a small eminence.
And then place it on the location of the pain, and hold your hand — so that the burning, which is below, is not intensified — and move the iron little by little with speed.
And if the amount of it that is burned by the sharpness of the cautery is only half of it, and lift your hand so that the artery beneath is not burned, so that a flow of blood does not occur.
And then soak cotton in salt water and place that on the area, and leave it for three days; and then apply cotton moistened with butter, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the tip of a knife as with a cautery, and beware of cutting the artery in this particular, not-ancient, migraine.
Chapter four.
On the cauterization of chronic migraine.
When you treat a migraine with what we have said in the preceding treatment, and what we have said in the divisions of diseases, and the treatment is not effective, and sometimes because of the disease it is not enough to use what we said about the first cautery with medicine or the cautery with fire, then it is necessary that you heat the knife cautery until it is white-hot, after you mark the location of the pain with a line whose length is half a finger, or whatever; and place your hand for a moment and press it until you incise the artery and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to be careful of the continuation of the jaw, which is moved during mastication, and that the muscle not be injured and the nerve which serves it, and that spasm does not occur.
And if it is about the care and caution of the flow of blood from the artery that is incised, because in its incision there is danger, and especially for him who is ignorant of what he does and is not studious and experienced; and leaves the operation to him at the beginning.
And I will come with a reminder of the treatment of accidental blood flow from the artery according to its manner, in its place in this book.
And if sometimes this disease is not relieved by this cautery and the patient's body is tolerable, cauterize it in the middle of the head as we have recounted, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you wish, administer the cautery which we have mentioned in the chapter on the constriction of an artery with a cautery having two blades, and this cauterization is better and more suitable.
Chapter 5.
Chapter 5: On the cauterization of earaches.
When pain in the ear occurs due to cold and is treated with laxative remedies and with the other treatments which I have mentioned in the division, and the pain is not relieved, then apply the cautery, and this is the one called "puncta," of which this is the form:
And then apply the puncture, then the cauterization, and let it be carried around the entire ear as a wheel is revolved, or in the circumference of each part if the pain is localized. Lengthen the cautery from the root of the ear little by little after you have marked the place with a probe.
And if the cautery makes ten punctures in the whole ear, or whichever ear it may be, then treat the areas until they are healed.
Chapter 6.
On the cauterization of a cough.
A cough which is cured with cauterization is only one of the types which arise from phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of diseases.
Avoid cauterization of the type of cough that originates from dryness or from a spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that type of cough with leeches and purgatives and gargles, and your treatment is ineffective, then you should cauterize the patient with three cauteries: one at the root of the ear, the second a little below the temple, and the third at the junction of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side from the affected area, because softening [of the humors, or healing] only occurs on the side that appears healthy.
And the manner of cauterization is that you cauterize it with the cautery at the very tip of the upper ear, a little above the temple, and at the temple another, the length of which should be according to the length of the finger, and hold your hand with the cautery until the amount of half the thickness of the fingernail is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a type of knife, the form of which is shown previously [in a diagram, illustration, or earlier in the text]. However, it is more subtle to do it a little like this; and it is necessary to cut away some superfluous thickness.
And then care for the area with what was previously described until it is healed.
Chapter 7.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic apoplexy.
When apoplexy is chronic and you treat it with what we have said, and your medication is ineffective, and the patient has no fever, then cauterize it with four cauteries:  on all the corners of his head; and with a cautery in the middle of the head, as we have said; and with a cautery on the neck. And the manner of cauterization is done as described above.
And it is now cauterized above the mouth of the stomach; and it is better afterwards; and then let it be cured with what follows.
Chapter VIII.
Concerning the cauterization of forgetfulness (amnesia) done by flame.
So that large enemas and purifying pills are given to the patient at the beginning.
And then shave the entire head of the patient and apply to their neck a plaster of cetre [a type of plaster/ointment] described in the treatise on plasters.
And apply that several times, for that is a manner of cauterization. And do this according to the same order which I have already said.
And therefore if he is healed by those means, it is good; and if not, then make for him three cauterizations in the back part of his head, which descend from the highest part of the head down to the lowest part of the neck. And place between each cauterization the width of a finger, and then treat the cauterization with what has been prescribed.
But if you see more inflammation and the patient is able to tolerate it, then cauterize him in the middle area; and if you see even greater inflammation, then cauterize above the two temples.
Then treat that until it is healed; and let the cauterization be olive-shaped according to the form given before.
Chapter 9.
On the cauterization of paralysis and the softening of the whole body.
So that you proceed in the purification of the head with leeches and with what we have said; and then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with a cautery on all the temples of the head, and with a cautery on the back part of it, and with three cauteries on the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary in the illness of softening of the body to find that and the patients are suffering and the illness is severe, then cauterize it with four cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and finally with a cautery until it is burned from the tail bone, and lift your hand. And then care for it until it is healed, and this should be an olive cautery.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the cauterization of epilepsy.
The epileptic is not cauterized unless that epilepsy is caused by phlegm.
And therefore it is necessary that you purify his brain at the beginning with herbs and with the other treatment which we have described in the section when the patient is strong and tolerates the purging of medicines.
And if it is a child who does not tolerate medicines, then administer gargles and masticatories that purify the brain, before that for many days, with improvement of the diet.
Then shave the head of the patient, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head according to the previous method, and with another cautery on the occiput, and cauterize above each temple. And if the patient is strong and tolerant, then cauterize him with the cauteries which I have mentioned, having paralysis and softening of the body over the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped, as has been previously described.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a small cautery according to this method.
Chapter 11.
On the cauterization of melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupt humors and a thick phlegm, then cauterize it with the cauteries which we have described in the treatment of paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is a superfluity tending towards melancholy, and the patient's body is humid, then give him something to drink that cleanses his brain, according to what has been described previously in the division (of treatments).
Then shave the patient's head, and then make a properly-made poultice for him on a circle (of cloth), and then place that in the middle of the head.
And let the patient be seated upright, and be held on all sides.
Then apply a liniment; and it is best to use old sheep's fat. And heat that over the fire to a moderate temperature, which the said sufferer can bear when it is applied to him. Then pour that into the middle of his head within the circle, and leave it there until it is cold.
And do this to the patient once a week, along with his other treatment, until he is healed; or, if you wish, cauterize it by making small punctures according to his need,  but hold your hand with a cautery thus prepared: this is a type of cautery which moistens the brain evenly.
Then apply a cotton ball soaked in butter or goose grease.
Chapter 12.
On the cauterization of water descending into the eye.
When the beginning of watery discharge in the eye is apparent to you, through the signs which I have mentioned in the division, then begin and give the patient something to purify his head, and protect him from all humidities.
And make him sweat in a bath for several days.
And then order him to shave his head, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head; and then cauterize it with two cauteries on the two temples if the onset of the watery discharge is in both eyes, or on one side if the onset of the water is in one eye.
Incise with a cautery all the veins and arteries which are below the occiput; and let the cauteries be far apart in the width of the temples.
And beware of excessive bleeding when it is cauterized; and if you see excessive bleeding, stop it with any medicine that is available to you.
And I come with wisdom which is in the extraction of arteries and with the excision of them and caution against excessive bleeding.
And sometimes it is cauterized in the neck below the two bones with two cauteries, separately applied.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the cauterization of old tears (likely referring to fistulas or chronic sores near the eyes).
When the tears from the eyes are old and persistent, and through the veins and arteries which are in the manifest location of the head and it is verified that this is a superfluity of cold phlegmatic humors, then cauterize that with the cautery which we have recounted in the initial chapter concerning water falling into the eyes, and with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with two cauteries in the neck below the two temples, and with two cauteries in the neck below the two bones. And if it requires additional treatment, then cauterize it at the corner of the eye, above the end of the eyebrow, with a small cautery.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the cauterization of nasal odor (or a bad smell from the nose).
When you treat this with what we have said in the division and your treatment is ineffective, then prepare and give the patient potions to drink for three nights; and then shave his head, and cauterize it with a moderate-sized olive-shaped cautery. And then cauterize it with a clove-shaped cautery, with two cauterizations above the two eyebrows, slightly below the hair.
And beware of burning too deeply so that you do not damage the underlying tissue.
And this is the shape of the clove-shaped cautery.
Chapter 15.
Concerning the cauterization for the softening of the eyelids.
When the eyelid is softened by illness or humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with a lunar cautery; and this is its form:
And if you cauterize, do so above the two eyebrows a little, with two cauteries on each side, and let them be elongated towards the temples. And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the eyebrow.
And do not press too hard with your hand and the cautery, but only to the extent that a third part of the eyelid is hardened/cauterized.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the cauterization of the eyelid of the eye when the hairs are turned inwards and prick the eye.
When the hairs of the eye are turned inwards so that they prick the eye, cauterization in those cases is done in two ways, for cauterization is either done with fire or with a burning medicine.
If you cauterize with fire, instruct the patient beforehand to shave their eyelashes like those who clean them, until they are lengthened and equalized; and if it pricks near their roots, then you should bind the patient's eyes with a bandage so that they are not injured until the hairs have grown back.
Then, when they have grown back and are equalized, place the patient's head in your lap, and then mark on the eyelid of their eyes with a probe, with a mark which is like the shape of a myrtle leaf, and let the beginning of the mark be near the hairs.
And then place a cotton ball soaked in egg white or in mallow mucilage on the eye.
And then heat the cautery, of which this is the form.
And then cauterize over the mark that you have made, little by little, in several times, until the surface of the eyelid, which is like a myrtle leaf, is completely burned.
And a sign of the success of your work is that you see the eyelid already contracted and the hairs raised above the puncture of the eye.
Then raise your hand and leave it for three days, and then place cotton with ointment on it until the scar is healed. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if something returns through the hairs near their origin and the eyelid is softened, then apply the cautery again in the same way as you did initially.
But if the hairs are below the eyelid, then cauterize it until it is raised to its natural place and is even, and the hairs do not prick the eye.
But cauterize with a burning medicine; that is, order the patient to pull the hairs until they are elongated and even, and then make a myrtle leaf-shaped dressing from a piece of cloth.
Then take known soap and quicklime, about an ounce, or whatever, and mix these ingredients well, and do this quickly so that it does not get cold; then spread this on the cloth that is made in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and place this over one or both eyes; and place under the eye cotton soaked in egg white, and the patient's head should be in your lap.
And place your finger on the medicine and press it lightly, but not too much, until the patient feels the bite of the medicine, because the bite is like fire.
And so, when you perceive that he feels the bite, then remove the medicine and wipe it with your finger; then, when the bite ceases, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; and then observe if you see that the eyelid is now raised; if it is not raised, cauterize it with fire or with an incision, because then it is well; and if not, return some of the medicine to the place where the medicine did not reach and it does not blacken until your work is even and the eyelid is contracted.
Then place cotton with a bandage over it until the scab from the cauterization is removed, and then treat it with healing ointment or other until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, during your operation, to watch with vigilant diligence that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye, and if the eyelid after some days is softened and some of the hair roots are in the eye, then repeat your medicine on that place which of the eyelid is softened, and properly as you did in the beginning, and then bandage it until it is healed.
And this is the method for myrtle leaf.
And know that the eyes of men are diversified according to their size and smallness, and therefore according to that is your contraction made.
And the way of rectitude shall not be hidden from him who studies this art.
Chapter 17.
Concerning the cauterization of a fistula that is in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula with what we have said in the divisions of diseases and your treatment does not succeed, then you must cauterize it in this manner: instruct the patient to place his head on your lap, and let a minister hold his head between your hands in such a way that his head is not moved or agitated.
Then place cotton soaked in egg white or gum arabic on his eye.
Then heat the cautery, whose shape is as follows:
which should be concave, like the shape of a reed or a vulture's feather, at the end with which the cauterization is performed.
And if you wish, let it be penetrating to the other end. And if you want, let it be shaped like a probe.
However, the concave one is better for your work.
Then press the fistula if it is open, and draw out the pus and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the pus from it.
Then place the cautery, which should be well ignited, over it; and hold your hand a little below the cautery, from the eye to the side of the nose, so that your hand does not burn or the patient is disturbed; and then place the cautery on the granulation of the eye and cauterize it.
And so, if at the beginning your cautery penetrates to the bone, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if necessary; and leave it like that for three days, and then place a dressing over it with lint and surround it with drying ointment until it is healed.
But if eleven days have passed and it is not healed, then place a sharp corrosive medicine over it until the bone is uncovered and scraped as is appropriate in its chapter.
And another method of cauterizing a fistula, which some ancients described:  approach the location of the fistula and open it, and then place in that fissure a thin tube whose shape is like this:  And pour into it the quantity of one ounce of molten lead; and hold your hand with a cloth with a good grip, and do not hurt the patient so that the molten lead does not reach his eye; and you should place over the patient's eye a cloth soaked in egg white or water, because the lead will cauterize the location of the fistula and heal it by a marvelous healing.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said of the cautery and the treatment, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to administer a nasal puncture and reduce the fistula to the body of the nose as it will come to its proper place.
Chapter 18.
On the cauterization of the fissure of the wells (or, possibly,  "On cauterizing the cleft of the [body part -  the exact meaning of 'potz' is unclear without more context]").
Many times fissures occur on the lips, and especially on the lips of children.
Therefore, if you treat those fissures with what we have said in the sections and your treatment does not work, then ignite a small, knife-like cautery according to this form that is shown:
And let its concavity be according to the thinness of a knife.
Then carefully place that [cautery] in those fissures until the cautery reaches the bottom of the fissures.
And then treat that with cerates until it is healed.
Chapter 19.
Concerning the cauterization of a fistula in the mouth.
When in the root of the uvula, or in the palate, or in the roots of the teeth, an abscess develops and then breaks, and then becomes chronic, and pus flows and a fistula is formed, and then you treat it and the treatment is not effective, then you should apply a cautery according to the size of the fistula.  Then place that heated cautery in the opening of the fistula, and hold it there until the heated iron reaches the bottom of it, and then do this once or twice. And then treat it with what we have said about treatment until it is healed.
And so if the wound is incised and heals, it is good; but if not, then it is necessary to expose the area and remove the corrupted bone according to what will be described in its chapter.
Chapter 20.
Concerning the cauterization of teeth and the softening of the gums.
When the gums are softened by humidity and the teeth are decayed, and you treat them with medicines and they are not effective, then place the patient's head in your lap, and then heat the cautery, the shape of which will be described later, in such a way that you place the small tool (likely a small instrument to hold the cautery near the tooth) over the tooth and quickly insert the heated cautery into it, and hold it for a short time until the patient feels the heat of the fire reaching the root of the tooth.
And then remove your hand, and then repeat the cauterization several times as needed.
And then let the patient fill their mouth with salt water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out, because the decayed tooth is sealed, the softened gum is tightened, and the corrupt humidity is dried out.
Chapter 21.
On the cauterization of toothache.
When toothache is due to coldness, or there is cold or a worm in it, and you do not achieve a cure with medicine, then cauterize it in one of two ways: either cauterize with a cow's horn or cauterize with fire.
Cauterization with a horn involves taking a cow's horn and boiling it in a cauldron of iron. Then take a cotton swab and wrap it around the end of a stick, then dip it in the boiling horn, and quickly apply it to the aching tooth until it cools.
And then repeat this several times until the virtue of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, submerge wool or cotton in the aforementioned horn, and place that on the aching tooth, and place ignited iron on top of it until the iron reaches the bottom of the tooth cavity.
However, cauterization with fire is that you use a small tube of bronze or iron, and there should be some thickness in its body, and the sharpness of the fire should not reach the patient's mouth.
Then ignite the cautery, the form of which will be apparent, and place it on the tooth and hold your hand there until the cautery cools; and do this several times because the pain will be reduced either that day or the next.
And it is necessary that, by the application of that cautery, the patient fill their mouth with good wine and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out; and this is the form of the cautery.
And cauterize with whichever extremity you wish, as is possible for you.
And this is the shape of the cannula:
Chapter 22.
On the cauterization of extrophuli (or possibly, external swellings/bumps).
When extrophuli are caused by phlegm and cold humors and are not responsive to medicinal digestion, and you wish to digest them lightly, then ignite the concave cautery, the shape of which is as follows [description presumably to follow], having two open ends so that the smoke escapes through one end of the cautery, and place this ignited instrument over the abscess once, or more times if needed, until it reaches the base of the abscess.
And if the abscess is small, then apply the cautery according to the size of the abscess, and then leave it for three days and apply a cotton pad soaked in a poultice until what the fire has burned is healed.
And then treat it with ointment and bandages until it is healed.
Chapter 23.
Concerning the cauterization of hoarseness and constriction of the throat.
When humidity affects the lung's canal, and especially when this is accompanied by cold constitution, then the patient should be evacuated at the beginning with laxative medicines, and then cauterize that area below the root of the throat in the depressed location.
And be careful not to reach the throat with the cautery, nor burn the uvula, only a little.
And cauterize that area with another cautery below the joint of the neck, at the last vertebra. And his cautery should be a small nail, following the previous method.
And then treat that with what has been previously prescribed until it is healed.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the cauterization of lung disease and cough.
When cough and lung disease are caused by cold and damp humors, and the patient has no fever or consumption, and the illness is chronic, then cauterize with two cauteries on the clavicle in the previously mentioned locations, on the left and right; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest between the two nipples; and let the cautery be a small nail according to the previous method.
And if you wish, your cauterization may be punctate with the cautery that is called a punctate cautery, the form of which precedes in the punctation of earache.
And let there be points within those [cauterizations]; and then treat it with the same things as preceded until it is healed.
And let the cautery have three prongs, for cauterization is done with this: with one application you can make three cauteries according to this method:
Chapter 25.
Of the cauterization of the testicle, which is the head of the penis.  *(Note:  The translation is interpretive.  "Ajutori" likely refers to the penis, but its exact meaning is uncertain and could also refer to something related to the urinary system.)*
When the head of the penis is dislocated because of lubricating humors and is not fixed in the moment of its return from its dislocation until it is accustomed to returning, and then by a small movement it is dislocated again as it has happened, as we have witnessed, then first you must reduce the dislocation; and then the patient should be turned on his back or on his healthy side; and then raise the scrotum which is in the inner part of the testicle towards the upper parts with the fingers of your left hand, if the joint is dislocated in the inner parts.
And then heat the cautery which has two heads; and this is its form:
And then cauterize the scrotum until it penetrates from one side to the other; and the figure of the cauterization will then be of four cauterized heads.
And sometimes it is adjusted according to this count, and therefore there are eight cauteries.
And then place over the cautery cooked figs with salt, and then keep the patient calm, and the limb should not be moved for some time until it is healed.
And if the dislocation is in the upper parts and this is done late, then cauterize over the muscle with a nail-shaped cautery or with many pointed cauteries, because the joint is strengthened and the humidity is removed, and the patient is healed.
Chapter 26.
Concerning the cauterization of the stomach.
When cold and many humors enter the stomach, exceeding its capacity and multiplying within it, and if you continue the treatments and do not cure it, then the patient should lie supine, extending their hands and legs.  Then cauterize it with three cauteries: one cautery below the feet, to the extent of one finger, using a nail-shaped cautery; and two cauteries below on either side of the first cautery, so that a triangular figure of cautery is formed; and space them so that they are not touching, which would cause decay.
And if the depth of the cautery [is] the amount of two-thirds of the thickness of the finger.
And let the figure of the cautery be according to this form without addition. And if you wish, cauterize with a large cautery in the middle of the stomach according to this form; and this should be a cautery in the form of a circle according to this form:
And let the stomach be cauterized, paying attention to that which is feared from this cautery:  and it is that you mark on the stomach points according to the amount you wish with a probe, and then cauterize those points with a cautery; and then treat it with the treatment that is prescribed until it is healed.
Chapter 27.
On the cauterization of the cold liver.
When pain occurs in the liver due to coldness, or humidity, or excessive flatulence, so that it is expelled from its normal composition by superfluous egress, and the patient is treated with what we have said and it does not help, then the patient should be supine on his back, and mark with a probe three cauteries according to this form and this same quantity on the liver under the hypochondria, where the decubitus of a man is perceived.
And let the space which is between each cautery be according to the thickness of a finger, and let the cautery be according to the length of a straight finger.
And do not press too much with the cautery.
And if the quantity of what is burned is the amount of half the thickness of a finger, and no more; and if the patient is standing on his feet; and if he is not standing, then let him lie down, having already spread his legs, and raise his arms.
And it is possible for you to cauterize these areas with a knife-like cautery, when you have subtlety and prudence in the art.
And beware that you do not proceed too much in the cauterization, and let the thickness of a whole finger be burned so that the whole body is burned and reaches the intestines, because the finger is subtle here; and know this.
Chapter 28.
Of the perforation of an abscess of the liver with a cautery.
When there is an ulcer or abscess in the liver and you want to know if that abscess is in the flesh of the liver or in its tunic, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient finds gravity and pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the tunic of the liver, the pain is very sharp, and sometimes it even tires the doctors in its treatment.
Therefore, the patient should be turned onto their side; and then mark the location of the abscess with a probe; and then ignite the cautery, which is shaped like a rake, the form of which was shown on the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until the whole abscess is consumed, until it reaches the bottom, until pus comes out.
And then treat it with the treatment for other abscesses until it is healed.
And it is not fitting that this type of cautery be used except by one whose study in the art of medicine is prolonged, and in whose hands many diseases have been known; however, someone with some experience may dare to perform this operation, but nonetheless, leave it to us, it is better.
Chapter 29.
On the cauterization of pleurisy.
The ancients said that a cautery made from the roots of birthwort is effective for cold pleurisy in this manner: that you take a long, dry root of birthwort, one finger in length, and of the thickness of a finger.
Then dip it in oil and hold it to the fire, and cauterize with a cautery in the place which is between the continuation of the clavicle and the neck; and with two small cauteries below the clavicle, slightly leaning towards the part below the beard, and with two cauteries in that which leans between the fifth rib and the sixth, leaning slightly towards the back; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest; and with another on the stomach; and with three cauteries on the back; and with one cautery in the place which is between the two shoulders; and with two cauteries on the two sides of the chest, below the cautery which is between the two shoulders.
And you should not grasp the cautery with your hand, but let it be held by a suitable instrument.
And some ancients said that some men use a cautery of iron similar to a ray and heat it, and apply it to the place which is between the ribs, until it reaches the abscess and it is thoroughly cauterized, as we have said in the abscess of the liver.
But this perforation with a cautery is not without danger, for one may easily cause a fistula there, which is not easily healed.
Chapter 30.
Concerning the cauterization of the spleen.
When you treat the illness of the spleen with what we have said of the treatment in the division and your treatment is not effective, then cauterize it in three ways, all of which are appropriate. One is to make three or four cauterizations along the length of the spleen, according to the length of the spleen, following the shape of the liver cauterizations, the form of which has been previously described.
And let the distance between each cauterization be the width of a finger or a little more, and the manner of cauterization should be the same as that which we described for the cauterization of the liver.
And do not press your hand with the cautery; and the patient should be lying on their side.
And another way to cauterize is to ignite the cautery having two points, which we described in the chapter on muscle dislocation, and raise the rib which is in the area of the spleen where the patient's left side lies.
And your elevation, with which you raise the rib, should be according to the length of the body, so that cauterizations are made according to the width of the body.
And then insert the two points, very hot, into that space so that with them you penetrate the rib from the other side.
And then withdraw the cautery; and thus four cauterizations are made.
And if you wish, cauterize with a cautery having three points.
And then you treat the cauterization once it has stopped discharging pus for many days, for this is more important than the other preceding things in the treatment.
Chapter 31.
Concerning the cauterization of a dropsical person.
Cauterization is not applied in dropsy, but only in a swelling, properly speaking.
When you treat a dropsical patient with the methods of treatment that we have mentioned in the division and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize them with four cauteries in a circle around the navel, and with one cautery over the stomach, and with another cautery over the liver, and with another cautery over the spleen, and with two cauteries between the vertebrae of the back, and one cautery on the right foot, and with another cautery over the stomach. And the depth of the cauterization should be proportional to the thickness of the skin.
And then leave the cautery open so that it discharges pus for a long time.
And do not remove the treatment from the patient near the cauterization with what is necessary, so that two intentions are added to it, and healing is hastened with help.
The shape of the cautery with which the abdomen is cauterized should be nail-shaped, as its shape is described above, and that with which the back is cauterized should be olive-shaped, as its shape is described previously.
Chapter 32.
Concerning the cauterization of the feet and thighs.
When the feet are inflamed in dropsy and filled with yellow water, it is necessary to cauterize the feet on the back of the foot, in the area between the little finger and ring finger.  Raise your hand with the cautery and do not twist it; then raise your hand again and do not completely withdraw it, because the yellow water will flow out.
The cautery should be of this shape:  Then make two cauteries on both thighs, and on each thigh a cautery should be made with the knife-like end of the cautery. And let the cautery be according to the length of the thigh: one below the knee and the other in the middle of the thigh; and two cauteries on each thigh.
And leave the wound open without treatment for a long time so that some water may drain from it.
And then treat it with those things which we have mentioned.
Chapter 33.
On the cauterization of the abdomen.
When the abdomen is afflicted by cold and humidity to the point that it weakens the vital functions—that is, the digestive and retentive powers which are in the stomach and intestines—and this is treated with methods of care and fails to improve, and sometimes the patient suffers from cauterization exhibiting virtue, then cauterize that area with a large cautery on their stomach, as was previously described in the cauterization of the stomach, with a circular cautery and with four small cauteries in a circle around the navel, using a slender, pointed cautery; and with a cautery on the flank above the hip, either a large one or two smaller cauteries.
And if sometimes the humors are excessive and the patient tolerates it, then cauterize that area over the pubic bone and over the intestines, and quickly apply two cauteries near the stomach and near the groins, for this is a perfect cure and a help from which you will not err.
Chapter 34.
On the cauterization of chronic hemorrhoids.
When there are in their nature chronic hemorrhoids, or ones caused by thick, cold humors or corrupted moistures, and they are treated with what we have said in the division and do not improve, then cauterize with three cauteries on the most prominent part of the coccyx, small and slightly triangular, and with a cautery above the navel, close to two fingers' breadth from it.
And if you suspect that the patient's stomach is chilled and their food is not digested, and their face is sometimes inflamed, then cauterize the stomach with a large cautery as previously described; and with one cautery on the liver, and with another cautery on their spleen with a nail-shaped cautery, and leave the cautery open for some time, and then treat them until they are healed.
Chapter 35.
On the cauterization of swellings near their excision.
When you excise a swelling, then ignite the cautery that resembles a ray (or, a spoke/rod). Then insert that ignited cautery into the excised swelling, and apply your hand until the cautery reaches the opening of the vein from which the blood flows.
And do this once or twice, for if the swellings are many, then cauterize each of them as we have recounted, and then treat those with ointments until they are healed.
And if you cauterize with a large cautery on someone, it is more slow in healing.
Chapter 36.
Chapter 36. Of the cauterization of fistulas that are formed in the lower parts of the body.
When nature does not obey the treatment which we have recommended in its place, and the patient is frightened by this, let it be healed with cautery.
Therefore, when someone has a fistula in the lower parts, and it is old, running with pus and corrupted humors, then that which is most worthy to be done is to probe it with a thin probe, and then know the quantity and depth of it with the aforementioned probe.
Then ignite the cautery which is similar to a ray [or, perhaps, a "rod"]. And then place that ignited cautery into the fistula, according to the extent to which the probe entered.
And iterate upon that the cautery until those corrupted bodies are burned, once or twice or three times, according to the amount of your necessity.
And beware of burning the nerves and veins; or if the fistula goes to the body of the bladder or the body of the intestine, then beware of all those places; and do not do this unless the fistula is in a fleshy place and you think that it is not penetrating [deeper structures].
And then treat the area until it is healed.
And if the place is sealed, and the materials are excised from it, and remain so for a long time, then know that it is already completely cured.
And if the materials are not removed from it, then know that it is penetrating, or in the depth of it something is corrupted, or this comes from those causes of which mention will be made in their place [later in the text].
Chapter 37.
On the cauterization of wounds.
When in wounds pain arises from cold or excessive inflammation, and because of this the patient's strength is diminished, then it is necessary that you cauterize that upon the wounds with a cautery, upon each wound with a nail-shaped cautery, the preparation of which was mentioned previously.
And quickly cauterize again a little lower, so that three linear cauterizations will result, and then they will be more effective in helping.
Chapter 38.
On the cauterization of the bladder.
When weakness and softening occur in the bladder due to cold and humidity, such that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize that in the lowest part above the bladder, where the hairs of the pubis begin, and cauterize with one cautery on the right side, and with another on the left.
And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the patient's walnut; and cauterize that with a cautery in the lowest part of the back, or with two cauteries if necessary.
And let the cautery be a nail, as is customary/appropriate.
Chapter 39.
Concerning the cauterization of the breast.
When cold and humidity come to the breast, and because of this pregnancy is prevented, and her menses are corrupted, and their provocation is delayed, and pain occurs before their arrival, then it is necessary that the place be cauterized with three cauteries in the area around the navel, as we have said in the cauterization of the bladder; and with a cautery on the lowest part of the back, or with two cauteries, and let the cautery be nail-shaped.
Chapter 40.
Concerning the cauterization of a dislocated hip.
When mucous humors are poured into the area of the hip, and are the cause of its displacement, and a sign of this is that one thigh is longer than the other when they are compared. And the location of the dislocation is found where it bulges.
Then it is necessary that you cauterize the patient on the part of their hip with a circular cautery, after you have marked with a probe the circumference of the affected area as it is swollen, so that the affected area falls within the middle of the circle.
And let the cautery be of the type previously described for the cauterization of the stomach.
And if that cautery is not at hand, then cauterize it with three cauteries using a large olive-shaped cautery.
And let the cautery be made according to the extent of the thickness of the entire swelling; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 41.
On the cauterization of the sciatic vein.
When pain comes in the hip area, and it is caused by cold and dampness, the patient is treated with what we have said and does not improve, and this is longstanding, then you must purge the patient of thick humors with foul-smelling pills or with artificial or similar pills; and then cauterize it.
And the manner of cauterization in that case is according to two methods: either cauterization with burning medicines, or cauterization with fire.
Indeed, according to two known methods, of which one is that you cauterize that area on the patient's hip with three triangular cauteries according to this form; and make them of good depth. And let the space which is between each cautery be the amount of the thickness of a finger, whether the cautery is olive-shaped or large.
But sometimes let the cautery be in the middle of the place, on the head of the hip, so that there are four cauteries.
And if you wish, cauterize with the circle whose form precedes in the cauterization of hip dislocation, that is, let it be a continuous circle and burn the thickness of the whole buttock.
And this is the method of cauterization in sciatica when pain is adherent to the hip and is not extended into the thigh.
But if it is extended into the thigh, then cauterize that with two cauteries on the thigh, on the place to which the patient points as the location of the pain; and with one cautery on the kneecap, about four fingers below the left side.
And if the cautery is knife-shaped, let the depth of the cautery be according to the thickness of the buttock.
And if the patient indicates that the pain is extended to the area near the toes, then cauterize there where indicated with a cautery in three or four points, or more if needed.
And if the patient indicates that the pain is below the buttock towards the thigh, then cauterize there with a knife-shaped cautery.
And be careful in all cauterization that you do not go so deep with the cautery that you burn nerves or large arteries, and that because of this you do not cause harm, unconsciousness, or convulsions to the patient.
And I have seen one and another of those whom I cauterized on the ankle and went too deep in the cauterization, and the leg swelled until the swelling reached the foot, and the whole foot was perforated and corrupted. And then a strong case of diarrhea occurred, and then death.
And if the pain is in two places at once, cauterize each according to this method.
And some wise men say, in the cauterization of the ankle, use a cautery of which this is the method: make a circle of iron similar to this, of which the circumference should be half a palm; and let the opening be according to the thickness of a date pit, or a little less.
And within that circle let there be another, a third; and let the space which is between them be the quantity of a hazel nut; and let those circles be open on two sides; and let the length of those irons be a hazelnut or two, and make in those irons a mark decently prepared in the circles; and this is its form:
Then let it be heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks, and then let it be placed on the part of the ankle, the patient lying on their side. And thus cauterize that with three round cauteries at once.
Then leave it there for three days; then plaster it with a bandage and leave the wound open for many days; and then cure it with ointment until it is healed.
Says Albucasis: O, son, of this type of cautery we have used little, because of the fear of its appearance, and because few have been found who dared to look upon it.
But of the good cauteries to him who suffers them, and finds with that its location.
But cauterize with adherent medicines, such that you make two circles having perforated handles in which you insert the bolt of an iron door; let the length of the walls in height be two nuts or whatever those are.  (This line is difficult due to the ambiguity of "nozes" and the unusual description of the cautery tool).
And if the space which is between each is the quantity of the thickness of a finger; and they are open below and above, and they contain themselves between them according to this form:
Then place the patient's afflicted hip on the side, lying on their side, and press your hand on the area of the hip; then take warm water and pour it between the two circles, and heat it gradually with little fire, and keep it at that temperature for about an hour, and let the patient suffer the biting and burning— for it causes a burning sensation like fire— until the burning stops.
Then raise your hand with the instrument and wipe the warm water from the whole hip with fresh water.
Then leave it for three days and apply a bandage until the blackness of the burn is softened, and leave it for a few days, and pus will drain from it; this is the final treatment.
Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if the pain extends to the hip or leg, make for him a bath that contains water, done in the same manner as for the hip.
And this is the method for hot water, and I have already written about it in the treatise on the rectification of medicines. Take alkali and quicklime, equal parts of each, crush them and place them in a new pot, the bottom of which is perforated by a small hole, according to the amount that will enter the ray. (The "ray" likely refers to a specific instrument or body part; more context would be needed for precise translation.)
And place below the bottom of the pot another inverted pot, and pour over the alkali and quicklime live (unslaked) fresh water which covers them by a finger's width, and after you compress them with your hand with good pressure, let the sharp water descend from above into the inverted pot below.
And then collect all that water, and pour it over more fresh quicklime and alkali, for then it will be of very strong acidity, which is used in many medical operations and in cauterization of other members, because it acts like fire.
And some medicines with which one also cauterizes are:  thrice-boiled honey and quicklime with soap, mixed together.  (The "tezizic" is likely a corruption or a word not readily translatable without more context;  "thrice-boiled" is a speculative interpretation based on potential meaning in similar languages.)
And Gualia recounted the operation and cure of a man of old times, by which pain of the hip and sciatic vein was cured, and greatly praised the efficacy of it, and said that he needed no other cure, and that he was healed at once, just as he recounted, for he quickly entered a bath and was immediately free of it.
And it is that fresh rue should be taken, and if fresh rue is not found, a fresh fig should be taken; and it should be well crushed with a little fat and placed on the hip where the pain is, or on the thigh, or on the leg, and bound on.
And it should be left on for three hours, or for as long as the patient feels a lessening of the burning.
Then put him in a bath, and when his body is reddened, expose him to the sun, for the pain ceases and he is healed.
And know that one should not use cautery with these medicines except after the evacuation of the body.
But Dioscorides said that when the varicose vein is cauterized with a goat's horn, it profits him much; and the cautery should be applied in this manner: wool should be taken and soaked in old oil, and placed between the toes and between the ankle bones.
Then take a dry goat's horn and heat it in the fire until it becomes charcoal; and then place it on the wool and leave it there until it is extinguished. Then repeat this, and do not cease doing this until the sensation of the cauterization reaches the ankle and relieves the pain.
And this manner of cautery is called cautery of the veins.
Chapter 42.
Of the cauterization of toothache.
Sometimes pain is in the teeth from various causes, such as a fall or a blow, or excessive evacuation, and from causes similar to those, by the infusion of cold and humid matter.
And cautery is only suitable for that single type which is caused by the effusion of cold matter.
Therefore, after evacuation of the patient with purgative pills and similar remedies,  make incisions on the tooth where the pain is, three lines according to its width; then mark with a tenaculum in each line five cauterizations or more, according to the amount tolerated by the patient and their strength.
And let the cauterization be done with a pointed cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize with three or four cauteries using a nail-shaped cautery according to this method:
Chapter 43.
Concerning cauterization at the beginning of gibbosity (or humpback).
Many times this illness happens to small children; and a sign of its beginning in children is that it occurs on their spine with elevation and movement.
And you find in the last vertebra of the back and neck that it already begins to rise above the other vertebrae.
And therefore, when you see this and want to prevent it, then cauterize that with a cautery that is circular according to this form: so that the cautery takes in the whole part of the vertebra equally.
And if you wish, cauterize in the circumference of the vertebra with the pointed cautery two or three lines. And let the points be close to each other.
And then treat the area until it is healed with what we have said.
But beware that cautery is not administered in swelling that is caused by a spasm of the nerves.
Chapter 44.
Concerning the cauterization of gout and joint pain.
When joint pains are caused by cold humors which are infused into each member of the body, then when pain comes in the feet, it is the custom of physicians to call this gout properly.
And therefore, if gout caused by cold is cured with a method of cure and the pains are not lessened, then cautery lessens them.
And it is so that you cauterize that area for evacuation in the circumference of the joint of the foot with many cauteries.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped, of which this is the form:
And if it is necessary that a puncture be made on the surface of the foot, then do it with a pointed cautery. And if the pains rise into the knees or other joints, and this often happens, then cauterize that area on each knee with three or four cauteries on each side with this olive-shaped cautery.
And if many cauteries are necessary, then cauterize that area and do not press your hand too hard with the cautery beforehand; let it only be around the thickness of a finger.
And if the pains rise into both knees, then apply the cautery which we have described in its chapter.
And if the pains are only in the hands and it is called  rheumatism, then puncture in the circumference of the wrists as they turn, puncturing those two lines.
And if the pain remains in the fingers, then puncture in the circumference of the wrists on each night and on the palm of the hand.
And if the pains rise in some days to the hips or muscles, then cauterize them on all sides of the affected areas.
And do not neglect the patient's good regimen and reception of medicines, for if a good regimen is followed and the inflammation is evacuated with this cautery, he will be healed, God willing.
Chapter 45.
On the cauterization of fractures.
When a rupture occurs in the groin and the intestines protrude before the scrotum, and this is beginning to happen, then it is necessary that you order the patient to cease eating on that day and that he use lenient remedies so that the rupture descends from his belly.
Then hold the patient between your hands over his back, and order him to hold his breath until the hernia or intestine is expelled or appears.
And then return that with your fingers and gently push the rupture over the pubic bone, noting that its extremity should resemble a half-circle in the upper parts of the body.
And then heat the cautery, of which this is the form:
until it is very red and throws off sparks. And then return the intestine or hernia to its cavity, and place your hand over the area so that the intestine does not come out.
And you have already opened the space between the patient's thighs and placed a cushion underneath; and another servant should be on their legs, and another on their chest, holding their hands.
And then place the cautery on that mark with your hand, holding the cautery straight, and hold it there until you reach with it the bone of the hip joint in the middle of the buttock; and repeat this another time if you did not reach the bone on the first attempt.
And observe wisely the protrusion of the intestine during your cauterization, so that you do not burn that intestine, lest death or other great harm befall the patient.
And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your operation is not complete/successful.
And it is necessary that the cautery for children be subtle, according to their size and measure.
And then care for the cauterized area for three days with a bandage until the burn scar is healed.
And then treat it with other ointments until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on their back for forty days until the area and wound are sealed/healed.
And it is necessary that you give him nourishment according to the length of his treatment, which will cleanse his belly, and that the intestine does not come out due to the exertion of healing.
And then, when he wants to get up after about forty days, apply a properly made bandage, and keep it on for another forty days.
And let him be lessened in work and the repletion of food and drink, and of vehement voices, for when he uses this entire regimen of healing he will be completely healed.
And I will say the treatment of ruptures in the other parts of the belly; and so, at the beginning, and if you want them not to spread, then cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size, and let that which is excised from the hernia be like a third part of it; and then treat it with what we have said, and it is well-suited.
And some first said that a rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery according to this form, since it is marked with a probe:
and a line is placed which is across the widest part of the rupture, and another line above it; and place your hand with a nail cautery, but the first cautery is lighter and better.
Chapter 46.
On the cauterization of torsion (or twisting/strangulation).
When in some member, because of a fall or a blow, pain arises and that pain persists for a long time and does not yield to treatment with medicines, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And let the cautery be for each member according to its size and smallness, and its strength and weakness, and the location of the torsion and its pain.
Therefore, if it is healed by the first cauterization, good; and if not, then repeat the cauterization; for it is customary for these pains to be punctured from one member to the neighboring member, wherefore it is necessary that you treat those with cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
On the cauterization of leprosy.
Lepers are sometimes treated with cautery with great benefit, and especially if the type of leprosy is caused by putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
Therefore, if you wish to cauterize a leper, consider whether the leprosy is spreading, and if you have treated it with what we have said in the division and it does not cease or remain, and you fear that the corruption may affect the entire body and complexion of the patient, then cauterize the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, and a second below it, between the forehead and the hairline; and two on both temples; and one behind, on the nape of the neck.
And finally, with the cautery, press gently so that a small impression is made on the bone, and the scabs are removed, so that the exhalation of thick vapors is relieved; and cauterize over the spleen as needed.
And if the leprosy is already widespread on the patient and manifestly apparent, then you should cauterize with the cauteries we mentioned on the head; and with a cautery on the tip of the nose, and with two cauteries on the two cheekbones, and with two cauteries on two vertebrae of the neck, and with six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and with one large one on the vertebrae near the coccyx, and one on the coccyx, and with two on the hips, and one on each buttock, and with two on the knees, and one on each knee, and with two on the ankles, and with two on the calves, and one on each calf, and with two on the heels, and with two on the muscles, and with two on the knuckles of his feet; and cauterize on all the joints of the fingers of the hands and feet, and on each heel of his feet, and on the knuckles of his hands.
And be careful of the nerves which are on the heel, that you do not burn them.
And sometimes it is cauterized with one cautery on the pubic bone, and another on the stomach opening, and another on the liver.
And know that the more accurately you apply cauteries, the more helpful and beneficial it is; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery, as they do from wounds, because his body is already numb.
And the cauteries should be large or small according to the limbs and joints, as is prescribed by the types of instruments.
Then treat the cautery with cooked barley flour mixed with honey and with other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
On cauterization of numbness/stupor.
When a limb is numb or asleep and has been treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then you cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the size or smallness of the limb.
And let your cautery penetrate the thickness of the skin a little. And afterwards treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes some numbness which extends to the hands and feet, and to the vertebrae of the back, is cauterized near the exit of the nerves which move that limb, and the numbness is relieved.
But do not attempt this except one who is learned in the anatomy of the limbs and the exit of the nerves moving the whole body.
Chapter 49.
On the cauterization of albaras, which is morphea.  (Note:  "Albaras" and its connection to "morphea" needs further research to give a completely accurate translation.  Morphea is a type of scleroderma.  Albaras may be a medieval term for a similar skin condition.)
When albaras is old and you do not apply any medicinal ointments to it, then cauterize it with a hot cautery iron a little, according to the size and nature of the affected area, until the whiteness and its color are relieved, and its color is changed.
Then treat it with flour of barley and lentils, with rose oil, and with arnagrosa leaves, and with pigeon's blood or raven's blood, each in equal parts, all mixed together; then apply a linen cloth over the area until it is healed.
Chapter 50.
On the cauterization of cancer. (Note:  "Cranc" here almost certainly refers to cancerous sores or tumors, not the crustacean.)
When a cancer is beginning and you want to remove it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery iron around its perimeter as it is swollen.
And the wise men say that it should be cauterized with a pointed cautery iron in the middle; but to me this is not advisable, for I fear that it will be ulcerated, and I have seen this happen on several occasions.
And it is more advisable that it be cauterized around its perimeter with a circle, as we have said, or with many cauteries. (The meaning of "motz cauteris" is likely "multiple cauterizations" or "many small cauterizations.")
Chapter 50.  (Assuming a Roman numeral system is being used.  The dot could indicate a break in the numbering system used in the manuscript.)
Concerning the cauterization of a boil (or abscess).  ("Adubilati" seems to refer to a type of swelling or inflammation, likely a boil or abscess.)
When a boil is delayed in maturation, and this is due to the superfluity causing it, or due to the patient's age, because they are old and decrepit, of little blood, or because of the time of year, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the boil; then cauterize around its perimeter with many small, pointed cauteries; and then leave it, for it will hasten its maturation.
But if you wish to perforate it with a cautery, then you heat the cautery, of which this is the form: and place it in the middle of the boil until it penetrates the skin. ( "Codena" likely means skin or the outer layer)
And if the cautery is in that place which is lower down, so that the body of the arm may be relieved; and then care for it until it is healed. (This implies a boil is located on the arm).
Chapter 52.
