Concerning the cauterization of a corrosive (or, a corrosive cauterization).
A corrosive is a corruption moving in the limb, which corrodes it like fire consuming dry wood.
Therefore, if you see this corrosive in a place which can tolerate cauterization with fire, then you should ignite very small, and large, cautery nails, according to what is appropriate for the place where the corrosive is.
And then cauterize it completely until you eradicate all the corrosion and none of it remains.
And then leave it for three days, and apply crushed sulfur mixed with oil to the cauterized areas until the scabs are completely removed and all the corruption is gone.
And then treat it with a flesh-generating ointment.
But if after three days you see that healthy flesh has grown where there is no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cauterization on what remains in the corrupted areas.
And a corroding ulcer is sometimes treated with a sharp medicine, for that serves in place of cauterization. However, cauterization with fire is of more certain help.
And I have already described its treatment with sharp medicine in the section, and therefore take that when you have need of it.
Chapter 53.
On the cauterization of wounds and other similar things.
Many times a disease called a wound happens in the parts below the foot, and it is a large, deep wound that hurts the foot; and cauterization in these cases is done in two ways, that is, cauterization with fire and cauterization with hot water.
But cauterization with fire is to ignite a concave cautery, similar to a vulture's beak, which should be made of iron according to the size needed to encompass the entire wound, and it should be of fine edges.
Then place that ignited instrument over the wound, and move your hand with the cautery in a circle around the wound until the cautery reaches the depth of the wound. And leave it there for three days until it begins to shed pus. Then plaster it with crushed wild mallow mixed with salt, and leave the plaster on it for one night, for this will eradicate it from its roots.
Then treat the area with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
And if it is not healed, and this often happens on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet, then you should apply cautery with water to those areas.  Take a cannula of bronze or iron or a vulture's quill, and place it over the wound; then pour a small quantity of hot water into the cannula, and hold your hand, and completely move it with the pressure of your hand; and let the edge of the cannula be pressed into the root of the wound, and the hot water will find its way to the root of the wound; and let the patient endure the bite of the hot water for a little while, about an hour, and then remove it, for the wound with its roots will be eradicated.
And do this to those [wounds] and then to the others, until you have treated all those that are in the body.
Then treat those sores, once the roots of the wounds are eradicated, with ointments that generate flesh.
Chapter 54.
On the cauterization of tremor.
When someone suffers from tremor due to cold in the nerves, or from a cold-caused fever, or from a similar cause, then you should cauterize it with four or five cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, between each vertebra with one cautery; and with a cautery on their feet, and on their stomach, with an olive-shaped cautery.
And their tremor will cease, and hasten the healing of the cold illness.
Chapter 55.
On the cauterization of accidental ailments of the body.
When in the body there are boils and pustules which are formed from thick, corrupted matter; then, at the beginning of their appearance, it is necessary that they be cauterized at the head of the fistula with a fine cautery with myrtle wood, the tip of which is heated with fire; or with long roots of  *erestologia* (a plant whose name may not be accurately translated); or with a lenticular (lens-shaped) cautery.
And sometimes a cautery is also suitable at the beginning of their expulsion in this manner; and they are not believed (to be serious), and the superfluity is broken up by making these (cauterizations), and heals from them.
However, it is necessary that this (cauterization) be done near an evacuation with phlebotomy (bloodletting).
Chapter 56.
Chapter 56.  On the cauterization of accidental bleeding from a wound caused by an arrow.
Very often, bleeding occurs from an arrow wound that is incised below the wound, which comes from outside, or from the incision of abscesses or cauterization of a limb, and similarly, and it is difficult to stop that (bleeding).
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then you press with your hand on the opening of the arrow wound, and place your thumb over it, pressing it many times until the bleeding stops under your thumb, and it does not come out.
Then put small and large olive cauteries in the fire, many in number, and blow on them until they are very hot.
Then take one of those, small or large depending on the location and wound where the artery is broken, on that vein quickly, after you have removed your thumb, and hold the cautery there until that blood is stopped and constrained.
And if the blood is expelled from the opening of the artery when you remove your thumb and apply the cautery, then take another cautery carefully from those that are prepared in the fire, and then do not cease doing this one after another until the blood is constrained.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is there, and that another harm comes to the patient.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, then it is not possible to restrain the blood in those cases, and especially when the artery is large, except in one of four ways: either with a cautery as we have said, or with extraction when it is not extracted, for when it is extracted the extremities are better burned and the blood is restrained, or that it be tied with thread or with a strong ligature, or that medicines whose virtue is to restrain blood be applied, and they are tightened with a cushion with decent compression.
But that which administers decent restriction with ligature and with stricture with cloths or by the application of cauterizing agents and similar things, does not confer [its effect] except slowly.
But if this happens to someone and the physician or medicine is not present, then gently place the index finger over the opening of that vein as we have said, and apply great compression until the blood is stopped from the superior part of the wound, and the finger should not be removed from the artery; and over the artery let cold water of great coldness be sprinkled until the blood is congealed and thickened; and then consider what need there is of cautery or medicine.
Of incisions and perforations and cupping and of phlebotomy and of wounds and their similars.
We have already discussed in the first chapter every illness in which cautery with fire and with burning medicine is suitable, and its occasions and causes, and the necessary instruments with the forms of the cauteries; and I have ordered the chapters from the top of the head to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that the one who seeks this will be relieved, then concerning that which he will have asked.
And before I begin this, it is necessary, son, that you learn that in this chapter is that which is in the first chapter on cauterization, but in a less terrifying manner. (or ...but to a lesser degree of terror).
And because it is necessary that there be great caution in this, for in the operations which are in this chapter, many times there happens an evacuation which is with blood, with which life is bound, through the opening of a vein, or incision upon an abscess, or opening of a fistula, or purification of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision of a stone, and things like that. And all those are accompanied by fear and trembling, and in the worst case, death.
And I warn you that this should not befall you, in which there is hesitation, for sometimes it happens to you through the handling of men with many kinds of illnesses. And of them, there is someone who is angry with his illness and death is light to him, and this is because of the vehemence of what he finds in his illness, and because of the length of his illness, and with the illness is a fear which signifies death.
And of those there is one who gives you his possessions and says, you are hoping for his health, and his illness is pernicious.
Therefore, it is not fitting that you consent to him who comes to you who is of this manner; and your caution should be greater than covetousness and your greed; and do not presume anything about this except from certain knowledge which verifies this to you, and to which follows praiseworthy success.
And use in the cure of all your patients prognostication and indication of him to whom health is restored, for in that there is for you help in the acquisition of fame and memory, of praise and of glory.
O, sons, may God inspire in you His understanding and not obstruct for you direction and protection, for this is in His hand.
And I have ordered this chapter, distinguishing it by chapters as preceded in the chapter on cauteries, from the care of the head to the feet, so that this which you ask for and of which you have need may be alleviated for you.
Chapter one.
Of the treatment of the water which is contained in the heads of infants.
Know that this illness often happens to infants during birth and when the midwife squeezes the infant's head without gentleness.
And sometimes it happens for a hidden reason which is not known.
But I have only seen this illness in infants; and in all those whom I saw, death followed, and thus it is a fatal affliction in them, in my experience.
And I have seen that an infant whose head was full of water, and his head was always thus enlarged until he could not support himself because of the size of his head, and the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture is either collected between the scalp and the bone, or collected under the bone on the brain.
Because if the operation is necessary and the moisture is between the scalp and the bone, and the abscess is small, then you should make an incision in the middle of the head with a fissure in width, and the length of the fissure should be about two nuts so that the moisture flows out, and this is the shape of the spatula:
And so if the moisture is more increased and the abscess is larger, then there are two incisions in this manner:
And if the moisture is under the bone, the sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on all sides and the water is squeezed out when you compress it with your hand on the inside parts and this is not hidden from you. You should make an incision in the middle of the head with three fissures in this manner:
And then extract all the moisture from the incision; and then bind the fissures with cloths and lint; and then dress it with wine and oil until the fifth day, and then untie the bandage and treat the wound with tents and ointments, and bind the head evenly; and let the patient eat dry food with little moisture until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of incision, that you consider where the magnitude of the abscess and the collection of water appear, for sometimes it is more in the occiput, or in the front part, or on the right, or on the left; and therefore aim with the incision where the abscess and the collection of water appear, and do it as best you can.
And observe that you do not cut the artery and cause a flow of blood, and the patient dies from that blood loss with evacuation of moisture.
Chapter second (or Chapter two).
When someone develops cataracts or rheum in the eyes or the feet, and this is longstanding, and medical treatments are ineffective, then the last resort is incision of those two arteries (temporal arteries).
And therefore, when you wish to incise them (the arteries), have the patient raise their head, and rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries appear, and the patient's neck should be constricted with a cloth; and observe where the vein pulses; and the location of each, above the locations which are near the ears, are compressed, and thus are hidden except in some men.
And then mark the artery with a probe, and incise each with a sharp lancet on each side, with the incision reaching to the bone, and this should be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the lancet under the artery and sever it at the incisions above.
And let the length of the incision be about two fingers' breadth, because when a vein is incised, the blood flows out with a pulsatile flow, which spurts out with frequent leaps.
But if the artery does not appear to the touch, then you must measure from the ear a space of three fingers' breadth, and mark it with a probe, and cut to the bone.
And the amount of blood to be let is about six ounces, and quickly remove more or less from him, and all this is according to the strength of the patient and the fullness of his artery.
And then examine the wound, and if any part of the splinter remains on the bone, then incise it so that a hot abscess does not develop.
And then bind the wound with lint and linen bandages, and treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Concerning the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
When someone suffers from chronic migraine or sharp headaches, due to acrid humors and a quality of laceration in the temples, or [the pain] is old, vehement, and similar to these, and is treated with medical methods of cure and this does not help, then we should attempt in these illnesses the extraction of the arteries in the temples, or their cauterization as we have shown.
And the method of operation and extraction of these is that the patient shaves the hairs which are in the temples; and he should attend to the artery apparent in the temple, for it is shown to you by its pulse, and it is hidden except in few men by the vehemence of cold.
And if it is hidden from you, then the neck of the patient should be constricted with some cloth; and then rub the area with a rough cloth or evaporate the area with hot water until the artery appears to you manifestly. And then you take the spatula of which this is the form: And then you scrape the skin with that [spatula] easily until you reach the artery; and then insert a hook there and extract it to the superior parts until you extract it from the skin and uncover it from the tunics which are beneath it, from every part.
But if the artery is subtle, then wrap it with the end of the hook, and then incise it a little according to the quantity by which its ends are lengthened and contracted, and there will be no flow of blood, for if they are not incised the blood will not rise at all.
And then evacuate from one to three ounces of blood.
And if the artery is large, then it is necessary that you tie it in two places with strong double linen thread or with cords at length, and do not allow it putrefaction before the consolidation of the wound and consequent flow of blood.
And then incise the superfluity which is between the two ligatures; and do this at that time or at another time.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery up to the bone with a knife-shaped cautery until its ends are incised, and it will be instead of that same procedure.
And it is a better procedure unless the patient has a fever or is of a hot complexion; for cautery is one of those things that helps to dry humidities, and therefore it is better in its application.
And it is necessary after the extraction of the arteries that you fill the place with old linen, and that powders are applied on it decently, and then it should be treated with dry medicines to produce new flesh and with bandages until it is healed.
And if, while you operate another time, a flow of blood from the artery occurs, then you must take care to restrain that blood, either with a cautery, or by filling the place with linen, and squeezing your hand until the blood is restrained. And if none of those things is at your disposal, then place your finger on the place until the blood is congealed, and bathe the place with very cold water until the acuteness subsides, and it is tightened as necessary.
And what is easiest in the extraction of an artery is that you cauterize the vein with this cautery having two blades, after you have marked the two places with a probe, and place each, very hot, until they reach the bone and sever the vein in two places, so that what is between the extremities is elongated, because they are not completely consolidated with this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And let the sharpness of the two blades be equal. However, if it has less sharpness than two knives it is better, because if it is as sharp as two knives it will approach each side too quickly and will not cut the flesh evenly.
Therefore, since in those [instruments] there is some thickness, the heat of the fire is held in them and the flesh is cut more easily.
And this method is better in every way and gentler.
And let the space which is between the two blades be the amount of the thickness of a finger.
Chapter four.
Concerning the treatment of sharp and acrid eye discharges.
When there are acrid tears, and the source is those veins which are on the scalp, and you find no cause for treatment in medicine, and you see the patient's face already reddened and feel on their forehead a crawling as if it were the crawling of ants, and their eyes are swollen, moist, so that the hairs around them are matted, and the eyelids are inflamed by the acridity of the tears, the ancients greatly valued this operation in the treatment of this, and they called this treatment *spassa*.
And it is that you command the patient to shave the hairs which are on their forehead, and then incise three fissures in the forehead, equally distant according to the length of the forehead. And if the length of the fissure is two digits: one fissure should be equidistant to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length a little of the movement of the muscles which are in the lacrimal duct, and the third on the other side.
And guard yourself from the incision of the arteries which are on both sides, and keep your hand away from the continuation of both jawbones. And if the space of each incision is the quantity of three adjusted digits.
And if you have a piece of prepared sponge or dried cloths ready, with which to dry the blood.
And then insert a spatula with two sharp ends, of which this is the form of its incision: which is from the part of the temples to the second incision. Excoriate with that [tool/spatula] the whole ridge which is between the two incisions, with the [surgical] knife which is on the bone, and then do this from the middle incision to the second incision. And then remove that spatula and insert into the first incision again another instrument which is called a scalpel, sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And place the sharp edge of that [scalpel] against the flesh attached to the ridge, and its blunt edge against the bone, and push it in until it reaches the middle fissure, and sever with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes.
But let the incision be continued to that appearance of the ridge, and then do this in the middle fissure to the other fissure.
And after a sufficient quantity of blood has flowed, press the places of the congealed blood clots.
And then place in each fissure a pledget of old linen, and place on them powders soaked in wine and oil and vinegar, and see that a hot abscess does not develop.
And on the third day, untie the bandage and administer a large infusion of warm or lukewarm water, and then treat it with basilicon ointment with rose oil and others until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
Chapter 5: On the treatment of tears caused by cataracts and descending to the eyes from the inner parts of the head.
When someone experiences sharp and persistent cataracts, and the patient's eyes appear small and sunken, and their vision is weakened, and the eyelids are ulcerated and their skin sheds, and there is sharp, persistent pain in the forehead and consequent sneezing, then know from these symptoms that the matter and cataracts originate from many veins proceeding from the inner parts.
And the best cure in such cases is this: order the patient to shave their forehead, and then incise a fissure in the middle of the forehead, a small one in width (and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision be between the bones; and extend your hand from the temples’ lacerations, which are injured by chewing), until the bone is exposed.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or a dry cloth and then fill the space between the two lips of the fissure with old cotton or linen cloths. And then tighten above with powders soaked in wine and oil so that a hot abscess does not develop.
And when you unbind this and see that the abscess has diminished, then it is necessary that you scrape the bone until new flesh begins to grow, and then treat it with a drying regimen which causes the flesh to grow, thus: if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony and mix them, and from them an emplaster is made and applied to these wounds to encourage the birth of new flesh.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the treatment of what falls into the ears.
Everything that falls into the ears is one of four types, for it is either a mineral stone or something similar to stone, such as iron or glass, or it is of plant origin, such as a seed or bean, or it is a fluid substance such as water and sour wine and things like them, or some animal.
And so when a stone falls into the ear or another similar thing amongst those things which do not adhere to or grow in the ear, and the ear is exposed to the sun, and if you see the stone, then instill into it some violet oil or sesame oil, and then administer its extraction, and induce sneezing with a [sneezing] powder, and plug the nostrils before the onset of sneezing. And then place some pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and pull the ear upwards, for often it is expelled by these means.
And if it is not expelled, then administer its extraction with the instrument called a delicate ear-spoon, of which this is the form:
And if it is expelled with the spoon, good; and if not, then administer its extraction with a thin hook of little curvature.
And so if it is not expelled by that, then make a cannula of bronze and insert the end of the cannula into the opening of the ear and seal around the cannula with wax and softened oil so that nothing is open except the cannula. And then extract it with your breath with strong suction, for many times it is done with what we have recounted.
But if it is not expelled in this manner, then take a small amount of lit tinder or something prepared with which birds are caught, and place that at the tip of a ray, and then carefully wrap a decent bandage over that. And then insert that into the opening of the ear easily, and then dry the ear of its humidity.
And so if it is not expelled by all the means which we have recounted, then you should consider cutting it out before it becomes a hot abscess or spasm.
And the manner of cutting it out is that you first bleed the patient from the cephalic vein and extract blood according to the patient's strength, and then hold the ear between your hands and turn the ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear below its cartilage in the well-prepared area. And let the incision be of a lunar shape until the stone is reached, and, the stone found, remove it if you can with appropriate instruments; and then sew the incision immediately with speed, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the cause falling into the ear is one of the [ear] stones that grows and is inflamed, then administer its cure and extraction; but if it does not obey your treatment, then take finely ground pumice stone according to this manner: and administer with that the excision of that stone which has fallen into the ear.
And do not do this when you think that it is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, and place it on thick cloths, and then extract it with tweezers or with a delicate instrument, or with an incision as we have said, for its extraction is thus eased.
But if water has entered the ear, the patient should use sneezing at the beginning, and fill his ear with some old cotton, and lie down on the ear in which the water is, and if it is expelled by this, it is good; and if not, then take some smooth, heated stones, about the length of a finger, and warm them a little in the fire, and the patient inserts one of these into the opening of his ear and jumps on one foot on that side and strikes it with another stone against the stone which is inside the ear. Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled.
Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled. And if the water is not expelled in this way, take some paper or tow, and put one end of it in the ear and apply the other end to a fire until most of it is burned.
And then put in more tow and do this several times until all the water is expelled, and remove it with a cannula as described above for the extraction stone.
But in the extraction of a creature entering that ear, if it is small, like a fly or something similar, treat it as we have said in the division; but if it is of a large size which appears to the sense of sight, then administer its extraction with forceps and hooks, and the cause of this is the easiest of all the other causes that fall into the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we have said in the division and in the treatise on distillations and your treatment is ineffective, then you must expose the ear to the sun.
And then, if any cause of the worms appears, extract it with forceps or with fine hooks; but if no cause appears, then take a cannula which is narrow below and wide above, of which this is the form: And insert the narrow end into the ear according to the amount the patient can tolerate; and then suck with it strongly, and do this many times, until all the worms are expelled.
But if it does not obey you in this way, then you plug and close the opening of the cannula with wax as we have said; then administer distillations which the ancients have already proven in the treatment of worms, and you will find this in the treatise on distillations.
And let your effusion, with which you pour the medicinal oils into the ear, be with this instrument, of which this is the form: And make it of silver or bronze, narrow below, in which there is a small hole, and flat above.
And so let it be so that it pushes what is in the cavity of the cannula to the outside where it will be necessary. And if you wish, take a ray (possibly referring to a thin, flexible instrument) and wrap its end decently with cotton, and then pour oil into the cavity of the ear, or the juice of some herb, or whatever you want of these medicines into the cannula — and that is in the ear —, and then introduce the ray with cotton on it and press with your hand, with a moderate compression, until the oil is expelled into the cavity of the hearing and the patient feels the oil inside.
And what is poured into the ear should be warmed a little by fire. And take care that what you distill into the ear is not too cold, for ear medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
On the treatment of ear obstructions.
Sometimes children are born from their mothers' wombs without perforated ears, and this also happens in some adults, an obstruction in the ear canal due to a wound or by flesh growing there; and this obstruction sometimes occurs in the inner part of the ear canal which prevents hearing, and sometimes in the outer parts.
And that which occurs in the inner part, which prevents hearing, is in the most difficult situations to heal.
And in that which allows hearing, you should place the patient's ear in the sun and examine it. And then if you see an apparent obstruction, open it with a fine spatula, of which this is the form:
If its extremity, where the width is small, has one sharp end and the other not; and let the spatula be two finger-widths wide so that it does not injure the ear.
And then, if the obstruction is due to flesh grown in the ear or its meatus, then take it with fine hooks and incise it vigorously until all the flesh is removed.
And if the obstruction is in the  entrance of the ear, then take a thin, fine  rod and heat it slightly with fire, and then place it in the ear canal.
And then, if you have felt an obstruction with what the patient finds easy [to feel], due to difficulty hearing,  remove the obstruction easily; and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
Then place in the ear a linen cloth, according to the size of the opening, which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment; and do this for some days until you are sure of the healing of the wound; or take some wool and dip it in warm water and sprinkle it with finely ground [powder], and use it; and if you see the ear already to be festering, then you must remove the wool and replace it with other wool which you will wrap in a plaster made with rose oil, until the abscess is completely healed; and then treat it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then submerge a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use the other treatment which restricts the flow of blood, which is described in the treatise on distillation.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the treatment of a stye which occurs on the eyelids.
Styes which occur on the eyelids of the eyes are sometimes moist, sometimes dry.
Therefore it is necessary that you hold them with forceps or with a hook, and incise them with a spatula from their roots, and then place a dressing on the incised area.
But if pain with bleeding occurs, then cauterize them with a thin, lentil-shaped cautery.
And the best cauterization is near the incisions, and often they return when some cause remains from their roots; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that comes to the eyelids of the eyes.
Often there appears on the eyelids of the eyes a certain something which resembles swelling in its firmness and hardness, and because of this swelling it is called [a swelling/tumor].
And it is an accumulation of thick humidity in the eyelid below and above.
Therefore, the operation on them is that you consider then if the swelling proceeds from what appears on the eyelid and is spread throughout; and its cause is light.
Then make an incision on it lengthwise, and gently peel it away entirely until it is removed, and then lift it with forceps and cut it off.
And if the wound is large, then you close it with sutures; and care for it until it is healed.
And if it is small, you will suffer no harm from it because the ointment will restore and heal it.
And if the growth is leaning towards the inner parts of the eyelid below the inner surface, then lift the eyelid and lift the growth with forceps, even if you need to make an incision, and press it from all sides, and if the eyelid is lengthened by the incision, this does no harm.
Then, after the incision, wash the eye with salt water; and treat the area with a healing agent until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the treatment of a stye on the eyelid above the eye.
A stye is a swelling that is generated in the membranes of the upper eyelid, and mostly affects children; it aggravates their eyes and causes them much discomfort; and they cannot look at the brightness of the sun because of it, for tears well up in them.
And therefore you see them lying on their faces all the time, and sleeping on their faces; and their lower eyelids, below the eyebrows, are moist, showing swelling, and the imminence of a stye is apparent in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the stye within.
And a certain method of operation is this: the patient places his head in your lap, and you place a cloth on him and make him a compress, and you make a circle around the stye, according to the size needed to encompass the whole stye, and place it over the stye and press with your fingers all around so that the stye is gathered in the middle of the circle, and then make an incision into the middle of that swelling with a lancet, with a slit, the slit not being larger than the slit that is made in bloodletting.
But in the operation, it is necessary that you cut through the skin completely until you reach the stye, because in most cases, the stye is expelled through the incision as a white, purulent matter.
Then extract it with cloths which you have wrapped around your fingers on the outside, and roll it between your right and left hand until it is gone.
And be careful that you do not push too hard in the incision so that with the lancet you do not reach the eye and injure it.
But if the stye does not appear at the beginning of the incision, press gently into the incision until the stye comes forth; and then extract it as we have said; and then soak cloths in wine and vinegar and apply them to the area, and compress those with powders.
And some crush salt and place it in the cavity of the incision to draw out any remaining moisture.
And then care for it until it is healed.
But if a hot abscess occurs at the site, then treat it with healing plasters until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the various methods of treating the eye.
When in the eyelids of the eyes, pustules form beneath the natural skin and are persistent, then these harm the eye and cause various diseases, such as continuous tears and softening of the eyelids and membranes, until this causes destruction of the eye, which is called tacting of the eye. It is treated in four ways: either by cautery with fire; or with acute medicine as previously mentioned in the chapter on cautery; or it is treated by incision and suture; or by canulae, as I shall explain.
And therefore it is necessary that you place the patient's head in your lap; then, turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand, and if it is turned, it is good; and if not, insert a needle into that which is full of pus beneath the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate with the pus upwards, and this should be – near the edge of the eyelid where the hairs grow – near the hairs, and pull the pus to the upper parts with the eyelid, and turn it back with a ray. (The "ray" here likely refers to a surgical instrument or a tool used to manipulate the eyelid.)
And then make an incision in the inner part of the eyelid beneath the pustules with a small, sharp instrument from the larger lacrimal duct to the smaller; and then extract the pus and place under the eyelid small powders of cotton or cloth, and then sew up the eyelid with a thread, and let it be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, the shape should be according to the amount of swelling of the eyelid, for this varies in people, for some it is necessary that a large amount of the eyelid be incised, according to the amount by which the eyelid is softened.
And then make [an incision] with the instrument along the two lines which you have sewn, and begin from the larger lacrimal duct to the smaller lacrimal duct. And if your incision is near the natural hairs, like the thickness of a king's [hair/beard]. (The meaning is unclear, and "rey" likely needs clarification.  It may refer to a specific measurement related to thickness of hair or a tool.)
And then place the hook in one of the two corners of the wound, and then scrape it all, and then join the two lips with glue or fine wool thread, and wipe away the blood, and stick what is superfluous of the threads over the two eyebrows with some adhesive substances, if you wish to do so, and if not, do not worry.
And then leave the suture of the threads for three or four days, and then care for it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without suture, and treat it with something that dries and constricts, because the eyelid is raised by the sealing of the wound and its healing; but suture is better. And therefore the ancients advised this.
However, let there be diligence regarding the patient, and it is a good operation, and there is no fear in it.
And there is another way to treat [this], and it is that you mark on the eyelid a figure like a myrtle leaf, and then raise the eyelid with three separate and joined hooks according to this form:
But if this is not possible, to hold the hooks, and this is not equal, then take a needle on which there is thread and place that in the middle of the figure, and insert the other thread near the larger tear duct, and the second thread near the smaller tear duct, and adjust the threads between your fingers with equality, and then with them, with equal elevation, raise the whole eyelid upon which the mark is made as we have recounted.
And then close the two lips of the wound with suture, and treat it until it is healed.
But if an abscess occurs near your incision or fissure, treat that abscess with a plaster and similar things, and with ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid that its hairs are inverted; then it is necessary that you use on that what we have said of incision; but suture in every case is more skillful [or artificial].
To straighten with splints is according to this method: and it is that you turn back the eyelid and cut the [skin] that is inside, as I have recounted; and then skillfully make two splints or two thin sticks whose length is according to the length of the eyelid and whose width is less than the width of the space [between the eye and the eyelid]. And you have already incised at the ends of each part, where you have the threads [or sutures].
Then carefully add each [splint] with subtlety over what is superfluous from the eyelid of the eye, and bind the splints to each part with a strong binding, and leave it for some days, for the hair by the binding will die and be dried up and rot until it falls out by itself.
But if it is delayed, then break it with forceps, and then treat the wound until it is healed.
Then, when it is solidified, the eyelid is raised and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of two splints which you will make according to this form: and let it have the quantity of length and width neither more nor less.
Chapter 12.
Of the elevation of prickly hairs in the eye with a needle when the hair is stiff.
When unnatural, hard, rough, and prickly hairs are in the eyes, then the operation is that you take a fine needle and place on it a fine silken thread and lion [possibly referring to a type of fine linen or a specific preparation], and then you adjust its ends and tie each one in a very subtle manner. And let the length of the threads be the quantity of a palm [handspan].
And then you prepare two other fine threads underneath, and tie their ends if you wish.
And then have the patient place their head in your lap, and be near the sun so that your work is illuminated, because it is a subtle and delicate operation.
And then insert the needle into the root of the offending hair, which requires skill, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you extract it from above the natural hair.
And then pull the hair upwards and insert it into the alixati (likely a solution or substance), and let there be one, two, or three hairs, and no more.
And then pull your hand with that until the hair is expelled with the alixolati (likely a solution or substance) from the eyelid along with the natural hair.
But if the alixalati is drawn out and the hair is not expelled, you will draw the alixalati towards the lower parts with the bile which you have mixed with it, until the alixalati is expelled from the upper wound.
And observe that with the vision of the eye, for then you will expel the alixalati and the bile, and clean each and close the eye, and let it be closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and good flesh grows over it.
And if the hair is shorter, then you attach a long hair from the natural hairs, and it is elevated with that, or you leave it until it is lengthened to a suitable space, and then you elevate it.
Chapter 13.
Of the treatment of the hordeolum which appears on the upper eyelid.
When a hordeolum appears in the eye, and it is a contraction of the eyelid when it is contracted upwards, or to the right or left side, due to poor healing of a wound or incision or cautery, and it is called lagophthalmos, and this hordeolum is either natural or accidental, and is caused by the healing of a wound or incision or cautery, or similar poorly healed conditions.
The method of operation is that you should incise that adhesion and separate its lips, and place between them a pledget of linen cloth, and bind them until they are healed.
And it is not fitting that you use treatments which dry or constrict; for if you do this, the hordeolum will return worse than it was before.
Use on it things that soften, such as fenugreek, and apply it with water in which mallow and linseed have been boiled, or diachylon ointment, which should be liquefied with some oil, and soak the pledget in it, and treat with that.
And the treasure of your treatment is that you ensure by every means that it is not consolidated according to the way it was in the beginning.
And this operation which we have described is nothing but a correction of the hordeolum so that it does not return to its former state.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the treatment of a stye that forms on the lower eyelid.
This stye which forms on the lower eyelid, which is truly called a stye; and it is of a natural or accidental type in it.
But an accidental stye is caused by a wound, or by a cut, or by cautery, or by other similar things.
And the method of operating on it is that you take a needle with double thread, and insert it into the flesh, and pass it from the left lachrymal gland to the right lachrymal gland, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
Then stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and sew it with the needle, and incise it with a wide spatula. And then if the shape of the eyelid returns as it should, it is good; and if not, take a splint and place it in the slit, and with it, reshape the eyelid, and close the two fissures in the lower eyelid.
And let the ends of the two fissures be two angles of the incision, which you have made so that they meet, and let an angle be formed between them, so that when they come together, their shape is formed.
And this shape is the Greek letter: lambda.
Then remove that flesh to the extent that the width of the sharp lower eyelid is, so that it becomes the eyelid.
And then join the separated parts and sew them together with wool thread; and then treat them with what we have said of emollient medicines and poultices until it is healed.
And if it is excised due to fissure, suture, or cautery, then it is necessary that you make a flat incision on the skin of the eyelid according to what has been described before. And then separate it with a poultice between two lips as we have said.
In summary, concerning the treatment of an excision whether above or below, let the operation proceed according to the preparation of the excision, which exists in many diverse forms.
And the skilled artisan always prepares the instrument in every way possible to restore the form to its natural state, or close to it, and the patient according to what is suitable for him in terms of operation and instruments in the best condition.
Chapter 15.
Of the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this consolidation happens to many men such that the eyelid is solidified above the white of the eye and its natural movement and function is impeded, then you must insert the tip of a probe beneath the eyelid and lift it up, or spread it with a probe.
And then you incise the adhesion with a fine spatula, which should not be sharply pointed, but rather blunt, so that the patient is not disturbed by the operation, and move the spatula so that it does not injure the eye with its sharpness.
And let your incision gently separate that adhesion until the eyelid is returned to its natural form and the adhesion is completely healed.
Then you put salt water in the eye with which you wash it; or distil into it some honey dissolved in water.
Then separate the eyelid from the eye with a small spatula, and place wool infused with egg white on the eye, and then on the third day use consolidating remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the incision of the nail and the fleshy eminence of the tear ducts.
Know that a nail is of two types, for it is either nervous, and resembles a hard, thin shell; or it is non-nervous, resembling a congealed white humidity, which, when the iron touches it, or is forced to take hold with tweezers, is severed and the tweezers do not penetrate it.
And both types begin only from the major tear duct until it grows little by little, covering the sight and hindering light and eye movement.
But the manner of operating in its incision is that the patient places his head in your lap, then open his eyes, and lift the eyelid with your hand, and then grasp the nail with small, double-pronged tweezers and extend it towards the upper parts. Then take a needle and insert into it a horsehair, or ox hair, or cow hair, or something similar, or strong thread, or double the point of the needle little by little and insert it into the middle of the nail, and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with thread; and extend it towards the upper parts, and scrape with the hair the edge of the nail which obscures the pupil. And thus you draw it with the hair into the back of it.
Then you incise the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a thin spatula or with small scissors, and leave the natural flesh of the tear duct so that, by the incision of it, continuous flow of tears does not occur.
And sometimes the nail, with only tweezers extended, or with thread, as we have said, and then it should be scraped with a lion spatula, of which this is the form:
And be careful that with the spatula you do not touch the cornea and that a rupture occurs in it, and that the pupil is perforated.
Then, into the incision, put a little crushed salt or a poultice made of swine fat, and leave it there until the next day; and then treat it with what is appropriate until it is healed.
And if the nail is not infected and you cannot insert the needle, nor is it embedded in the quick, then many times this type of nail is cured with medicines, and it is healed as I have described and recounted in the section.
And if you wish to incise it, then you open the wound of the patient and take a thin lion spatula of sharp steel, according to this form: with subtlety. / delicately. And if you see that it is rarefied and is confined and pressed into that root, then distill from your still into the wound a poultice made of swine fat or with goose grease or with crushed salt, and occlude the wound until the next day.
And then repeat the operation on it until all that is there is removed, unless on the contrary a hot abscess forms in the wound, for then you leave that, and treat first the abscess until it is healed.
And then repeat your operation on it with reason until it is healed.
And the eminence of the lacrimal flesh, if noticeable in the wound through great damage, should be suspended with hooks; and incise it in part, and do not extend the incision so that you do not cut the flow of tears.
And then distill into the lacrimal [area] goose grease and swine fat until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the incision of a whitlow and of that which is born of flesh attached in the wound.
Sometimes in the eyes of some, red flesh is grown, and it is super-imposed until it covers the pupil, or approaches or tightens on the eyelids, and quickly compresses the eyelids to the outer parts and is similar to a rose of a balustrade.
But the operation on him is that the patient places his head in your lap; and then open the eyes of that one and collect all that flesh with suitable scissors for that, and hold that with forceps or with tweezers; and then incise the flesh at the beginning until all is finished with the lancet, and guard yourself from the eye that you do not injure it during your operation.
And let your incision with which you incise those be with a spatula which we have recommended in the incision of the nail, or with small scissors with which hair is collected, according to what the form of it will allow near this chapter.
Then when your incision is completed and all that flesh is removed, then fill the wound with crushed salt, or distill into it red powder similar to that of corrosive medicines, and place over the wound outside a compress with egg white until it is secure from a hot abscess.
And if some cause of inflammation remains and hot blood is released, and you fear a hot abscess, then leave the eye and treat that abscess with what will relieve that abscess, and then iterate on it with an operation until it is healed, and similarly do in the swollen flesh which occurs in the eye of this kind.
But however, you should know that you should avoid all treatment of the weak eye by natural or accidental means, operating with iron, and do not put your hand to any of these operations until you see that that member can tolerate that operation.
Chapter 18.
Concerning the collection of pus in the eye.
Pus is bloody veins which are stubborn in the eye and prohibit the sight from its function, and weaken the eye with the length of days.
Therefore, it is necessary at the beginning that you consider if the eye in which there is pus is strong and in it there is no other disease than pus; then you collect that pus.
And it is that you command the patient to place his head in your lap, and then suspend those veins with an ounce or two, according to your judgment; and let that ounce be of subtle duplication according to this form:
Or two ounces gradually in a single piece according to this form:
And then let those veins be collected with fine forceps, with subtlety, and wipe away the blood little by little, until you see those veins be removed from the eye and be retracted.
And take care that you do not injure the eye with the ends of the spatula; and if your operation is in the midday before the sun, and you are very firm in your operation that you do not incise except those veins.
And when you have completed [the procedure], then you distil onto the eye verdigris or vitriol so that with its sharpness it corrodes what has been removed from the eyelid.
But if this is not possible for you, collect everything at that time, and then you plaster everything with something that soothes his pain, and leave it for a few days until his pain is calmed and the risk of a hot abscess is averted.
And then repeat on him according to the same manner until he is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps:
Chapter 19.
On the reduction of nasal dislocations.
When you treat a nasal dislocation with cautery or with a sharp, burning medicine, as narrated previously, and it is not healed, then there is no pus in it, but rather fissures on the abscess before its maturation. You should extract all the humidity which is within it and the pus until the bone is uncovered.
And then, when the bone is uncovered and you see corruption or blackness in it, then you scrape it with an instrument of which this is the form:
And it is named a rasp, and it should be made of Indian iron, and its head should be round like a chisel, which should be pointed by filing or sharpened by subtle pointing (as it is in the following form).
And place that [rasp] upon the corrupted part of the bone, and then rotate it between your fingers, and press your hand little by little until you know that that corruption is scraped away.
And this should be done several times, and then restore the area with drying and astringent substances.
Then, if the place is consolidated and good flesh has grown there, and the body of the pus is excised and remains for forty days, and is not renewed and is not festering and no accident befalls it, then you know that it is healed.
And if not, then there is nothing to do but to reduce the fissure to the shape of the nose, and according to this manner:  it is that you uncover the underlying bone with an iron instrument or with a sharp medicine, and when the bone is uncovered, then you take a trephine according to this form:
Let the end of the iron trephine be triangular, and its shaft be slender, tapering to the end like this.
And then place that [trephine] on the bone, and let it be near the lacrimal bone; and move your hand a little away from the eye, and then rotate your hand with the trephine until it penetrates the whole bone, and the patient will feel this because they will feel air escaping when you hold their hand.
Then restore the place with styptic and drying medicines as has been shown to you.
Then, when the flesh grows and the place is hardened, the matter which flowed to the outer parts returns to the nose, therefore this is easier on the patient.
Chapter 20.
Of the reduction of the protrusion of the eye.
When the eye protrudes in its entirety and there is no accompanying wound, lesion, or diminution, then it is necessary to leave the patient at rest, and see that he is followed up, and apply cupping glasses to the neck without scarification and suction for good evacuation; and apply to the eye an ointment which is made of [ingredients: the text seems to be listing ingredients, but precise translation requires knowing what "acatra" is,  and the others may also be medieval names], and tightly bind a plaster over the eye with much powder.
And the method of the powders is that you take many lion's cloths, and each cloth should be folded in four layers; and make many of them.
And then place those one on top of the other according to the amount the eye needs. And then bind tightly over them with a strong cloth with firm pressure; and repeat the cupping with suction without scarification; and then remove the cupping glasses and leave the eye bound day and night.
But if meanwhile the ligament is softened, then it must be bound very tightly.
And then remove the binding during the day and night, as we have said.
And if sometimes the eye is already reduced, it is good; and if not, then repeat the plaster with the powders and the binding and the cupping until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Concerning the incision of the eminence of the uvea tunic.  (Note: "uvea tunic" refers to the uvea, the pigmented middle layer of the eye.)
When there is a rupture in the uvea tunic, and it is prominent and protrudes beyond the eyelids like a grape, and the shape of the eye is marred by it, and you wish to incise it, then the operation is as we have recounted, that you insert the needle into the root of the uvea from below upwards, and then place another needle with double thread from the lachrymal (tear duct) side, and make it penetrate and leave the first needle according to its position; and then incise the place with the double thread and lift with it the portion of the uvea in the circumference of the needle, and tighten it strongly.
And then remove the needle and place on the eye wool infused in egg white and let the thread adhere to the uvea.
And then treat the eye with whatever strengthens it until the wound is healed.
And sometimes gather from all sides with a circle of cloth within the eye the large prominent area, and then it is perforated with a thin spatula that reaches the pupil of the eye, and the albugineous (whitish) moisture flows, and the eye descends and enters immediately.
And then tighten it until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
On the treatment of alconati (likely a type of eye disease or injury).
This illness, which is called alconati, is nothing but a swelling which is located in the eye, resembling descending water, but it is not that.
But the way to work on it is to make the patient sit on a prepared and straight seat, and then take their head in your hands on both sides and gently press it until you see the swelling descend to the lower parts, and then it should be dried so that it does not descend, and light should be blocked, and the patient should see the causes like that as they saw.
And if it does not descend downwards, know that it is water.
But if the descent of the swelling is not prepared with what we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands and take a thin spatula and use it on the pannicle, gently scraping near the conjunction of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the crown (towards the empty space), until the swelling is expelled.
Then, when it is expelled, you instill warm water into the eye in which you have mixed honey, or water in which you have previously boiled fenugreek and honey.
And then it should be treated with other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
On the treatment of the watery discharge from the eye.
We have already discussed the types of watery discharge in the division and which ones are appropriate to treat with cautery, then that which follows according to the health of the patient.
Then you should make the patient sit between your legs on a chair and bind their healthy eye shut, tying it tightly.
Then place your left hand near the eyelid of their eye, if the eye with the watery discharge is the left eye; or with your right hand, if the eye is the right one.
And then with your right hand take an almond, if the eye is the left one; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right one.
And then place the tip of the almond near the cornea, along the edge of the iris in the white of the eye, on the side of the tear duct, and then gently press the almond and roll your hand with it.
And then penetrate the white of the eye and feel that the almond has reached the empty space (or cavity).
And it should be that the path of the almond to the preon (likely referring to a specific anatomical point near the pupil) is the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the edge of the blackness (iris), and that is the cornea of the eye; for you will see with that pupil through the clarity of the corneal tunic.
And then place the almond into the place where the water (presumably referring to aqueous humor) is, and then you draw it down one time then another, and then if the water descends immediately, then the patient will see that upon which he focuses his sight immediately, and the almond in his eye.
And then rest a little; then if the water has descended, you should remove the almond before it is expelled.
And then when it is dried and does not descend, then you extract the almond easily, and gently roll it with your hand, and then dissolve a little clear rock salt in the water and wash the eye inside with it; and then place on the outside of the eye cotton or tow or wool infused in rose oil and egg white and bind the eye with this.
But we and those of our time place upon that a bruised comfrey (herb) with egg white.
And if the almond does not obey you upon entering the eye because of its hardness—for there are some men whose eyes are very hard—then it is necessary that you take the spatula which is called an alberid, of which this is the form:
And then perforate that joint with it, and do not thrust your hand into the hole, and it is only that you make a subtle pathway for the almond.
And then introduce the almond as we have said.
And for the completion of your work, let the patient be prepared a quiet, dark sleeping place where he may lie on his back, and let all movement be prohibited.
And place him in such a position that his body is aligned, and he does not turn his head to the right or left at all.
And let the dressing remain, according to its suitability, until the third day.
And then unbind it in that dark place and expose the ulcer to view, and show him certain things, and then reapply the dressing until the seventh day.
And it is not fitting that you do this at the hour of the treatments or immediately after the infusion. Beforehand, it is necessary that you remove this, because the water might lightly, through great and vehement examination, [escape/leak/be lost].
But if a hot abscess occurs, then it is necessary that you unbind the wound before the seventh day, and treat it with something that reduces the abscess.
Then you cleanse his sight and place over his face a poultice by which his sight may be governed beneath him for some days, him existing in the darkest place.
And then let him be removed from that dark place gradually and attend to his matters/affairs.
And know that in the case of a cataract, the pupil is not excused from vision at certain times, and then you should be bold about the operation.
And it has come to us from some of those who are in Artach, and some say that fire is made in Ayrac, a perforated vessel by which the water is sucked.
However, I have not seen anyone in our land who has perfected this, nor have I read this in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that this is new.
And these are the forms of the kinds of vessels, so that he who is ignorant of them may know them:
And let them be made of bronze or iron properly, and let their ends be with this triangular subtlety, the sharp end thus, as that is the form.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the treatment of rotting flesh in the nose.
When in the nose there are born various fleshy growths, of which some are similar to a very small scorpion, or to a polyp fish; and this illness is called polyp because it resembles that fish in appearance. And from it is cancerous flesh,  scaly, of dark color; and from it is flesh like a lion, not of dark color.
Therefore, in that which is in these fleshy growths, not malignant nor cancerous, you should make the patient sit between your hands and place [him] in the sun, and open his nostrils, and lightly touch those growths with your finger. Then extract that to the outside parts, and then incise what you have grasped of those growths with a sharp, thin spatula from one side until you know that all the flesh has been removed; that if any part remains from which excision is possible, then you scrape that with one of the subtle instruments easily until nothing remains of it. But if it bleeds profusely or a hot abscess forms, then you should apply what is necessary; or if it is one of the malignant abscesses, then you should incise and cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the cavities are removed.
Then let there be poured into the nose near the incision, strong wine and water or wine. But if the nose is open and moisture flows from it into the palate, then know that it is healed.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then know that inside there is flesh or pus remaining in the upper bones of the nose to which excision has not reached.
Then it is necessary that you introduce a linen thread into it, to which some thickness is added, and tie knots in it, placing between each knot the quantity of a finger or less, and  guide the patient, and introduce one end of the thread into the nose with a rod or with any other means that is possible for you.
And after this is done, let it draw its odor until it reaches the canals of the nose, and it will come out over the throat, because often children do something similar at school, and it is an easy thing for someone who wants to do it.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end emerging from the nose and the other remaining inside the nose, and then administer sawdust or small pieces of meat with the knots that are on the thread, and do this until you know that the flesh is now held by the knots of the thread.
Then remove the thread, and place near the nose, near the extraction of blood, a warm compress, and do this for three days or more until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
Then, at the end of the treatment, place a lead cannula in the nose for a few days until it is healed.
But if you need a more drastic cure, use that one.
And this is the form of the instrument, a nasal purgative, with which oils and medicines are distilled into the nose:
And make this of silver or bronze, which should resemble a small open lamp, like a small shell, and the opening similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula like a tube, and the cannula of the nasal purgative instrument should be flat and open;  it should have a handle at its rear, with which you hold it when you heat the oil or whatever you want of the juices and extractable substances in it.
Chapter 25.
Of the pimples [or boils] in the extremities of the nose.
Many times a pimple forms in the extremities of the nose, and it is magnified and increased in a few days to the point that it is easily visible.
And for that reason it is necessary that you incise it at the beginning of its appearance, and eradicate all of it. And then place on the area either a cautery or a burning medicine which will remain in place.
But if its incision is delayed until it is magnified, then consider if it is hardened, firm, of a dark color, and with little feeling; then do not touch it with iron, for it is a cancerous abscess; because many times whoever incises this abscess suffers great harm from it.
And if the abscess is soft to the touch and not of a dark color, and sometimes incision is possible throughout, then evacuate the patient and incise it without fear, and treat the area with something that dries and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Chapter 26. Of the suturing of the nose, lips, and ear when their continuity is broken by a wound or similar injury.
Know that when a solution of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, you should rarely undertake that operation except in certain cases.
Therefore, when such a thing happens to someone, consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood, and then you should approximate the two lips of the wound with sutures, and afterwards treat it until it is healed.
And if the lips of the solution of continuity are already separated, and each is healed, then you must scarify each part of its apparent edge until blood comes out. Then approximate the two lips with sutures and tighten them, and over them sprinkle dragon's blood and finely ground egg shells, and place over the powder a piece of diachylon plaster, or some other consolidating plaster, and leave it bound for two or three days, then unbind it and change the plaster and leave it until the stitches themselves are removed, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
But the manner of suturing is that you should close the solution of continuity either with needles, as we have described in the suturing of the abdomen, or close the solution of continuity with thread, as has been shown to you.
Chapter 27.
Of the extraction of those things which come between the two lips in the inner parts.
Often there appear in the inner parts of the lips of many men certain hard abscesses resembling goose grease, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore it is necessary that you should separate the lips and open each of those hardnesses on all sides, and then fill the place of the abscess with crushed sage until the bleeding is stopped, and then wash it with sharp wine and treat the place with something styptic until it is healed.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the incision of the flesh grown in the gums.
Very often a fleshy growth appears on the gum, which the ancients called an albulus; then it is necessary that you lift that up with forceps or hold it with forceps and incise it down to its root, and let the serum or blood flow.
And then place crushed sage or other styptic or drying powders on the place.
And if that flesh returns, then incise it a second time, and cauterize it, for then the cautery will prevent it from returning.
Chapter 29.
Concerning the extraction of teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth, both inner and outer parts, and between the gums, rough, sticky, and sometimes blackened crusts are found, and they become citrine or green, extending to the gum, then corruption occurs due to this, and the teeth become loose.
And therefore it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands and rest their head on your lap, and scrape the teeth on which those crusts and similar substances appear, like sand, until none remains, removing the blackness, yellowness, and other substances until they are cleaned.
Therefore, if what was mentioned at the beginning of the treatment is achieved, it is good; and if not, then you repeat the treatment on those teeth on the following day, and the second, and the third, until you finally achieve what you want.
And know that teeth require many different kinds of instruments and various shapes, according to the preparation for your operation. And because the instrument with which the tooth is scraped from the inside is different from the instrument with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other forms, these forms of instruments must be prepared by you, which you will see immediately:
Chapter 30.
On the extraction of teeth.
That you treat the tooth's pain with all skill and delay its extraction, for there is no cause that compensates for its loss when it is extracted, for it is a sublime substance whose extraction is to be excused.
But when the patient is forced to have that tooth extracted, you must be firmly certain which tooth is affected, for many times the pain deceives the patient and he thinks it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one and then the pain does not cease until the diseased and aching tooth is extracted, for we have already seen this through the operation of barbers.
Therefore, when you are certain which tooth is aching, and you know it well, then you should scarify around the tooth with a spatula containing some virtue, until it is loosened from the gum on all sides, and then remove it with your fingers, or with fine forceps, little by little at first, until it is completely loosened.
And then apply strong, large forceps for good traction or firm hold, and place the patient's head between your knees so that you hold him well and he does not move, and then pull the tooth straight in such a way that you do not break it, nor cause the patient greater pain.
And then, if it is not removed, take one of those instruments and place it under the tooth on all sides with ease, and force its removal in the same way as was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is cracked or decayed, then you should fill that crack with cloth and tighten it with a fine thread so that it is not broken at the moment of your traction on it with forceps.
And you must carefully open with scarification around all parts of the gum and take great care that you do not break it and leave part of it behind, and it returns to the patient with even worse consequences than the initial pain.
And beware that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their haste and boldness in extraction, without administering what we have recommended; for many times they cause great illnesses in men, which are more difficult to cure, and their roots remain behind, either all of them, or some of them, or they extract them along with some bones from the jaw, as we have witnessed many times.
And after its extraction, the mouth should be washed with hot wine or vinegar with salt.
And if bleeding ensues from this, as often happens, then crush some quantity of alum and sprinkle it on the wound, and if this alum does not work, cauterize it.
The shape of the forceps and the fine tongs with which the tooth is grasped at the beginning should be long in the gripping part, short and thick in the handles, so that they do not bend when you use them.
And let them be of such thickness in the handles that when you squeeze them, they are not bent; let the gripping parts be short.
And let them be made of Indian iron, carefully made with straight gripping parts, and at the ends of these let there be teeth, some interlocking with others, and clamping with strong constriction.  And sometimes the ends of these should be shaped like the beak of a bird called a *ciconia* (that is, a stork), and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter 31.
On the removal of teeth and the extraction of broken jawbones.
When you must remove a tooth which is fractured and some part remains, then you should place a poultice on the area for a day or two until the area is softened; and then insert forceps, whose ends are shaped like the beak of a bird called a *ciconia* or stork, the shape of forceps:
Their ends should be made inside like a file or rasp.
And if it does not yield to you easily with these forceps or pincers, then you should excavate around the root and remove all the flesh and insert the instrument which is called an *atali*, that is, a small lever, whose shape is this:
And let it have a short, somewhat thick end, and let it not be curved so that it will not break.
Therefore, if by this the root is extracted, it is good; and if not, help yourself with these other instruments, of which these are the forms: the form of the first instrument is of a triangular end, which should have some thickness:
The form of the second instrument is that it should be triangular and of a more subtle/fine point.
And you are already aided with this instrument having two prongs, of which this is the form approximately/following: and with other instruments which precede in tooth extraction; for help is made with this instrument which resembles a large hook, and this is its form:
of a triangular, curved or short end, which should have some thickness, and should not be bent so that it is not broken.
And know also that the instruments for molars are similar to the other instruments.
But the wise and subtle artisan in the art finds for himself, according to what the operation and the various diseases signify to him; for there are diseases to which the ancients did not give or devise a proper instrument, because of the forms of diverse species.
But if the bone of the jaw or of the other bones of the mouth is broken, or if it can be, then let it be cured in its place with what is convenient for it from among the instruments and forceps which we have mentioned in the extraction of the roots of the teeth, and help yourself with a splint, of which this is the form:
And let there be some thickness in it little by little, and let it be tightened or held with the mouth so that it does not escape or flee until the bone is completely set; and then restore the place with medicines convenient to it.
But if in the bone there is a small rottenness, then scrape away that rottenness and its blackness, and cure it until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
Of the treatment of teeth that are prominent above the others.
When the teeth are prominent, not according to their natural course, their form is flawed, and especially when it happens to women, and especially to venerable old women; therefore you must consider: if a tooth is close to another tooth, and closure or alignment is not possible, then extract it; but if it is not attached to another tooth, then you should incise it with this instrument, of which this is the form; and it is similar to a boar's tusk, and should be made of Indian iron with a very sharp tip.
And let your incision, with which you incise it, be done in several days because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that no other teeth are injured.
And if it is a prominent tooth suitable for filing, then file it with an Indian iron file of which this is the form:
And let it be entirely of Indian iron and very sharp; and let it have very small points, like a file with which needles are made, with which the tooth should be filed little by little over several days easily, so that no other tooth is injured or damaged. Then smooth it at the end, and scrape it with some scrapers.
And if it is a tooth of which a part is broken and injures the tongue, then it is necessary that you file it until the roughness of that fracture is removed and smoothed, and it is filled so that the tongue is not injured nor the speech impaired.
Chapter 33.
Concerning the restoration of damaged teeth with gold or silver wire.
When the front teeth suffer concussion and loosening from a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on what he eats, and you have treated this with styptic medicines, and your treatment has not been effective, then, so that they do not fall out, contrive that they be fastened with gold wire and not silver, for silver turns green and soon powders after a few days, but gold is never changed.
And let the wire be of such fineness and thickness according to the amount that can fit between the teeth; and the manner of binding is that you take the wire and pass double the length between two healthy teeth, and then let it loop with two turns of wire between the damaged ones, whether one or several, until the wire reaches a healthy tooth on the other side. And then repeat the weaving back to the point where you began, and tighten with your hand gently and skillfully until it is firm.
And let your tightening, with which you fasten the wire, be around the roots of the teeth so that it does not come loose.
And then cut off the superfluous ends of the wire with forceps or scissors, and smooth those ends at each location, and fold them and hide them between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth, so that they do not injure the tongue. And then leave it thus tightened, like a Roman [brace/bandage].
But if it is loosened or broken, tighten it or them with another wire in the same manner at all times.
And this is the form of the teeth and the figure of the straightening of two healthy teeth and two damaged teeth, thus as you see these bound:
And when it is done that one or two teeth return to the alignment of each in their places, and they are grown as we have recounted, and they remain firm.
And this is only done by good and skilled artisans.
And sometimes some material from cow bones is used, and it is fashioned into the shape of a tooth, and is placed in the place where the tooth is missing, and is fixed as we have said, and remains and serves in that way for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Of the incision of the ligament which occurs in the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament, which is found under the tongue, is sometimes natural, with which one is born, or accidental and occurs due to a wound which has already healed.
And the operation is that you open the patient's mouth, their head being in your lap; and raise their tongue, and then incise that nervous ligament according to its width until the tongue is freed from its restriction.
But if in that area there is some hardness or knotting, and this is due to the healing of a wound, then you make an incision and divide it across its width until you have incised the ligament and the knotting is dissolved.
And be careful that no fissure is made in the base of the flesh and that you do not cut too deeply and cause a flow of blood.
Then let the site of the incision be washed with warm water or with sour wine or with cold water; and then place under the tongue linen; that is, a cloth which the patient should keep under their tongue each night so that it does not heal again.
