But if bleeding occurs, place crushed vitriol on the area; but if the bleeding persists, then cauterize the area with a lancet-shaped cautery which is suitable for this. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
On the extraction of a ranula from under the tongue.
Sometimes an abscess resembling a small tumor occurs under the tongue, preventing the tongue from its natural function; and it is quickly enlarged until it fills the mouth.
And the operation on it is that you open the patient's mouth, sitting in front of the sun, and examine the abscess.
And if you see it is dark or black in color, hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it, then do not touch it, for it is cancer.
And if it is inclined to whiteness, and is moist, then you place your fingernail there, and incise it with a fine spatula, and incise it in all parts; and if blood flows during your operation, then you place crushed verdigris, that is, verdigris, upon it until the bleeding is stopped.
Then return to your work until you have completely removed it, until it is washed with sharp vinegar and salt; and treat it with the other appropriate treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 36.
Chapter 36. On the extraction of an abscess from the two ears and from that which is located in the throat and of other abscesses.
When a swelling occurs in the throat, these are glands which are gathered together like cotton, which protrude externally and are called tonsils; when you treat them with what we have said in the previous section and it is not healed, then you consider if the abscess is hard, of a dark color, with little sensation; then do not touch it with an iron instrument.
And if it is red and the root is thick, then do not touch it with an iron instrument because of the flow of blood; rather, let it mature, and then either you will extract it, or it will burst of its own accord.
And if it is white, soft, and its root is thin, then it is one that should be incised.
And the operation is that you consider, before your operation, if the abscess has already matured through complete rest or if it has diminished; and then have the patient sit facing the sun, with their head in your lap, and open their mouth, and the assistant, taking hold with their hands, presses down the patient's tongue with a very fine instrument of which this is the form:
And let it be made of silver or bronze, and let it be as thin as a knife.
Then, as you press the tongue with that and the abscess is shown to you, and you look at it carefully, then take tweezers and place them in the tonsil, and pull it outwards as much as you can. Also, if you pull out something from the membranes, then immediately incise it with an instrument of which this is the form:
Also, if you pull out something from the membranes, then immediately incise it with an instrument of which this is the form: and it resembles forceps, however its ends are curved, so that the opening of each is opposite the other, very sharp, which should be of tempered iron.
But if this instrument is not at hand, then incise it with a spatula of which this is its form: And let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you have incised one side, incise the other in the same manner of incision, and immediately after the incision, let the patient rinse with cold water.
But if there is a flow of blood, rinse with a decoction of pomegranate rind or myrtle leaves and similar styptic substances until the flow of blood is restrained, and immediately treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses appear in the throat besides those two ears, and incise those as I have said in the incision of two sides equally.
And I myself once treated a woman with an abscess which had formed in her throat, which abscess was of a dark color, with little feeling, which had already closed her throat, and the woman was breathing through a narrow passage, and I had already forbidden her food and drink of water, and she would have reached death if she had lasted one or two more days.
And the abscess had grown so much that it had made two openings through which it exited.
Therefore I treated it with a fistula, and inserted a probe into one of the openings, and pulled it, and a large portion was extracted from it. And immediately I incised the part which I understood to be related to the other opening, and immediately I opened its mouth, and compressed its tongue, and immediately I inserted the probe into that abscess.
And immediately I excised a large part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Therefore the throat of that woman was freed, and she immediately began to drink water, and soon she took food. Therefore I did not cease to incise that abscess many times over a long time, and another abscess succeeded the first, which I incised until it was completely healed.
Therefore I cauterized his throat, thus I stopped the infection; and immediately I went for a walk and did not know what God would do about it immediately.
Chapter 37.
Concerning the incision of a wen when it is abscessed.
When catarrh descends to the uvula and it is abscessed and elongated, then it is called a perpendicular swelling; and if it is in the lower part, thick and round, then it is called a bubo.
But when it is treated with what we have said in the division, and your treatment is not effective, and you see that the cauterized abscess is removed from it and is subtle, then you should incise that which is round and aggregated; and if it is of such length, and is bloody, of dark color or black, and there is no feeling in it, then you should avoid incising it, for in this there is danger for the patient.
And when you see it according to the manner I have described, its whiteness and length, you should make the patient sit and press down their tongue with an instrument of appropriate shape, and then insert the lancet into the uvula, and pull it downwards and incise it with one of the two instruments which are described in the incision of the tonsils.
And you should not incise it except for that which is within the natural limit without addition, for if you incise more, you will impair their speech.
And for the incision, use what we have recommended in the incision of the tonsils, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision, you should use ingenuity in cauterizing it without causing fear, and the method of cauterizing it is only with sharp water, and it is that you place the patient's head in your lap, and then press their tongue with the instrument we have mentioned, and then take the water which we have mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and apply it with a live coal, and do not apply it thickly or thinly, and with this fill the concavity of the instrument of which this is the shape:
Let its extremity be concave, in which the medicine is placed, like the concavity of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula, and let the patient lie on their side so that saliva or mucus from their mouth flows away because of the inward medicine, so that nothing descends from it onto their throat and harms them.
And then take your hand with the medicine, and press it on the uvula for half an hour until you see it become blackened and dried by the bite of the medicine.
And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the end of the spoon, and submerge the cotton in the medicine, and insert the spoon with the cotton into a tube above so that the cotton adheres to that tube, and do this as many times as needed until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the uvula, and then leave it because it will dry and fall off after three or four days.
And if you judge that you should repeat the medicine, repeat it, and after the cauterization, wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in wine, and cleanse with it what remains around it from the medicine, and wash it with cold water, and treat externally with an embrocation, and internally with gargles until it is healed.
And this kind of treatment is safer than incision and less frightening.
Sometimes the uvula is cured with a method more subtle than cautery or incision, because it is cured with fumigation in this manner: take calamint, hyssop, origanum, rue, dried arabic gum, chamomile,  and similar herbs, and combine all these ingredients, or some of them, in a pot, and submerge them in sour wine, and boil; and the pot should be well sealed with a suitable lid, and in the middle of the pot there should be a hole, over which a concave instrument of brass should be placed in this manner; it may be made of silver or brass.
And insert the end [of the concave instrument] into the patient's mouth so that the vapor may reach the uvula through the funnel, until the uvula is sufficiently fumigated.
And then repeat this several times until it is dry, and be careful not to perform this treatment at the beginning of the development of an abscess, because it would excessively irritate the abscess, and you should not do that, but rather you should treat the declared abscess with cautery.
But if you do not have this instrument at hand, then take a reed and attach a piece of eggshell to its end, so that the patient's mouth may be protected, because the eggshell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter 38.
Concerning the extraction of thorns and that which adheres in the throat and other causes.
Many times a fish bone or some other thing adheres in the throat.
Then it is necessary that you extract that which is apparent, upon which your sight falls, after you pull the tongue forward with an instrument in the sunlight, so that that which is in the throat may be revealed to you.
And that which does not appear and is hidden from you in the throat, it is necessary that you make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for very quickly the cause attached to the throat will be expelled by vomit; or that the patient swallow a piece of turnip or lettuce root or a piece of dry bread; or take a piece of dried sea sponge, and bind it tightly with bile, and then have the patient swallow it, for when it arrives at the place where the thorns are, it will extract them and they will be expelled. And moreover, take an eel and bind it with bile, and let it be put inside and quickly extracted; or take a leek and quickly insert it and quickly remove it.
But if they are not expelled by those means which we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument according to this form:
And let it be thicker than a finger by a little, and at its end let there be a concavity which you introduce into his throat with ease.
And he should have his head raised up, and be careful not to touch his throat bone, so that it does not happen to him that with that you push the bone or the thorn, or that the physician does it with his hand.
But however, it is better that the patient himself introduce it, because of his knowledge of the location of the attached cause, and pushes it down, and pulls his hand with the instrument upwards, all this according to the amount that is prepared for him until it is expelled.
Chapter 39.
Concerning the extraction of a leech attached to the throat.
When a leech is medicated with what we have said in the division of treatment with medicines and it does not work, then consider placing the patient's throat in the sun, after you have compressed his tongue with the instrument which we have recommended to you.
Therefore, if you see the leech, extract it with small forceps or with gentle, careful movements.
And if it is not possible for you to extract it with that, then take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat, and then place a heated iron in the concavity of the cannula, and do this as many times (until the leech is dead and detaches from the throat) and abstain from water all day.
And then take a vial full of cold water, and open his mouth into it, and wash the mouth with it; and do not swallow anything from it and stir the water every now and then with your hand, because the leech immediately falls when it feels the water.
But if it is not expelled by what we have recommended, then fumigate the patient with cyme (or possibly *cyminum*, cumin) or with asafoetida using the instrument that we recommended for the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it will fall.
And another method of operation in the fumigation of it, is that you take a pot in which there are burning coals, and the pot is covered with a cover in the middle of which is a hole, and the end of the instrument is placed in that hole; and then let the fumigation go out from above; and let the patient put his mouth on the end of the cannula, and close his mouth so that the fumigation is not expelled until you know that the fumigation has reached the leech, for the leech will immediately fall.
And if it does not fall, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and abstain the patient from drinking, and eating salty things and garlic, and do not drink water, and thus it is necessary that with this regime it will be expelled.
And this is the form of the instrument with which a leech is drawn from the throat when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps like these sometimes, except that there is some curvature in it: which is placed in the throat, and the end of it is similar to a stork's beak, which is hollow, in which there is a roughness from filing, so that, when it grasps something, it does not let go.
Chapter 40.
In which is the sum of the discourse concerning the opening of abscesses and their incision.
Abscesses are of many species, of many kinds, according to what their recollection shows, of each one in this book.
And they are diverse in their perforation and incision in two ways, of which one is by the species of abscesses themselves; and by what we understand of the humors.
And the second way is according to the locations in which they occur in the body, for an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the buttocks; and an abscess in a fleshy place is different from an abscess occurring in a joint; and each has its own judgment for operation.
And moreover, some abscesses should not be perforated except near the maturation of the pus within them or their completion.
There are other abscesses which should be opened when they are raw, and not yet matured according to their completion, such as abscesses that are near the joints, because when an abscess is near a joint, its being is prolonged until it consumes that which is in its circumference, and very quickly the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted, wherefore a cause of contraction of that limb is made; or it is an abscess near a principal member, and if you delay its perforation until it is matured, you will harm the principal member; or it is near the anus, therefore perforate it even if not matured; and you should not perforate it raw, of incomplete digestion, except that which is near the anus, so that its pus may not flow and be elongated to the inner parts of the anus, and a fistula be made, or that it be made in such a way that it is not healed. Moreover, you must know the time for the perforation of the abscess, and this is when it is matured.
And know that it is matured when there is a lessening of the pain and the cessation of the fever, and also the diminution of the redness and of the pulsation, and the acuteness of the head of the abscess, and the other signs.
And because it is necessary that you know how to make the perforation fall into the lowest part of the abscess and from where the cure proceeds from the prominence.
And the perforation should always be along the length of the flesh or body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands or feet, and in the place of the tendons, of the cords of the nerves, of the arteries; and, finally, in all smooth places where there is no reflection; because in those which are reflected, use a perforation according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy place, then it is better that you leave the perforation of it until its digestion is completed, as we have said; for if you perforate it before that, the course of its pus and much filth is prolonged, and very quickly its lips are hardened and its depth increased.
But some abscesses are perforated according to the width of the flesh or body because of necessity, or according to what the member requires.
And it is necessary that you administer a small perforation in small abscesses, and in large abscesses a large and wide perforation, or many incisions, according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And others are abscesses in which it is necessary that the skin be incised and hollowed out, when it is made like bread and is ripe, as happens in cases of buboes and swellings.
And some of these are incised with a three-angled incision; and others are incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf, like the abscesses of the armpits.
And there are those in which a round incision and a crescent-shaped incision are administered, and similar ones.
But that which is not like the aforementioned superficial abscesses, you must perforate only with a superficial or flat perforation.
And when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, you should perforate it and do not worry about extracting all the pus at once; having extracted a portion of it, then pack the abscess until the next day, and then extract a larger portion, and do this many times gradually until it is completely evacuated.
Especially if the patient is of weak constitution, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an elderly person in decrepitude, because the animal spirit is often dissolved with the egress of pus, and the patient dies very quickly, and you open them.
Therefore, observe this chapter with great caution, and concerning your perforation in which you perforate these abscesses, you must clean the wounds and consider whether the wound of the abscess is small, or is a flat incision; then you apply lint or old linen; and if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the perforation are many, then you must place a wick in each opening until they join together.
And if in the abscess a part of the skin is excised or you have hollowed it out in a circle, then you must fill it with old linen or thread made from linen cloth, as when you have drawn out splinters or roots, without moisture; and dress it on the third day; and then treat it and care for it with the ointments needed until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs during your operation, then apply cold water and sour wine in which you soak a linen cloth, and apply this to the bleeding site many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then you must administer the regimen and treatment and powders which I have described in many places in this our book and division.
But if it is winter, and the location of the abscess is a place of many nerves, then you must mix powders in warm wine and oil, and apply those to the area.
And if it is summer, and the areas are fleshy, then you should apply powders moistened with water and oil or with wine and oil, and all this should be cold until the third day, as we have said, and then you should open the abscess and cleanse it and administer to its treatment what is appropriate for it until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally, but according to the way of distinction I have already spoken of every abscess, in what manner is the way of its treatment, by chapters individually.
Chapter 41.
Concerning the incision of abscesses which occur in the scalp.
In the scalp, small abscesses are found, and they are a type of node which contains tunics that are in their layers; and their types are many.
And some of them are purulent, and those contain a humidity similar to slime. And some of them contain a humidity similar to pus, and resembling it; and some of them are stony, hard.
And in the incision of all of them, and in the extraction of those, there is no fear, since the extraction of those does not cause contrary harm.
And the operation in the incision of these is that you probe them with an instrument whose shape is appropriate for this, which is called a probe, until you know what it contains.
For if what it contains is humidity, then incise it lengthwise with a superficial incision according to this form:
And begin the incision from line B to line G.  Then, when the humidity is evacuated, you scrape that skin which contained the humidity, and do not leave any of it behind, for often the malady returns if any of it remains.
And then submerge cotton in Egyptian ointment, if it is available, and if not, in water and salt; and fill the wound with it and leave it there until the next day, for with this you corrode everything that is remaining of that membrane.
And then repeat the application of cotton with Egyptian ointment a second and third time if necessary, until you are confident that no part of that membrane remains; then heal the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodules, then you incise it according to this form: with an incision in the shape of a cross, and insert forceps between the two lips of the incision and scrape it from every part, and exert your strength in the extraction of the tunic which contains it.
But if your attempt meets with resistance, then do what I recounted to you at first.
And similarly proceed with a stony abscess in incision and treatment as we have told you; and incision on a stony abscess is easier, for it has little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes some of these abscesses, which are in the head in some men, have no moisture at all; the reason for this is that I incised an abscess on the head of an old woman and found the abscess like a dull, rough, white stone, which could not be cut, and if anyone were struck with it, it would hurt them.
But in other accidental abscesses in the head other than this, this chapter does not apply, such as abscesses that occur on the heads of children and behind the roots of the ears, but the incision of these should all be superficial incisions.
And make the perforation of those in the lowest part of them so that the body is relieved of the pus in the lower parts.
And then treat those with what is appropriate for that treatment.
Chapter 42.
Chapter 42. On the incision of scrofulas which occur in the neck.
Often these abscesses occur in the neck, and in the armpits, and in the groin; and sometimes they occur in other parts of the body.
And those that occur in the neck, and many that are generated among them, and every scrofula of those is within its own tunic, and just as in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have recounted.
And the types of these scrofulas are many, for some of them are stony, and some of them contain humidity, and some of them are malignant which do not obey cure.
Therefore, in that which you see to be of good disposition on touching, of which the color that is manifestly apparent is close to the color of the flesh, and is softened in every part, and is not attached to the nerves of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the artery, and is not deep, it is fitting that you incise that with a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body along the line of G. within the line of B., and scrape that from every part, and extend the lips of the incision with forceps, or with many forceps if you have need of them, just as we have said in the abscesses of the head, and extract that little by little.
And it is most important that the vein or nerve not be incised.
And let the scalpel not be too sharp, lest it slip in your hand during the incision, or that it disturb the patient, and that you incise what is not necessary to incise; for if you incise a vein or artery and prohibit yourself from your operation, then place on the wound crushed vitriol or other powders which stop the flow of blood, and bind the wound, and leave it until the acuteness of the abscess subsides and the wound is softened and begins to become purulent. When the blood is cut off.
Then return to your operation until you have completed it.
And then inquire with your finger if there is any cause of the other small scrofulas, then incise those and cleanse them.
And if there is a large vein at the root of the scrofula, then you must not incise that scrofula at its root. Beforehand, it is necessary that you bind that with strong doubled thread and that you leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old linen that you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment, and then care for it.
And if you incise the entire scrofula, then you must adjust the two lips of the wound and stitch it as soon as there is no remaining superfluity.
But if you see that there remains superfluity because of the size of the scrofula, you must understand how to incise its root, and extract it and administer suture and what has been said.
And as for those scrofulas that contain humidity, perforate it with a superficial perforation where the place of its maturation appears to you, and position the perforation so that it is the lowest part of the body, as we have said.
Then administer a perforation, perhaps with ointment and similar things, so that it corrodes what remains of the corruption. And then, when you see the wound to be already cleansed, then you cure it with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
Chapter 43.
Concerning the incision of the head of the abscess accidentally within the throat.
The ancients described this incision in the abscess, but no one in our land has been seen to have performed it.
And this is the description/writing of the discourse of those (people/ancients).
But in men having a throat abscess, the extraction of the head (of the abscess) must be avoided, because it would not be beneficial, for all the veins and major ones of the patient are there.
But in those who have abscesses located in the mouth or throat or in both ears, when the illness is not in the throat (itself/specifically), it is necessary to use the incision of the head (of the abscess) to avoid the damage that is done by perforation (or, a more literal translation, "that which is made by piercing").
Therefore, when we want to do this, we should incise the head (of the abscess) below the three circles of the circles of the head (of the abscess), or four with a small incision in the width, which is between two circles in quantity, so that the incision is in the membrane, not in the cartilage, and this place is suitable for incision, because it is devoid of flesh and its blood vessels are far away.
And if the physician is timid/fearful, then it is necessary that he extend the throat's lining with a hook, and then split the lining until he reaches the canal, but he should avoid the blood vessels if he sees any of them.
And then incise the membrane as we have recounted; and the incision of the canal is indicated by the inflammation which comes from it with what is broken from it and by the loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Then, as the time is reached in which perforation is feared, bring together the two lips of the wound's lining, and sew that alone, without the cartilage.
And then administer medicines that promote the growth of flesh until it is healed.
And Albucasis says that the summary of the discourse which we have recounted, the explanation is only that you see that some of these abscesses have already obstructed the throat of the patient, and the patient has come close to death, and his breath is cut off, and some proceed to incise the epiglottis so that the patient may breathe above the location of the wound for some respiration, and be saved from death.
And for that reason I order the wound to be left open until the malignancy of the illness is consumed; and its malignancy lasts for three days; and then I order the wound to be sewn, and cure it until it is healed.
And this which I have seen with my own eyes, is that a servant took a knife and put it in her throat and incised with it a part of the windpipe, and I was called to her cure, and I found the mucus as it is found in those who are dying, and I uncovered the wound and found the blood which had come out from the small wound.
Then I was confessed that she had not incised a vein or artery, and the wind issued from the wound, and I cured it until it was healed.
And there occurred to the servant nothing but hoarseness in the voice, and after some days she returned to improvement in her condition.
And now I say that in the incision of the throat there is no fear.
Chapter 44.
Concerning incision upon an abscess which occurs in the throat externally, and is called a throat hernia.
This abscess named throat hernia is a large abscess according to the constitution of the body, and is very common in women.
And it is according to two manners, for it is either natural or accidental.
And in the natural [case], there is no way to cure it.
And the accidental is according to two manners, of which one resembles fatty nodes, and the other manner resembles an abscess which is caused by a knotting of an artery, and in that case there is fear [of danger]. And it is not fitting that one should use iron [surgery] on him except in that which is small. And you probe that and investigate it with the finger; and if you find it similar to a fatty node, and it is not connected to any of the veins, then you incise it like the nodes, and extract it with that which contains it from the skin and blister; and if not, then its entire being is examined, and then cure the place with that which is needed for healing until it is healed.
Chapter 45.
Concerning the incision of the types of nodes.
There are many types of nodes, and I have already described all their types in the division [of chapters].
And it is necessary that I recount thus the difference between a node and an abscess, lest there be doubt.
Therefore I say that with an abscess there is heat and fever and vehement pain until the boiling of the superfluity is settled; and putrefaction is complete, then the fever, pain, and heat cease.
But with a node there is neither heat nor fever nor pain, and it contains that lymphatic fluid which is in its own sac.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is like a swelling and is made like a vesicle, either larger or smaller, and there are two kinds, for they are either fatty and contain humidity, and the colors of the humidity are many, as I said in the division [of chapters].
Therefore it is necessary, when you arrive at the cure of a node, that you explore it and investigate it at the beginning with an instrument which is called a probe, according to the form which will appear in the chapter which is next to this one.
And the manner of investigating abscesses and all nodes is that you take this instrument and introduce it into the most humid place which you find in the abscess, and you will move your fingers with it little by little until you know that the instrument has penetrated the pus.
And then place your hand according to the size of the abscess, and then remove the probe and consider what has come out in its wake.
And if the flowing humidity, of whatever color it may be, is expelled, then you should incise it with a superficial incision as I have said in the case of other abscesses.
And if no humidity comes from the wake of the probe, then know that it is fatty (or thick/dense).
And dry it out with an incision in the form of a cross, according to this shape: as I have shown you, and suspend it with hooks, and easily peel the skin from all sides, and keep the skin or sac, if you can, over it and extract that pus with the node (or knot, referring to a surgical instrument).
But if it is broken during the operation, it is not possible to extract all the pus, and this often happens, and then you extract it in pieces until nothing remains.
Because if anything remains, little or much, the node (or abscess) will return again.
And if you see that some small part remains, then fill the wound with some sharp corrosive powders and bind the wound tightly, and place a hot poultice over it, and treat it with other cures until it is healed.
And if it is a large node (abscess), then you sew the two lips of it together and treat it so that it heals by granulation (fleshing over).
But if a pulsating vein is found in the opposite direction or a non-pulsating one, then you cauterize and fill the area with crushed vitriol and leave it tight for two or three days until the wound dries and the boiling of the blood ceases; and then return to incise what remains of the node.

Chapter 47.
On the treatment of the breasts of men which resemble those of women.
Sometimes the breasts of some men are inflamed due to excessive sleep, and then the breasts become inflamed and resemble those of women, and remain as purulent, leprous sores.
And for someone who has this, it is necessary to incise them with a lunate incision, according to this form, from line B to line G, and then excise all the thickness, and then fill the wound with incarnative medicine, and then approximate the two lips of the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the nipple declines towards the lower parts and is softened by its size, as it happens in women, then it should be split in its two superior sides, with two incisions similar to a lunar figure, each contained within the other, until the major line contains the minor, according to this form, from line B to line G. And then excise the skin that is between the two lips of the wound, and remove the fat, and administer what we have said about the suture and the powders, which is necessary in the healing until it is healed.
And if you do not complete the incision that should be made because of the patient's unease and bleeding, then you should fill the wound with cotton soaked in corrosive ointment and leave it until it corrodes the remaining fat, and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
On the perforation of abscesses that appear under the nipple.
These abscesses that appear under the nipple are a type of scrofula, hard, and containing a sac-like skin.
And some of these contain humidity; and those that contain humidity should be incised with a lunar incision according to this figure: from line G to line B. And when all the pus in the abscess has been evacuated, then you fill it with old cotton and leave it there until the next day.
Then the cotton should be removed and the area treated with a suitable ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess is of the type of nodes and scrofula, then you incise it as we have said in the section on scrofula.
But if the wound is extensive, so that decay sets in, then administer cautery as we have previously described.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the incision of abscesses that occur from archery or a vein.
When an artery is broken and the flesh that is over it is consolidated, then often an abscess occurs. And the signs by which it is known that inflammation and abscess are from an artery or vein: because when an abscess is from an artery, it is long and deep within the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel a sort of whistling sound within it.
And that which is from a vein is round and superficial on the body.
And incision of this abscess is dangerous, especially in that which is under the armpits, and in the groin, and in the neck, and in many places of the body; and it is very large, therefore its treatment with iron should be avoided.  And that which is in the extremities, or on the head, should also be avoided [or,  the incision should be avoided].
But concerning that which is caused by inflammation of the opening of the artery, make a long incision in the flesh; and then widen the incision with forceps and approach the artery, and free it from the tissues until it is exposed.
And then place a needle on it and make it penetrate to the other side, and tie off the artery with double thread in two places—the method of which I have shown you in the extraction of two arteries which are in the temples.
And then puncture the place which is between two ligaments with a spatula until all the blood which is there comes out, and the abscess is dissolved.
And then administer treatment in which powder is generated until the ligaments fall away; and then treat it with ointments suitable for this until it is healed.
And if it is an abscess caused by a severed vein, then it is necessary that you hold with your hand as much of the abscess as you can with the flesh, and then insert the needle below the place which you have held under your hand, and make it penetrate; and let there be double thread on it so that you extract it from the other side, and then tie the abscess with good thread, as I have told you in the tying of the uvula4 in two parts with two ends of the thread.
But if you fear that the thread will pull out, then insert the thread with that needle under all the abscess below the incision of the first needle, and tighten the threads in four places.
Then dry the abscess in the middle of it until, when what is in it has come out, then you incise the superfluity of the flesh, and leave what is tied in it.
Then place on it a powder which you have submerged in wine and oil, and then administer the treatment which is done with poultices and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 1 of the abscess which happens because of a twisted nerve.
Just as an abscess happens in an artery and in a vein, similarly an abscess happens in the nerves when it happens in their percussion or happens due to excessive work, and similarly.
And it is made worst in the places of the joints or of the heel, or in any place where the joints are weakened.
And the abscess is serious, and is similar in color to the other body parts, and is in the worst dispositions of them and when it is squeezed with force, the patient feels a kind of numbness.
And the abscess is not aggravated in the front of the body, and therefore it is below the ribs, and it is weakened in every part and does not go to the front or back parts.
Therefore, that which is in the joints should not be invaded with iron, for contraction will very quickly result.
But that which is in the head or on the forehead, treat with a spatula. But if it is a small abscess, then grasp it with forceps and cut it away from the root.
And if it is large, then you should lift it with a hook, and flay it, and then remove it; and close the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 51.
Concerning the incision of an abscess which happens in the area which happens in the abdomen.
It happens to some men many times in their bellies and in other parts of their bodies, those things which are called funguses by similarity to fungi, of which the root is subtle and the head is large; and sometimes the lips of these are inverted, and sometimes they are small, and sometimes they are very large.
And I have already seen one, and in his belly were two such things which were more similar to fungi, and there was no difference between them, white, with a thin root.
And sometimes the lips of these were inverted and split open, and humidity flowed from them constantly.
Therefore I incised them and found in one about eighteen ounces, and in the other about six ounces.
And the operation in the incision of these is that you consider then if the patient is humid, and the color of the swelling is white, humid, with a subtle root; then you incise it with a broad spatula, and have cauteries in the fire at your hand, because many times after the incision of it much blood is expelled; and if the blood overcomes you, cauterize it.
And if you see the patient fearful and timid, beware of incision with iron; but then take freshly made lead plaster, and bind the swelling with it, in the manner which we have described; and leave it for two days.
And then adjust the tightness of the lead thread; therefore, do not cease to tighten the lead greatly on the swelling, until it is cut off and falls off by itself without effort.
But if the swelling is of thick root, then in the incision of it there is some cause for fear and dread, and especially that which is in the belly.
But it is possible that you incise half of it, and then cauterize it so that it does not return.
And then care for it until the wound is healed.
And beware that you do not presume to incise a swelling which is of dark color, little feeling, and of slow growth, for that is likely cancerous.
And I come with the reminder of cancer concerning this.
Chapter 52.
Concerning the treatment of umbilical hernia.
Umbilical hernia is caused by many things; either by fissures of the diaphragm which is above the belly, and through it the intestine or bowel protrudes as it does in other ruptures; or by blood which flows from a vein or artery as previously mentioned, or by constricted wind within it.
But if it is due to a fissure of the diaphragm and protrusion of the intestine, then the color of the swelling is similar to the color of the body; and it is soft without pain, and appears in various locations. And if it is due to the protrusion of the bowel, then its location is as we have recounted, of greater and more varied intensity, and when you press it with your fingers it is hidden, and readily returns; and it is easily felt there, and is greatly increased by the entry of a bath and strenuous exertion.
But if due to humidity, then it is soft and is not hidden when you press it with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor does it grow.
And if it is due to blood, then the swelling appears with these signs, tending towards blackness.
But if it is due to raw flesh, then the swelling is heavy, large and hard, and is fixed in one place.
But if it is due to wind/gas, then the touch of it is soft.
And the operation on it is that you consider then if the swelling of the umbilicus is from arterial blood, or venous blood, or from wind/gas; then it is fitting that you are forbidden from the treatment of it, because in that there is fear and dread, just as I have taught you in the chapter in which I discussed swellings from artery and from vein.
And if the swelling of the umbilicus is from the intestine or from the membrane, then it is fitting that you command the patient to hold his breath, and be lying down extended; and then mark with a probe around the whole umbilicus, and then command him to lie between your hands upon his back, and then surround with a broad spatula around the umbilicus over the area which you have marked with the probe; and then extend half of the swelling towards the upper parts with a large inch; and then bind around with strong thread or with a bandage, and in a proper manner of binding.
And then open the middle of the extended swelling over the bandage and introduce your finger into that opening and search for the intestine; and if you have found it and held it by the bandage, then you carefully extract and push the intestine back into the interior parts of the belly.
And if you have found the membrane, then take that with forceps; and incise its superfluities.
But if it is contrary to your [expectation of finding only] artery or vein, then surround that area, and then return to your operation; and take two needles and put in those two strong threads and insert two needles in the circle which you have made around the swelling in the manner of a cross, which you make penetrate; and then tighten the swelling in four parts over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the extended flesh dries and falls off by itself; or incise that when it is thinned and has dried, and then cure that with whatever ointments are necessary until it is healed.
And if the prominence of the navel is of raw flesh there or from humidity, then it is necessary that you concavate the swelling as we have said and extract the blood or the humidity, which you find there, and then cure that with what is necessary and that consolidates the area.
Chapter 53.
Concerning the treatment of cancer.
We have already said, in the division, of the type of cancer and in what manner there is a way to its cure with medicines, and that it is to be avoided in it the cure with iron so that it is not ulcerated; and we have said of cancer generated in the mother and caution in its treatment.
And the ancients said that sometimes cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible, as is the cancer which is in the breasts or in the thigh and in similar parts of the body, to extract all of that, and especially when it is small and beginning.
But when it is old and large, it is not advisable to apply oneself to it, for I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen another, nor that it has been achieved.
And the operation on it, when it is located as we have said, is that you proceed and let the patient bleed and purge of black bile sometimes, and then bleed him, if a manifest repletion is found.
And then prepare the patient with a preparation in which he is positioned for the operation, and then apply to the wound ointments which are suitable for it; and then hollow out on all sides with the curved knife, carefully, until no part of its roots remains. And let the blood flow, and do not staunch it lightly, and press the areas, and wipe away the thick blood all with your hand or with an instrument with which it is possible for you among the instruments.
But if you find an excessive flow of thick blood from an incision of a vein or artery, then cauterize it until the flow of blood is stopped, and then treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 44.  (Assuming a standard chapter numbering system)
Concerning the treatment of dropsy.
We have already recounted, in the division, the types of dropsy from water, and the signs of each type, and their treatment with medicines.
And that which is cured with iron is only the species of ascites, and iron is not applied to the species of tympanites or to the species of anasarca, for that is mortal.
And when you have treated this type of tympanitic dropsy with medicines and your treatment is not effective, then consider, for if the patient has already developed debility, or has another illness in addition to this dropsy, such as a cough or abdominal pain and similar conditions, then be careful not to treat with iron, for there is danger in that.
But if you see the patient with exuberant strength, and with no other illness but dropsy alone, and neither a child nor an old person, then the manner of operating on him is that you raise the patient between your hands, and the assistant gently presses his abdomen with his hand, driving the water towards the area below the pubic bone, and then take a lancet in this way:
Let it be of two sharp edges, long, with a sharp point like a lancet; but however, let there be some shortness in it, little by little, so that in operating with it you do not penetrate the intestine, and injure it.
And then consider if the generation of the dropsy is from the intestines, for then it is necessary that you, with the incision, distance yourself from the navel by the amount of three fingers towards the lower parts, to the right of it, above the pubic bone.
But if the generation of the dropsy is due to illness of the liver, then your incision should be to the left of the navel by three fingers.
And if its generation is due to the spleen, then your incision should be on the right.
And it is not fitting that your incision be on the side on which the patient wishes to lie, lest the superfluities flow to that weak place.
And then perforate the entire abdomen with the instrument, and then insert it into that incision, and raise your hand with the spatula between the abdomen and the peritoneum, and you will scrape that; and if [it is] the quantity of a fingernail or thereabouts.
And then perforate the peritoneum until the spatula reaches the empty place, which is the location of the water. And then extract that and insert an instrument of which this is the form: and it is similar to a fine silver cannula; it is made of silver or bronze or tinned lead, which at its lowest part has a small hole, and on its sides there are three holes: two on one side and one on the other; and its incised extremity is shaped like a feather, thus:
And at its uppermost extremity is a ring, because when the instrument reaches the water, then that immediately settles on the instrument, therefore evacuating the water in a half-hour quantity, because if you evacuate more than is necessary at the time, the patient very quickly dies from the dissolution of their animal spirit, or a syncope occurs which is close to death.
But evacuate according to the quantity of its virtue and according to what the patient's disposition signifies to you, the virtue of their pulse, and their well-being.
And then extract the instrument, and retain the water, the reason being that it is immediately retained by the chain which closes the hole that is on the cup, of which I have recounted to you that you should pierce it in that manner.
And then repeat the procedure with the instrument on another day if you see the patient tolerating it, and extract a small quantity of water, and do this for several days cautiously, and guard against error so that little water remains.
But if you fear for the patient, and leave a large quantity of water, then treat him with burial in warm sand, and induce sweating in a bath, and expose him to the sun for a long time; and treat him with drying medicines until he is healed.
And cauterize the stomach, the spleen, and the liver after the extraction of the water, as I have recounted in what precedes.
Chapter 55.
Concerning the treatment of children born with imperforate urinary tracts.
Sometimes a child is born from its mother's womb and the opening of its urethra is not perforated (or is narrow or not in its proper place).
Therefore, it is necessary to treat it with perforation as soon as it is born, using a very fine instrument, according to this method:
And then place a thin lead probe into the opening and leave it there for three or four days.
And when it wants to urinate, it should be stimulated, and it will urinate; and then return the probe.
And if you do not place the lead [probe], it is possible that the urine which goes to that place will not allow it to close.
But that which has a narrow opening should be treated with a lead probe, as we have said, for many days until it is widened.
But those in whom the opening is not in its proper place, it is said, the cause is that in those who are born with the opening below the tip of the penis, so that they cannot urinate into the appropriate vessels until they lift the penis with their hand to a higher position; and it is not conducive to generation, because the semen cannot reach the womb in a straight line, and it is a very long-lasting illness.
And the manner of the operation is that the patient should be placed on his back, and then extend the tip of his penis with your left hand with forceful traction, and incise the tip of the penis at the location of the prepuce with a sharp scalpel, like a feather incision, or you should not cut any part, so that the middle of it comes to resemble the head of the penis and that the opening falls into the middle place as it should.
And be careful in your operation about the flow of blood, for this often happens; and stop the bleeding with something appropriate, and take care of the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 56.
Concerning balanitis, which occurs in the prepuce at the head of the penis, and of corruption, blackness, and adhesion of the prepuce to the head of the penis.
This balanitis often occurs on the penis and is a fleshy swelling.
And of that, one is malignant and the other is non-malignant.
For the non-malignant type, you should suspend it with a fine thread and excise it until you have removed it all; then place on it a pledget soaked in Egyptian ointment; and after that, treat it with diachylon ointment until it is healed.
And if the balanitis is malignant, with a dark color, then you should apply cautery near its incision and its root.
But if the balanitis is in the prepuce of the glans penis of an uncircumcised man, and one part of the balanitis is inside the prepuce and the other part is outside, then you should remove the balanitis that is inside first, until it is consolidated; and then you treat the outside part, because if you treat both together, the prepuce might be damaged.
And if you find in the genitals and prepuce blackness and corruption, then you should remove everything that is already blackened and that is beginning to corrupt or is already corrupt; and then apply to that area honey, which has been mixed with crushed and sifted pomegranate rind flour and finely sifted crumbs.
And then treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
But if there is a flow of blood, then apply lunar cautery according to this method:
because cautery is helpful for two conditions at once, that is to say, for bleeding and for the wound when it is corrupted.
And if the head of the penis is corroded and completely destroyed, then you should insert a lead cannula into the meatus of the prepuce so that the patient urinates through it.
In the adhesion of the prepuce to the head of the penis (and this adhesion does not happen except in one whose prepuce is healthy, and circumcision has not been performed on him; and when adhesion occurs due to a wound or abscess) it is necessary that you scrape with a blunt spatula until the adhesion is dissolved and the head of the penis is freed from all parts.
And if it is difficult to separate these carefully, then you should scrape with a spatula some part of the head of the penis and of the prepuce, and this is because the prepuce, when it is thin, is very easily perforated by its slight thinness.
Then separate the prepuce from the head of the penis with a very thin linen cloth, which has already been moistened in cold water so that they do not adhere again.
And then let it be treated with astringent wine until it is healed.
Chapter 57.
Of the cleansing of children and the treatment of what happens to those circumcised.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity like other wounds; however, since it is of our operation and our will, and it is especially performed on infants, it is necessary that we assign to it the best operation and the easiest path leading to health.
Therefore I say that the ancients did not discuss circumcision in any of their books, because they did not use it in their laws, and it is only because we have acquired it through experience, which is why I have found most of the artisans and barbers use circumcision and cleansing with a lancet and with tow; and they use ligature with thread and incision with the fingernail.
And I have tried all these methods and I find no better cleansing with scissors, with ligature with thread than cleansing with the fingernail, because many times the skin peels off from above, because the foreskin has two layers, and the lower layer remains, and it is easily forced to another incision and certain pain. And with cleansing which is done by scraping, and is digital, which shoemakers use, the incision of the tip of the penis is not safe, because it easily enters its hole.
But in the operation with a fingernail, it easily becomes rusty and corrupts your operation. And the foreskin of a child is short by nature, and many times it is born in such a way that it has no need of cleansing, and I have seen this.
But of the cleansing that is done with scissors and ligatures with thread, the goodness, experience reveals to me, because scissors are for proportional incision, because the sharpness that is above is the same as the sharpness that is below; and therefore as you place your hand, two sharpnesses are proportional, and you incise according to a comparison at one time; and compression is made by the thread in a manner that contains the foreskin of the penis in every part, with which no error at all is made.
And the method of the first operation is that you make the child lie down, especially if he is among those who understand a little, that you do nothing but tie the thread on his penis only, and that you leave it until the next day.
And then make him happy in every way possible that he can understand. And then make him lie still between your hands, prepared and not at all moving; and hide the scissors in your hand or under your foot, so that the child does not see them or any other of the instruments.
And then place your hand on his penis and blow on the foreskin, and pull it until the glans penis is exposed, and then clean it of any dirt that is attached to it.
The shape of the scissors for circumcision, this is the shape:
Let one end be blunt and the other sharp, so that the nail does not come out, and let the length of the two points be the same as the length of two fingers joined together.
But the error in circumcision is that you too quickly remove the entire foreskin, or part of it, below the incision.
Therefore it is necessary that you extend that [foreskin] with your fingernail before the suppuration of the area and that you incise it evenly.
And if you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then pull it with forceps and incise it.
But if three days have passed, and what is swollen remains under the infected penis, then you leave it until the hot abscess subsides, and carefully scrape it, and incise it as you see fit.
And be careful of the head of the penis, for this will harm him.
And then treat it with that which consolidates the wound, from the powders which we have recommended in the treatise on powders.
And if it is incised excessively from the scrotum and affects the parts above, this will not harm him.
And moreover, treat it with what we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 58.
On the treatment of urine retained in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder is caused by obstruction from a stone, or by congealed blood, or by pus, or by a tumor, and similar to those [causes] of which we have spoken of the methods of treatment in the division [of this text].
And if it is not dissolved by what we have said, and the condition is severe in the patient, then it is necessary to administer extraction with an instrument which is called a syringe, of which this is the form: Let it be of silver and be subtly hollowed like a bird's quill, with the subtlety of a long ray, similar to a palm and a half in length, to which a fine tube is attached to its head.
And the manner of drawing out the urine by this means is that you take a doubled linen cloth and tie wool or cotton to its end with a good knot; and insert the ends of the cloth into the part below the syringe, and cut with scissors if any excess wool is present, but insert it into the cannula with force. Then lubricate the syringe with butter and egg white.
And make the patient sit on a stool, and rub their bladder and penis with moist oils, olive oil and rose water.
Then gently insert the syringe until it reaches the root of the penis; and then bend the penis upwards towards the navel; and then push the syringe inside that; and then push it until it reaches the bladder, and let the patient know that it has reached the empty space, and this is not done according to this order, but the meatus where it goes is tortuous.
Then slowly extend the cloth with the wool, because the urine will soak the wool.
Then extract that and use it for urine treatment, and use a syringe; do not cease doing this and using the syringe until the bladder is emptied and the patient finds relief.
Chapter 59.
Of the quality of bladder irrigation with a catheter and with similar instruments which are suitable for this.
When an ulceration or abscess occurs in the bladder, or blood clots in it, or putrefaction is constricted there, and you wish to instill waters and medicines into it, then it should be done with an instrument which is called a catheter, and this is its form:
except that its extremity is blunt, that is, closed. And in it are three holes: two on one side, thus arranged.
At the beginning is a concave space, in which is what is pushed in, and let it be according to the quantity that fills it, without addition, except that when some matter is drawn from the humors with it, it is drawn out, and when it is expelled, it is drawn out with it, and when it is expelled long, as the catheter does with which naphtha is inserted in sea battles.
Therefore, when you wish to draw out humors from the bladder, insert the tip of the catheter into the humor, and draw with it that which is pushed up to the upper parts, because the humor is drawn into the concavity of the catheter.
And then insert the tip of the catheter into the urethra as we have recounted in the syringe; and then push the humor with what you push, because that humor arrives at the bladder immediately, and the patient feels it.
The shape of the fine catheter with which the bladder is catheterized, and this is its shape:
And let it be made of silver or brass, of which the upper end should be similar to a small feather, and below it a straight tube.
And then take a goat's bladder, and place in it the humor that you wish to catheterize into the bladder, and then tie it between two with a strong double ligature, and heat that humor over the fire.
And then insert the tip of the catheter into the penis (or urethra).
And then squeeze your hand on the bladder with the liquid with gentle pressure until the patient says that the liquid has already reached the bladder.
But if you do not have a bladder at hand, then take a piece of parchment and make a circle from it; and perforate it all around its circumference; and then insert strong thread into those holes, so that it is tightly bound; and then prepare the circle as it is prepared for the *alsofatur* (presumably a type of instrument or vessel), after you have placed in it what you wish of the humors, oils, and waters.
And then you tie that to the instrument and do with that as you have done with the bladder, and express it with your hand until the humidity reaches the bladder.
Chapter 60.
On the extraction of a stone.
We have already discussed in the division the types of stones and their treatment with medicines, and the difference between stones generated in the kidneys and stones generated in the bladder, and their signs.
And we have discussed the stone in which one operates by hand and incision.
And the stone generated in the bladder properly is that which is lodged in the bladder.
And I am narrating this with clear exposition and brevity.
Therefore I say that stones generated in the bladder mostly affect children; and among its signs is that the urine that is expelled from the bladder is similar to water in its thinness, and sand appears in it; and the patient feels a burning sensation in his urethra, and often has pain, and is frequently urged to urinate; and with this, he expels a lot of this sand.
And the healing of children from this is easy until they reach fourteen years of age; and the cure is difficult in the elderly. But in young people it is moderate because it is between those extremes.
And of that in which the stone is larger, the cure is easier; and if it is small, it is the opposite.
Therefore, to arrive at the cure of this, it is necessary at the beginning that we purify the patient with a clyster which removes all the impurities that are in his bowels; for this impurity sometimes prevents the discovery of the stone through its examination.
And then let the patient be taken with his feet up and be massaged and manipulated downwards so that the stone descends to the bottom of the bladder, or that it may sometimes leap from its place.
And then make that [patient's body] lean between your hands, prepared in such a way that his hands are below his thighs, so that the entire bladder is made to incline downwards.
And then search for it and remove all of it from there.
Therefore, if you feel the stone in the space [bladder], then you must immediately take care of it with an incision.
And if it does not entirely descend with your touch, then it is necessary that you anoint your index finger of your left hand with oil, if the patient is a child, or the middle finger if he is a fully grown youth, and insert it into his anus. And search for the stone until it is below your finger. And bring it back little by little into the neck of the bladder.
And then press on it with your finger, and expel it outwards towards the place where you will make the incision.
And instruct the assistant to press on the bladder with her hand; and instruct another assistant to extend the genitals upwards with her right hand, and with her other hand to hold back the perineum which is below the genitals, at the location where the incision is to be made.
And then introduce a well-sharpened spatula, of which this is the form: and cut what is between the anus and the genitals, not in the middle, but towards the left thigh; and let the incision be above that stone, and your finger in the anus, and let a transverse incision be made, so that the external incision is wide and the internal narrow, according to the size that the extraction of the stone allows, and no larger, for you will quickly compress with your finger, which is in the anus, the stone against the incision, and it will be expelled without difficulty.
And know that some stones are angular and rough, which makes their removal difficult because of this.
And those that are like an acorn, and are round, are easily removed.
Therefore, if it is one that is angular and rough, adjust it in the fissure.
And if it is not expelled in this way, then you must press on it, or squeeze it with a suitably shaped instrument, the end of which is like a file with which you press on the stone, and it does not escape; or introduce a slender instrument with a curved end underneath it.
But if you cannot reach it, then widen the opening slightly, and if you see a flow of blood, staunch it with crushed vitriol.
But if there is more than one stone, then initially you should place your hand at the opening of the bladder, and then incise it; and then push a little more; and similarly do if there are several more than two.
And if the stone is very large, then it is unwise to incise it with a large incision, for two things may happen to the patient: either death, or a continuous dripping of urine, because the area is not completely healed.
But administer its expulsion until it is removed, or work on breaking it with forceps until you extract it in pieces.
Then, when you have completed your operation, fill the wound with aloe shavings and dragon's blood, and bind it. And place over it a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or in rose oil and cold water, so that a hot abscess may be calmed.
Then lay the patient on their back, and do not unbind the bandage until the third day.
And when you have unbound it, bathe the area with water and plenty of oil, and then treat it with diafinicon ointment and diabazilicon ointment until it is healed.
But if a hot, burnt, and corrosive abscess occurs in the wound, and it resembles blood frozen in the bladder, and urine is thereby prevented (and this is known by the passage of blood with urine), then insert your finger into the wound and extract that blood, for if it remains it provokes corruption of the bladder and putrefaction thereof.
Then wash the wound with sour wine, water, and salt, and apply, according to the type, whatever is suitable for its healing until it is cured.
And it is necessary during the entire treatment that you bind both thighs and apply the medicines which are placed on the area.
And if the stone is small and is in the meatus of the penis, and is lodged there, and the flow of urine is obstructed, then treat it with what I have recommended before you resort to incision, for many times through this treatment incision is avoided; and I have proven this, and that is that you take a sharp iron perforator, of which this is the form:
of triangular, sharp, outward-extending end, fixed in a shaft.
Then take bile, and bind the penis with it below the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then insert the iron into the penis, perforating easily until the perforator reaches the stone. And rotate the perforator with your hand against that stone little by little, and you will widen the perforation until you make it penetrate from the other side; for the urine will immediately be released.
Then press your hand on what remains of the stone on the outside of the penis, because that is fragmented, and it is expelled with the urine, and the patient is healed.
But if this cure is not suitable for you due to any reason that prohibits it, then bind one bandage below the stone, and another bandage above the stone. Then make an incision on the stone in that penis between the two bandages. And then extract it. Then untie the bandages and clean the congealed blood which is formed in the wound.
And it is not necessary to bandage below the stone except so that it does not return to the bladder, and the other bandage above, because when the bandage is untied near the extraction of the stone, the stone returns to its place and closes the wound.
And for that reason it is necessary for you, when you bandage above, that you raise the stone to the upper parts, or that it return through your efforts, and the wound closes as we have said.
Chapter 61.
On the extraction of stones from women.
Stones are rarely generated in women.
But if a stone appears in any of them, then its cure is difficult, and is prohibited in many ways.
One reason is that a woman is very easily a virgin (meaning, easily made a virgin again, i.e., her hymen could easily re-form or the reference is to the chastity of women generally).
And the second is that you will not find a woman, whether chaste or married, who willingly reveals herself to a physician.
The third is that you will not find a woman who knows this art well, especially the operation with the hand.
And the fourth is that the location of the incision for a stone in women is far from the location of the stone itself, requiring a deep incision, and there is danger in that.
But if necessity compels you to this, then you must take a female physician who is skilled, and few such are found.
But if you are deprived of that, then seek a cautious and subtle male physician, or present a matron well-versed in women's matters, or a woman who has some knowledge in this art.
Therefore, have that person present, and command them to do everything you order, beginning with the examination for the stone; the reason for this is that if you need to check if the woman is a virgin, then it is necessary to insert one's finger into her rectum and seek out the stone.
Then, if you find it, grasp it under your finger, and then order that an incision be made upon it.
But if she is not a virgin, then order the matron to insert her finger into the vagina of the patient and search for the stone, while placing her left hand on the bladder and compressing it with good pressure.
Then, if she finds it, it is necessary to gently draw it from the opening of the bladder to the lower parts using the force of her hand until it reaches the root of the thing.
And then incise it near the place where half of the vagina meets the root of the thing, wherever it is lodged, and center the stone in that part, and your finger should not be removed from the stone while grasping it from below.
And the incision should be small at first; and then insert the probe into that incision, and if you feel the stone, then adjust the incision according to the size that you know the stone to be, so that it may be extracted.
And know that there are many kinds of stones: for some are small and large, and smooth and rough, and long and round, and having branches (or protrusions).
Therefore, you should know how many types there are of them, so that you can signify what you desire.
And if you see a flow of blood, apply crushed vitriol to the place and hold it there for an hour until the bleeding stops, and then return to your operation until the stone is removed.
And ensure that you prepare with you the instruments which are mentioned in the extraction of stones in men, so that you are aided by them in your operation.
And if you see a flow of blood, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from the artery which is incised, then you should put powder on the place and bind it tightly with powders and a descending bandage, and do not repeat this; and leave the stone and do not extract it, for the patient will quickly die. And then treat the wound.
Then, when the sharpness of the bleeding has subsided after a few days, and the place has healed, then you return to your operation until the stone is removed.
Chapter 62.
Concerning the incision of a strangulated hernia.
A watery hernia is nothing but an accumulation of humidity in the white tunic which is below the skin of the genitals, containing the testicle, and is called the tunic.
And sometimes it is in the proper tunic which nature prepared for it in the part of the testicle, so that it seems that there is another testicle; and it is the skin of the genital between the white tunic which we have mentioned, and this is nothing but a swelling.
And the generation of this hernia is due to weakness which affects the genitals, so that this matter is poured into them, and sometimes it happens due to a blow inflicted on the genitals.
And this humidity has many colors, for its color is either tending towards yellow or bloody, or reddish, or black and foul-smelling, or watery white, and this is most common.
And the signs by which it is known where this accumulation of water is are these: for if it is in the white tunic which we have mentioned, then the abscess is round, inclining slightly to length like the shape of an egg, and it surrounds the testicle, for the humidity contains it on all sides.
And if the humidity is in the proper tunic, then the abscess is round in the part of the testicle and therefore one believes it to be another testicle.
And if the humidity is between the sheath of the genitals and the white tunic, then it falls below the feeling/sensation.
And if you want to know the color of the humidity, then you probe the color of the humidity in the abscess with a probe, whose shape precedes; and according to that which has come out on the probe, judge what is inside it.
Therefore, when we come to its cure with iron, then it is necessary that we order the patient to be bled if that is possible for you, and fill his body.
And then let him lie supine on his back on something slightly raised, and place many cloths underneath him.
And then sit on his left side, and command the assistant to be on his right, who should extend his finger to one of the two sides to the sheath of the genitals and the part of the navel of the belly.
And then take a broad spatula and split the sheath of the genitals in the middle lengthwise almost to the pubic bone.
And let the incision be made straight, equally distant from the line that divides the skin of the genitals, until it reaches the included white fatty tissue.
And scrape that [fatty tissue], and observe, and be careful not to cut that; and your scraping with which you scrape it, let it be done gently, and  carefully examine with the scraping as much as is possible for you.
And then pierce the water-filled cyst with a wide perforation, and extract all the water.
And then separate the two lips of the incision with hooks, and extend the cyst upwards; and do not touch the sheath of the genitals, and incise the cyst in the way that is possible for you. And incise it in its entirety or in pieces, and especially its thin layer, for if you do not carefully examine its incision, there is no guarantee that the water will not return.
But if the egg [testicle] protrudes from its sheath during your operation, then when you have completed the incision of the cyst, return it inside, and then join the two lips of the sheath of the genitals with suture, and then treat it like other wounds, until it is healed.
And if you find the testicle corrupted by another disease, then it is necessary that you tie the vessels which are on the spermatic cord, and remove the testicle, and then treat it as we have said.
But if the fluid is collected in two separate parts, then know that there are two hernias; therefore, incise that part in the same way as you did the other; and if it seems to you that it is a single operation, then you perform it.
And then you wipe away the blood, and insert into the incisions wool soaked in oil or rose oil; and place on top more wool which you have soaked in wine and oil, and spread this over the genitals and the pubic area; and place over it folded cloths and powders, and bind them over with a bandage which is according to this form:
And then two cloths should be taken and filled with carded wool in this manner; and the ends should be made with which it is pressed with cotton or damp wool, and they should be attached with a structure in the waistband of the breeches, and one of the ends should reach from the buttocks to the groin, and the second should reach to the part of the scrotum, and two ends on the two thighs and two other ends on the two knees, and the whole structure should be fastened in the waistband of the breeches.
And sometimes this hernia is treated artificially with cautery instead of incision with an iron; and it is that you take a thin, knife-like cautery and that you incise with it the skin of the genitals, and it is ignited as we have described until the white pus containing the water is exposed, and then you take a cautery of this form: and it resembles the Greek letter.
And then perforate the abscess with it, and ignite it until all the moisture is expelled.
And then extend the abscess with tweezers, and scrape with tweezers, and scrape with a thin, ignited cautery; and incise it as much as you can until you eradicate all of it; and be careful that the fire does not touch the egg (presumably referring to the testicle).
And know that this operation is safer and less prone to bleeding, and better than an operation with incision.
But if an abscess causes you difficulty during incision or cauterization, or bleeding, then you must treat whatever difficulty it causes you, with the treatment that is appropriate to it, as described.
But if the patient is fearful and does not expect a second operation because of its horror, then administer perforation as I have recommended; and this is when you are certain, because of what we have said about the signs, that the hernia is watery.
Then you should have the patient sit on some raised seat, then perforate the abscess at its lowest part with a broad spatula, and position the perforation according to the length of the body (presumably the length of the abscess).
And let the opening be wide enough so that all the water drains out.
Then place a cotton dressing over the area and bind it, and leave it so that the remaining water drains out. And then care for it until the wound is healed.
But if there is an obstruction to the drainage of the water, then you should perforate it. This is done only because the white panniculus (fatty layer) at the opening of the wound obstructs and prevents the exit of the water.
