Then you should put into the wound a probe or instrument with which the water is extracted from dropsies, the shape of which is described earlier; or apply a small incision to the opening of the wound.
And know that the water will return, and it will be added again in about six months or thereabouts; and when it is accumulated, then it should be perforated in the same manner, for thus the patient will spend his days.
Chapter 63.
Concerning the incision of a fleshy hernia and its treatment.
Know that in the incision of this hernia there is a danger leading to death in most cases; therefore, in my opinion, its removal and being saved by this method is preferable.
And I am recalling the operation in this case and the types of abscesses.
Therefore, I say that abscesses arise in the genitals, which are composed thus, and this is caused by many things: either by an acute, acrid superfluity, which is poured into the genitals, or by percussion.
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it, and it is a serious abscess.
And very quickly is a stone-like abscess formed, whose color is dark, and there is no feeling in it.
And sometimes it is caused by a knotting of the arteries, and by the inflammation of these, as is perceived by the recollection of it, or by inflammation of the veins.
But that which is from inflammation of the arteries is known by the dispersion of the abscess when we compress it with our fingers; and it is not necessary that one touch it.
But that which is caused by inflammation of a vein is not dispersed by any means from compression with the fingers.
And it is possible that an incision be made on this type of [abscess/swelling] and on the other flesh; and it is that you incise the skin of the genitals, and then stretch the testicle upwards, and extract it from the white sheath, and free the suspensory ligament, and tie it, and incise the suspensory ligament after you have freed it from all parts of the testicle.
Therefore, if the testicle is already consolidated with those inflamed tissues, then it is necessary that you extract the testicle and incise it.
And if there is adhesion between some part of the sheath, or between that which is between the vessels, then it is necessary that you free all that adhesion and incise it with a circular incision.
But if there is a growth of flesh at the place of adhesion which is behind, then it is necessary that you incise all that which is there, and extract the testicle as we have said.
Therefore, when your operation is completed, then fill the wound with wool infused in rose oil and wine; and then treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 64.
On the treatment of a hernia which is of the scrotum (or groin).
A scrotal hernia is an abscess enveloped by a certain covering similar to a boil, with softening of the testicles, and it makes exercise and walking difficult for the patient.
And in the treatment of this illness, fear is involved,  because of what precedes its recollection (or because of what is remembered preceding it;  the meaning is somewhat ambiguous and depends on the exact intended meaning of "rememoracio").
However, it is necessary that we describe the operation on it according to how the first (doctors/surgeons) did it.
And I say that it is necessary that you make the patient sit on a raised chair; and then pull up the suspensory of the testicles towards the lower parts; and then hold the scrotum with your fingers along with the vessels that are felt on the penis; and let another assistant hold those, and extend them with strong extension.
And then incise with a wide, sharp scalpel with a transverse incision to the right of the vessels until the vessels are uncovered.
And then scrape the entire area, just as we have told you in the extraction of arrowheads which are in the temples.
And then place in those a double-threaded needle, and tie it at the first of the places where you have seen blood, and tie it again at the back of them. And then incise those in the middle with a straight incision according to the length of the body, and extract what is accumulated of fetid and corrupt humors.
And then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds in which you want healing to be generated so that the vessels which nourish one of the two genitals fall, for it is not harmful because of that.
But if blood flows from all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be removed or that the testicles be cut off with the vessels, so that they are deprived of all nourishment by the incision of the vessels, and that they be removed, or that they are cut off so that there is no nourishment to them.
Chapter 65.
Concerning the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a rupture that happens in the peritoneum extended over the abdomen on the side of the groin, so that the intestines protrude from that rupture to some of the intestines.
And this rupture is either of the peritoneum or an extension of it, and these two types occur for many reasons:  a blow, a jump, a shout, lifting something heavy, or similar.
And the sign of this, when it is due to an extension of the peritoneum, is that it occurs gradually over a long time, and does not occur suddenly, and is a swelling gradually increasing towards the groin because the peritoneum compresses the intestine.
And the sign of it, when it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum, is that at the beginning it causes a sudden, vehement pain; and there is an apparent swelling below the groin, close to the apparent area, and this is because of the protrusion of the intestine, and its body is outside the peritoneum.
And sometimes the omentum is expelled along with the intestine, and this hernia is called an intestinal zirbal hernia.  (Note: "zirbal" appears to be a term specific to this text and its meaning requires further context within the treatise.)
And sometimes it is caused by flatulence, and sometimes a tear occurs in the intestine, and it is retained there, and this causes great harm to the patient, and sometimes it causes severe pain, especially when pressed.
And in the treatment of this type of illness, iron is feared, therefore, cauterization should be avoided in this case.
And the method of operating on this is that you instruct the patient to return the intestine with their hand to the inside of its cavity if it obeys its return, and then lean over their abdomen between your hands, and raise their legs.
Then hold the scrotum which is the covering of the testicles upwards, and incise the scrotum completely lengthwise; and then insert two hooks into the lips of the incision as needed to keep the incision open with them.
And if, according to the amount that is possible for you, the egg (testicle) can be expelled from that incision.
And then scrape away that layer of skin which is under the scrotum, until the hard white tunic is exposed on each side (of the testicle).
Then you insert your finger into what is the testicle, into what is between the white tunic which is under the covering of the testicle and the second tunic, and break up with it the adhesions that are in the back part of the testicle.
And then gently pull the scrotum outwards with your hand, and with that spread the white tunic upwards with your left hand, and elevate the testicle with the tunic to the edge of the incision; and instruct the assistant to hold the testicle upwards while you dissolve the adhesions that are behind it with a complete solution (of the problem/adhesions).
And search with your fingers that there is no intestine enveloped in the hard white tunic. But if you find any such thing (intestine), then you carefully push it back down into the abdomen.
And then make an incision in the part under the covering of the testicles, through which incision blood and pus will flow, just as we have described above.
And then apply wool soaked in oil and place it on the wound; and apply the ligature which we have described (and sometimes the white tunic is cauterized, which we mentioned near its incision to stem the flow of blood, because this often happens) and leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling off is delayed, then let it be bathed with warm water, and keep it there until it falls off as we have said.
And then treat the wound with the other treatments for wounds until it is healed.
Chapter 66.
Concerning a Windy Hernia (or,  Venous Hernia/Hernia with Swelling).
In this type of hernia, I have not seen anyone who dared to treat it with iron (cautery).
And the first [doctors] said that it is done as we have recounted in the hernia that is with wine; which is that you tie the vessels near the incision on that [hernia] with ease; and then dry the middle of the place around the abscess with that which opens it, until the vessels fall away/collapse.
And cure the wound according to what we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 67.
Of the rupture that is made in the inguinal region (groin).
Sometimes a rupture occurs in the groin as we have said, and it swells the area.
And do not allow the genitals any cause of the intestines in the genitals; and if they do, it is little; and it returns at all hours. (This likely refers to a hernia and the need to keep the intestines from entering the scrotum.  The phrasing is awkward and perhaps incomplete in the original.)
But if the time with this is prolonged, a fissure is formed in the inguinal canal until the intestine descends into the inguinal canal; and this happens because of the extension of the inguinal canal which is in the groin as we have said, the cause of which is that the canal is stretched, and then it is softened and the place is inflamed and swollen.
And its cure is with cautery, as its description has preceded.
And sometimes it is cured with iron according to this manner:  the patient lies on his back between your hands, and then dry the location of the rupture of the swollen abscesses with incision across the width according to the quantity of three fingers.
And then tighten the white inguinal canal that is under the groin until it is uncovered.
And then take the ray (presumably a surgical instrument) and place it on the prominent part of the inguinal canal, and press it towards the abdomen.
And then sew the two prominent locations on the end of the inguinal canal's ray (instrument), and join one to the other with a suture.
And then remove the end of the ray (instrument), and do not incise the inguinal canal completely, nor extend the wound, nor anything else like that as I will let you know in the treatment of intestinal hernia.
And then treat that with the treatment for wounds, and as you incise the fibers, cleanse it and repair the wound until it is consolidated; because the inguinal canal is constricted and the prominence of the rupture is not adjusted.
And cautery is better in this location, because it is closer to health (i.e., more likely to promote healing).
Chapter 68.
Concerning the softening of the scrotum.
Many times the scrotum in some men is softened because the flesh that is between them is softened, and this hinders its appearance (i.e., makes it difficult to see properly).
Then it is necessary for the one who is charged with his cure to make the patient lie on his back, and to sew all the softened scrotum onto the natural scrotum; and then to adjust two lips with the suture (i.e., to close the opening with sutures).
And if you wish, sew together at the beginning the excess of the softened scrotum with three or four stitches and with a firm suture.
And then incise between the sutures; and then treat it as you would other wounds until it is healed and the stitches can be removed.
Chapter 69.
Concerning castration.
Know that castration is forbidden by our law, but because of this I am obliged to remember it in this book; and its remembrance is necessary only in two ways: one is so that the physician may know it when questioned about it, so that he knows how to treat what has happened; and the other way is that we often have need of the castration of other animals for our benefit from them, such as mules, goats, pigs, and similar animals.
And therefore I say that castration is done in two ways: either by crushing, or by incision.
But in that which is done by crushing, the method of operation is that we should immerse the animal in warm water until its genitals are softened and relaxed and hang down. Then grasp them with your hand until they are resolved and do not appear beneath the touch.
But in castration done by incision, it is necessary that you hold the animal and press the scrotum of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament, and incise upon each testicle with a single cut until both testicles are severed.
Then incise them after you have flayed each one. And do not place any substance on those of the cyphus (presumably referring to a surgical instrument or anatomical part), except for the fine cyphus which is over the vessels.
And this manner of castration is better which is done by crushing, because crushing quickly leaves no part of the testicles remaining, so that the animal desires no more copulation.
Then care for the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
Concerning the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men is of two kinds: one is where there appears in the space of the scrotum, that which is between the two testicles, a form like a woman's vulva in which there is hair.
And sometimes urine flows from that which is in the scrotum.
But in women there is one type: and it is that the vulva of the woman above the mons pubis is like two testicles of a man, somewhat smaller, externally resembling the foreskin of a man and two testicles.
And the cure is of three types: two for men and one for women. And it is necessary that you incise those tightened flesh until the swelling escapes, and then that you cure them with the treatment for other wounds.
And in the second type of men, from which urine flows that is in the scrotum, there is no cure or operation for him.
Chapter 21.
Concerning the swelling and prominent flesh caused by women's ailments.
A slight swelling is often caused by natural reasons until it is squeezed and made light by observation; and sometimes it is magnified in some women to such an extent that it is spread as in men and extends to the genitals.
Then it is necessary that you hold back the excess of swelling with your hand or with a bandage and incisions.
And do not overdo the incisions, especially in the root area, so that bleeding does not occur.
And then treat it with wound care until it is healed.
But a fleshy growth is flesh that grows in the mother's womb until it fills it, and it soon comes out through the external parts resembling a tail (and for this reason some first called this "tailed disease"); therefore, we must incise it as we incise a swelling, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 72.
Concerning the treatment of gout.
Gout is when the vagina of the woman is not perforated, or the opening is small. And this is either natural or accidental.
Accidental [gout] is due to a preceding illness, and that is caused either by poorly formed flesh or by a thin or thick membrane.
And it is formed either in the front of the mother, or on the sides of it, or in the upper part of it, or in the lower part of it, and prohibits coitus and conception and childbirth, and strongly inhibits menstruation.
And the diagnosis of this is made by examining the womb; if the obstruction is apparent, [it is diagnosed]; but if it is not apparent, then you must search for it with your fingers or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is due to a thin membrane, and it is close to the two labia, then you should incise and break it.
And you should place powders on the two labia, and then place your index fingers of both hands together [on the labia].
And the woman should be on her back, her thighs spread, and then spread the two labia forcefully until that thin membrane is broken and the obstruction is opened.
Then take wool and soak it in oil and place it on the area, and keep it moist all day so that the incised area does not close up.
But if the membrane is thick and dense, then you should incise it with a broad spatula or with a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is due to flesh, then suspend it with an ointment, and incise it.
And use with them medicines that stop the flow of blood without biting, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and incense, mixed with egg white.
Then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not close too quickly.
And keep it there for a few days and administer some dry lint.
And then it should be treated with another healing method until it is healed.
And sometimes it happens in the mother's birth of other flesh, and then it is necessary to incise according to this method. (This likely refers to a complication during childbirth). But if this is due to a cancerous abscess, the cancerous abscess which is in the mother should not be treated with iron in any way.
Chapter 73.
Of the treatment of hemorrhoids and boils and red swellings that occur in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are an enlargement of the orifices of many veins, from which much blood continuously flows; and when hemorrhoids are old, boils are formed.
And I have already mentioned in the division the types of these and their signs, and I will say thus which receive treatment and which do not receive treatment.
Therefore I say that hemorrhoids and boils, when they are in the womb and do not appear externally, then in those cases no treatment should be done with iron (i.e., cautery or surgery).
But those which are at the orifice of the womb, upon which one feels [them], are those which are cured.
And it is necessary that you place the woman in a cold place and then extend the boil with forceps, or with a rough cloth, and incise them at the roots.
And then sprinkle on them, to stop the flow of blood, one of the incisive powders without biting [properties], such as acacia and similar things.
And then bathe her feet for an hour, and then place them in cold water.
And if the flow of blood persists, then make her sit in a decoction of summac and pomegranate bark and oak bark and similar things.
And then take barley flour and mix it with honey and sour wine and make a plaster with this and apply it to her back.
And if the blood is stopped, it is good; and if not, apply two cupping glasses, to the breasts without scarification.
And then take wool and soak it in coriander juice or in comfrey juice or in  ivy juice with diluted wine and apply it to the area; and then treat the area with ointment until it is healed.
And treat that until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
Of the perforation of the rectum by accident in the mother.
Many kinds of abscesses happen in the mother, such as cancer, and stony abscess, and ulcerative, and inflamed, and erysipelas, and hemorrhoidal fistulas, and fissures, and boils, and other abscesses. And I have already discussed these illnesses, and their types, and their symptoms, and their treatments in the division [or section].
And it is necessary that I speak in this treatise of a hot abscess which occurs in the mother, when it is one of the abscesses which gathers pus; and if you want to know in what manner the operation is performed on it with iron.
Then you must consider if the pain of the abscess at its beginning is acute, with throbbing, and phlegm, and fever, and sometimes redness, and if the feeling is intense; then do not attempt perforation, and treat it with that which aids maturation until the acuteness of the symptoms ceases.
And then you must make the woman sit on a stool that has two legs; and lean on her side, and adjust her feet to the lowest part of her belly, and her thighs should be spread, and place her arms under her knees, and bind each with a suitable bandage.
And then the midwife follows from the right side and administers the instrument with which the orifice of the womb is opened, the form of which will be described in those things which are done next.
And it is necessary, when you wish to administer this instrument, that you place with the ray the neck of the woman's womb, and that which of the instrument is inserted into the womb should not be more than the neck of the womb and it hurts the patient. But if the instrument is larger than the neck of the womb, then it is necessary that you place powders on the texture of the neck of the womb so that the entry of any instrument into the neck of the womb is prevented.
And it is necessary that you place the vertige (likely referring to a rotating mechanism or a handle) that runs in the instrument on the upper side, and that the person administering it holds the instrument and turns the vertige until the orifice of the womb is opened.
And when the incision appears, and it is touched with the hand and is soft and moist, then it is necessary that you dry that area with a linen cloth around the head.
And when the blood has been evacuated, then it is necessary that you place in the wound a wick submerged in rose oil or green oil in which there is some astringency; and place the caul (possibly referring to a membrane or protective covering) on the incision in the neck of the womb.
And place outside the womb and on the vulva clean wool submerged in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And afterwards it should be treated for about two days with an ointment which is suitable for this, until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and the wound are washed in such a way that it is rinsed with water and honey, and afterwards with water in which the root of lily or of birthwort has been boiled, and afterwards it returns to the ointment.
And if the wound is deep inside the womb, then you must be forbidden from treating it with iron, taking care with that in accordance with what we have said in the division (presumably a previous section of the treatise).
Chapter 75.
To teach midwives in what manner live embryos should be treated when they are expelled in an unnatural way (or form).
It is necessary that the midwife know at the beginning the characteristics of a natural childbirth, and it is that when you see the woman strain in her lower parts and desire to smell the air, and on top of that is lightened by that which is upon her of the birth, and hastens the exit of the embryo, then you should know that this birth is according to the natural form, and it is the exit of the child head-first, and the afterbirth with it, either suspended or hanging from the umbilicus.
Therefore, when you see those signs, then it is necessary that her belly be compressed so that the embryo descends easily, for when it descends head-first, the afterbirth descends with it, and it is cleansed of those superfluities with good cleansing.
But that which is other than this birth is unnatural and unfavorable, for when the embryo is expelled feet-first and its body is expelled before its head and feet, and one of its hands, or its foot, or its head and hand, or its belly, and very quickly it is turned on its neck, and similar to those unfavorable positions.
Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to be wise and skillful in all these matters, and to guard against error and deception.
And I am showing all the forms of those [births], and what skill is required in them so that it may be understood and acted upon.
When the fetus is expelled head-first by natural effort, and this is strenuous for the woman and the birth is difficult, and sometimes her strength is already weakened, then have her sit, or have the women support her, and evaporate her vulva with cooked fenugreek and warm oils.
Then the midwife inserts a small, flat spatula between her labia and with it cuts the secundina (afterbirth), or scrapes it with her fingernail until it is free of moisture. And press the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then the woman should be given an enema of mallow and fenugreek poultices with a syringe.
Then instruct her, during the enema, to be compressed and make her sneeze with a sternutatory; and hold her mouth and nose for an hour, for the fetus will be expelled immediately.
And if both hands of the fetus are expelled at the beginning, then you must gently reduce them little by little. But if it does not obey the introduction, then place the woman on a stool and raise her legs, and then shake the stool on the ground; and the woman should be held so that she does not fall from the shaking.
And if the hands are not returned inside and the fetus dies, then incise them and extract the remains of the fetus, or tie the body to its hand with a cloth and extract it evenly, for it will be expelled.
When the fetus comes out feet first, then you must push it upwards, and gently turn the fetus, and then grasp it, and then take one foot and extend it little by little.
But when it has come out between the thighs, then you command her to strain, and make her sneeze with a decoction, for it will be expelled if God wills it.
And if it is not expelled by what we have recommended, then you return the fetus little by little until you place it in its natural position, for then it will be expelled with ease.
And if it is obstructed despite all that we have recommended, then take mallow and dissolved gum mucilage, and pound everything well in a mortar, and then anoint the woman's vulva and the lower part of her belly with this. And then make her bathe in lukewarm water, up to the hypochondria (region below the ribs).
And when you see that the lower parts are softened, then give her a fumigation of myrtle and apply it to her. And when she has had the fumigation for an hour, then make her sit on a chair, and then make her sneeze and hold her mouth shut, and remove this [fumigation], and press the lower part of her belly with gentle compression, for the fetus will then be expelled immediately.
Thus [you deliver] the fetus lying sideways, supporting it with one of your hands. Be skillful in reducing [the dislocation] with your hand; but if you cannot reduce it, then raise the woman and make her walk.
And if she cannot walk, then make her lie on the bed, and shake her hips with vigorous shaking, and then administer bandages which I have recommended made of mucilage.
And if labor has begun, then make her sit on a stool, and then work on reducing [the dislocation] of the hand, and grasp the fetus according to its natural position, and make the woman sneeze and command her to strain until it is expelled.
Thus [deliver] the fetus over the cervix, spreading your hands, and its face is towards its mother. Let the midwife hold the hand of the fetus and then rotate it little by little, and thus [the delivery will be] facilitated.
And when it is grasped, then place on the cervix that which we have recommended of oil and mucilages.
And then instruct her to strain little by little, and when you have done this, then make her sit on a stool and instruct her to lean on her right side, and then instruct her to bear down; and make her sneeze with a concoction [or medicine]. The prepared fetus exits on its side.
When the mother's horror subsides according to this manner, consider, and then you incise the afterbirth with your fingernail until it is emptied of its moisture.
And if the fetus is suspended by its face and neck, then you incise it at the umbilicus, so that it may not be retained and die.
And then push the fetus towards the inner parts until you place it as needed, and tell the woman to push herself a few times, and it will be expelled easily in this way, if God wills it.
The exit of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three or four, but this is rare and happens late; but there are causes of this in nature, and they do not live.
And although they are multiplied, they share one placenta, and are separated by a membrane, distinguishing them within the umbilical ligament of each one. And sometimes they are expelled as one fetus by easy natural delivery, and sometimes they are expelled by difficult delivery as we have recounted.
And if some of their births are difficult and reach the point of those unnatural presentations we have mentioned, then you should treat them as we have recounted.
And if the midwife is skillful and does this with ease and skill, and with patience, and is ingenious according to every presentation, as she is prepared by her skill leading to health.
Concerning that which is formed in the womb from embryos and is aborted.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and even ten or more are formed in the mother.  And it has been verified by me that one woman aborted and expelled six, and another ten, all formed. This is established by the measurement of the glorious wise men.
And all this must be within the knowledge of the midwife, because it easily appears similar to her, and therefore she must oppose it with what is necessary.
Chapter 76.
On the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat a dead fetus with what we have said in the division of medication, and it has not yet been expelled, and you are forced to use a manual operation, then you must consider whether the woman is of sound constitution and is not afflicted by any of the conditions that endanger her life.
Then the woman should be positioned on the bed on her back, with her head inclined downwards and her thighs raised.
And the assistant should hold her from both sides, or she should be tied to the bed so that her body does not extend during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you moisten the mother's vulva with warm oils and a mucilage of mallow, fenugreek, and linseed.
And then let the midwife anoint her hand with those oils and mucilages, and gently insert her hand into the mother, and then search with her hand for the location where the umbilical cord is attached to the fetus.
And convenient places for this: then you consider if the fetus is positioned so that it descends head first, and then you grasp the umbilical cord at its eyes, or neck, or mouth, or palate, or under the chin, or at its clavicle, or in the appropriate places on the shoulders, or under the hypochondria.
But if the descent is feet first, then at the groin, or in the middle of the back.
And it is necessary to hold the umbilical cord with the right hand, and place its curve in the space between the fingers of the left hand, and gently insert the hand and place the cord in one of those places which we have mentioned, until the evacuation is complete.
And then place and add another umbilical cord, or a third if necessary, so that there is equal traction, and it is not extended only in a straight line.
Rather, it should be moved in all directions, so that its exit is facilitated, as is done in the extraction of a tooth.
And it is necessary that what is between them be mollified; and if any part of it is retained, then the midwife should lubricate some of her fingers with oil and expertly insert them and loosen what is retained.
Therefore, if part of the fetus is expelled, then the umbilical cords should be moved to a higher position; and do this until the entire fetus is expelled.
But if one hand emerges before the other, and it is not possible to reduce it because of its constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it; and then extract it until it is entirely expelled, and incise it either at the joint of the hand or at the elbow.
And thus you should proceed with the other hand and with both feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or there is constriction during its exit, or there is accumulated water on its head, then it is necessary to insert a spatulate and pointed instrument between the fingers and split the head and extract the water, or to break it with an instrument called an "alundat," the form of which will be shown with the forms of other instruments in the chapter which follows this one.
And similarly proceed if the fetus has a naturally large head: and then it is necessary to incise it with a scalpel, or to grasp it with the alundat as we have said; and then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head is out but constricted by the clavicle (furcula), then it should be split until the fluid which is in the feet is discharged.
But if it is not complicated, nor does it obey the attempt at extraction, then you should incise the head immediately as much as is possible for you.
And if the abdomen of the fetus is abscessed or dropsical, then it is necessary that you perforate it until the fluid within it runs out.
But if the fetus is feet-first, then its extraction is easier, and its direction to the mother's birth canal is easy.
But if it is constricted around the belly and feet, then it is necessary that you extract it with a cloth which you will have wrapped around your hand, and that you incise the belly and feet until that which is within them flows out.
But if the other limbs are obstructed and the head is turned back, then the midwife inserts her left hand; but if the mother's birth canal is open, then she inserts her hand into the womb, and searches with it for the head, and draws it with her fingers to the mother's birth canal, and then inserts one or two fingers into it, and pulls it with them.
And if the mother's birth canal is constricted by a hot abscess which harms her, then it should not be disturbed; instead, it is necessary to administer a perfusion of moist, oily substances. And apply plasters; and bathe the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the fetus is on its side, then, if possible, it should be rotated; and what we have said should be administered in the case of a live fetus.
And if that is not possible, then let it be cut into pieces, and extracted.
And it is necessary that no part of the afterbirth be left inside.
And when you have completed all your work, then administer what we have said concerning the treatment of the hot abscesses which may occur in the mother.
And if bleeding occurs, then bathe in styptic waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have seen a woman in whose womb was a dead fetus, and soon after that fire [i.e., pregnancy] again she became pregnant, and soon after that fire [pregnancy], the fetus died again. It happened to him that for a long time there was an abscess in his navel; and it swelled until it was opened, and it began to discharge pus.
And I was called to his care, and I treated him for a long time, and the wound did not heal.
Then I applied to him plasters of strong attraction for some causes, and I extracted a bone from him, and after a few days, I extracted another bone; and I was very amazed, since there should not be bones in the belly.
And then I considered that they were bones from the dead fetus.
Then I cleansed the wound, and extracted many bones from him, the woman being in good condition, and he lived in this way for a long time, discharging a little pus.
And I did not consider this strange, but because in this there is science and help in that the skilled physician administers healing with the hand.
Of the forms of instruments that are necessary to extract the fetus.
Form of incision by which the mother is opened:
This is the form of a constrictor in which the bandages are tied, to which tying there are two incisions at the two extremities of two poles/rods. However, this incision should be more subtle than a constrictor, and be made of ivory or boxwood.
And let the width of each rod be about two fingers, and the thickness of each about one finger, and the length of each is a palm and a half; and in the middle space between the two rods are two fittings of the same wood which are already fixed, of which the length is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mouth of the mother so that it is opened with them when you rotate the two prescribed incisions.
Another similar form to that one, but more subtle and lighter:
Let it be made of ivory or boxwood, similar in appearance to forceps. However, at its extremity are two additions, just as you see, and the length of these additions is about a palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you want to open the mother with them, then you make the woman lie on the bed with her legs hanging down, having opened what is between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument, just as you do with scissors, evenly, according to the amount you want to dilate the opening of the mother's womb until the midwife does what you want.
The form of vertigo which the ancients described:
Form of the impellent by which the fetus is expelled:
Form of the hindrance by which the large head is held back.  (The "2" likely indicates a superscript or annotation in the original text, perhaps clarifying the word "amisidat" or referring to a specific type of hindrance.)
And it resembles forceps which have teeth at the end, like a saw, and sometimes it is made long like forceps, according to this form, like a saw, which has teeth like saw teeth with which it is incised and held back. Form of slight hindrance:
Form of a hook having a spine/spike:
Form of a hook having two spines/spikes:
Form of a spatula having two ends/extremities with which the head is severed/cut.
Form of two broad spatulas.
All those instruments are exercised, and when the types of these are multiplied and prepared by the artisans, it is easier in its operation and more valuable to men because of its quantity/number.
And do not neglect those instruments, whatever they may be, that you have prepared, for the necessity of those is not excusable.
Of the extraction of the afterbirth.
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then you should instruct the patient to hold her breath, and then make her sneeze with a concoction, and press your hand over her mouth and nostrils, and if by this it is expelled, it is good.
And if not, then you take a pot and pierce a hole in its lid and place herbs in it, opening the mother's mouth, such as  mallow, rue, anise, chamomile, Arabic gum, cassia lignea, and centaury. All these herbs, or some of them, should be submerged in water in the pot, and place that over the fire.
Then place a reed cannula in the mother's mouth, and hold it there until the vapor reaches the mother's womb.
Then make her sneeze as we have said, and the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.
But if this treatment remains ineffective and it is not expelled, then you instruct the midwife to submerge her left hand in sesame oil or mallow poultice, and then insert it into the vagina and search for the afterbirth with it; and when it is found, then take it and remove it little by little. But if it is attached to the womb's walls, then insert her hand as we have instructed, and when she has found the afterbirth, extract it little by little as instructed, so that the mother does not collapse from a violent pull. Furthermore, it should be turned easily to the right and left sides.
And if the mother's mouth (cervix) is constricted, we have already recommended treatment with sneezing and herbal oil medication.
But if it is not expelled by all the means we have recommended, then be careful not to tire yourself with repeated attempts at extraction. However, what has come out from her into the mother's mouth (cervix) should be left attached to the woman’s vulva, and then cauterize it with ointment and tetrafarmacum; for that will cause it to rot over a few days and it will be dissolved and expelled.
But when it rots, a foul odor rises to the head and stomach, and this harms the patient; and then appropriate fumigations should be administered.
But some of the Ancients already tested fumigation with hellebore and dried figs.
Method for the instrument with which a woman is fumigated for the retention of menstrual blood and afterbirth:
Let it be made of glass or bronze.
And let the thin end be placed in the vagina and the wide end over the fire, and the fumigating substance placed on the embers of the fire.
And let her hold that inside until that fumigating substance is consumed and then another.
Concerning the treatment of an imperforate anus in a child.
Many children are born and their anuses are not perforated, which are very narrow and obstructed. Then it is necessary for the midwife to perforate that narrow passage with her finger; and if not, perforate it with a sharp instrument, and be careful not to touch the rectum.
And then place on it wool soaked in wine and oil; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is obstructed, then place a cannula in the opening for several days, and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
And if anal obstruction occurs due to the consolidation of a wound or abscess; then it is necessary that this consolidation be incised, and then it should be treated with what we have said about the treatment and the lead [presumably referring to lead-based ointments].
Concerning the treatment of fistulas that develop below [presumably referring to fistulas in the anal or perineal region].
The fistulas that develop in the lowest part below are knots and thickenings which develop near the anus externally or in the space between the buttocks.
And it is one fistula or several.
And as that knot is old and discharges a watery white humidity or subtle pus, and sometimes some of these fistulas are penetrating.
And those penetrating ones are known because of what comes out of them through discharge and by flatulence; and sometimes the patient suffers from discharge, and worms easily come out of them.
And sometimes these fistulas penetrate into the space between the buttocks, into the bladder, and into the urethra; and some penetrate into the hip joint and into the buttock.
And the way to distinguish between a fistula that penetrates into the anus and one that does not, is to insert your finger into the anus, and insert a thin probe of brass or iron into the fistula when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in it, then insert into it a thin probe of lead or horsehair until you feel the probe or hair against your finger.
And if you do not feel it at all, nor does anything come from the opening—neither discharge, nor worms, nor flatulence, as we have said—then know that it is non-penetrating, and then attend to its cure.
And when the fistula penetrates into the bladder or into the urethra, then its indication is thus:  suppression of urine from it, and prohibition of this so that the place may be consolidated with medicine.
But if it penetrates into the joint of the hip or into the bone and causes pain in the surrounding area, then its sign is that the probe penetrates there if there is no tortuosity in the bone, and pain in the surrounding area; and that there is always pus from it; and that medication is ineffective, nor is it consolidated with ointment.
And in all those penetrating fistulas, there is no complete cure, and the cure of these is the labor and vanity of foolish physicians on which they are fixed.
But in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing with iron is hoped for, according to what I shall recount to you, and this which I have already proven.
And it is that the patient lies between your hands on his back and raises his legs upwards, his thighs being inclined towards his belly, and then introduce a lead or bronze probe if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know the place where the probe penetrates.
And if the patient feels pain around the anus, then you should insert your finger into the anus.
And if you feel the probe itself penetrating by itself, besides what you feel between your finger and that fistula or flesh, then you know certainly that this is penetrating; and then do not work on it, for there is no cure in that, as we have said.
However, some say that it is healed slowly in some men.
And of the medication by which help is expected for him, it is that you ignite a fine cautery according to the width of the fistula, as is described before, and introduce that ignited cautery into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat that two or three times until you know that all those fleshy parts adhering to the fistula, which resemble a bird's quill, are cauterized.
And then treat it with poultices wrapped in linen until all those cauterized parts fall off, and then treat it with medicines that generate and consolidate flesh, and if it is healed, good; and if not, then it will not be healed at any time by any other treatment.
And if the probe enters, but does not penetrate beyond your finger which is in the anus, and between that and the probe there is a thick layer of flesh or fistula, and you see the fistula in that way, that it is the surface of the rectum, then dry the rectum from the beginning of the fistula, and you will see it dry with the probe, and it should be in the fistula until the incision reaches the place where the end of the probe arrives, and by the probe it is freed and removed.
And then cleanse those fistulous fleshy parts which resemble a bird's quill, and let no part of them remain.
But if you see a flow of blood come between you and your operation when you are cauterizing those tissues, then the best and lightest instrument is to cauterize with fire or with a sharp medicine, because cauterization with fire achieves two good things: that is, the cauterization of those adherent tissues, and the stanching of blood and drying of the humors.
And then treat it with poultices wrapped in linen or crushed flaxseed until the area forms a scab, and then treat the wound with ointments that generate and consolidate hard flesh.
And these are medicines in which there is stypticity and desiccation; when it is restored, it is healed and we do not fear recurrence.
But if the fistula penetrates to the rectum and is elongated from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the rectum and explore with it.
And so if you feel the fistula and between you and it there is a covering of fat or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then there is no operation there except by way of trust and hope.
Which requires one of three methods: either cauterize it as we have said; or dry it until you reach its cavity so that you can insert the poultices and the cure is close; and do not proceed much with incision lest you cut the muscle containing the rectum, and cause harm to the patient and thus without his will; and then treat it with what we have said, for it will heal quickly; or lengthen that covering and clean out the pus after drying it near the anus, as we have said, either with a probe or with another instrument of sharp point, and then clean those fistulous parts which are in the incision where you can with cauterization; and then strive to make the restoration of the whole wound with incision, and if the upper orifice is not consolidated, and the fistula remains open in the part of the anus, it will be easier for the patient. And sometimes the fistula ends in this way: when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is in the side of the anus around the surface of the body with the tail and in the margin of the anus, then take a probe with a perforated tip, which is like a needle for preparing hides in this shape:
and insert it into that fistula wrapped in five threads or around them; and then insert a probe with thread into the fistula until you reach its cavity.
But if it penetrates into the margin of the anus further inside, then you pull the thread from that hole so that you insert your finger into the anus; and pull out the end of the thread and join the two ends together, and tighten each one, and leave it for a day or two, and when you further cut the thread into the flesh, tighten it well until those parts of flesh are cut which are between the two ends of the thread and so that they fall away.
And then treat the wound until it is consolidated and healed.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, in whatever way is prepared for you, unless it is very much in the perineum, because it is not for you to do that lest you cut the muscle; and then treat it until it is healed.
The form of the pointed probe with which the fistula is probed: its curvature is very sharp and the other part is not sharp, so that it does not cut what is not necessary, as sometimes happens.
Concerning the suturing of hemorrhoids from which blood flows during their incision.
Hemorrhoids are of two types, for either they are between the buttocks similar to charcoal, or they are like bunches of grapes. And of these there are small and large ones, and the blood flows from them constantly and it is one, and there are many, and they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are according to the months of little humidity, from which flows citrine water or a little blood with continuous discharge, and they are according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is inside the anus is that you command the patient to strain and express himself until the anus emerges and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you should grasp and lift them with hooks or hold them with your fingernail, and then incise them at their roots.
But if the hooks cannot hold them because of their humidity and softness, then grasp them with rough cloth or cotton or a wool pledget, and extract them with your fingers, and then incise them and sprinkle on them some hot powders near the incision, so that they act on them in place of cautery; or cauterize them as is described in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat them with the other treatment until they are healed.
And if the anus does not obey egress, then stimulate the patient with a small, mildly irritating enema, and let it be washed with that water which is in the anus and which easily allows egress when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then their cause is slight, and it is that you grasp them with your fingernail, or that you grasp them with a hook, and incise them, and then treat them with what we have said until they are healed.
And whoever wishes to incise them, must administer sutures to them in this manner: take wrapped thread and insert it into a needle and then pull the hemorrhoids upwards, and make it penetrate with the needle into the root of the other part, and wrap the two ends of the thread in the lower part of the needle, and do so laterally; and tightly constrict the hemorrhoid with a strong suture, and then knot the thread and remove the needle.
And do this to all of them, and leave one of them untied, so that the superfluous blood may flow from it.
And then place a cloth soaked in rose oil or cerate over the anus; and place that over the anus and command the patient to rest. And then leave it until it falls off; and when it has fallen off, treat it with ointments or with another cure until it is healed.
And since they are old and that medicine is not effective, then you must scrape them with the point of a spatula or with your fingernail until they are moist and the crust above is removed, which prevents their consolidation; and then treat them until they are consolidated (healed).
Then remove them with more force than the first time until they bleed a lot and are excoriated (abraded); and then treat them until they are healed.
Know that an alamermar (or almesamir, likely referring to a boil or abscess), that is a boil, is nothing but a red, round swelling, according to the color of the body, similar to the head of a clove, and it is found throughout the body, and especially in the parts below the feet and in the fingers, and because of it, pain occurs below the swelling.
And then it is necessary that what is in the circumference of the boil be incised, and it should be held with forceps or a hook, and eradicated from its root; and then treat that (the wound).
And if you wish, cauterize it according to what has been previously explained in its chapter.
Moreover, a wart is a small, rough, fistulated (possibly meaning  "pus-filled" or "with an opening"), round eminence on the surface of the body, and many times it appears on the hands of children.
And its cure is that you stretch and incise it, or that you bind it tightly with a thread or with a hair so that it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize that, as we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine (caustic substance).
Moreover, a mole is a small, hairy or fistulated (pus-filled/with an opening), large eminence on the surface of the body, often growing in the face; and when cold touches it, it is felt as if a kind of ant bite, and it is found all around the body.
And its cure is that you take a cannula (small tube) of goose quill or vulture quill, and let it be strong, and place that over the mole until the circle of the cannula contains the parts of it; and then, rotate your hand with the cannula until you incise what is in the circumference of the mole, and you place that in the depth of the flesh, and you eradicate the mole from its root.
And if you wish, do this with a brass or iron cannula, according to this method:
Let the upper part of the cannula be drawn out to a thinness, blunt (that is, non-perforated), wrapped so that on the said [mole] the application of it may be smoothed and its rotation [easier].
And if you wish, cauterize the mole as was previously mentioned in the cauterization of athel in the chapter on cautery.
Concerning the instruments that are used to give a clyster (enema).
Sometimes a clyster is made of silver, or of tin, or of molten or hammered brass; and sometimes it is made of these instruments, small and large, according to those to whom they are administered, for that which is administered in the treatment of small children should be small; and for those whose rectums are fragile or painful, the clysters for them should be small.
The form of a large and a small clyster:
The head of its cannula, above which the bag is tied, should be wide according to this manner; and let there be a distinction where the bag is tied, as you see.
And the lower extremity, which is inserted into the rectum, should be blunt (that is, not open) and very smooth, tapering to a point; and on one side of it there should be two holes, and on the other side one hole, and let the size of the hole be according to the thickness of the rays and a little larger.
And let the bag, in which the medicines are placed, be made of animal bladder or sheepskin, and let its length be one palm.
And let it be perforated with many holes, so that it is woven; and let the space which is between the holes be the thickness of a finger.
And then insert into those holes strong, raw flax thread, about ten threads, or thereabouts; and attach the bag with that; and when the bag is placed in position, tighten it at the head of the clyster with two distinct, strong constrictions, and then cauterize each hole with that: But the manner of cauterizing with that is that you fill the bag with the medicine, which should be heated according to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is neither too hot nor too cold.
Then let the patient lie supine on their back, and raise their legs up and place under them a cushion on which there is already linen or a sheet; and then anoint the tip of the clyster with oil, or with egg white, or with fenugreek mucilage, or something similar; and then insert it into the rectum easily, and then let the attendant take the bag and squeeze it with two hands and vigorously until all that is in the clyster is evacuated into the bowel; and then remove the clyster and the patient lowers their legs.
And if the medicine forces him to evacuate, retain it as long as possible; and if he sleeps with it all night, it is more beneficial.
But if you administer this clyster in cases of diarrhea and the evacuation harms him, then you repeat the clyster until he is healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the rectum, then let the clyster be very thin and soft, of the kind with which infants are clystered.
On the treatment of wounds.
I have already said in the division on the treatment of wounds what was appropriate for the book, and I say thus the treatment of them with hand and with medicines according to the situation.
Therefore I say that wounds are diversified according to what caused the wounds and according to the location on which the wounds fall.
And the causes by which wounds are often made are thus:  a stone throw, or an incision by a sword or knife, or a blow with a lance, or with a stick, or with an arrow, or similar things from many causes.
But the wounds that are in the locations of the body are thus: a wound which falls upon the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the belly, or the thigh, and similar members.
And I am recounting the treatment of some wounds so that you may apply that reasoning and rule to other wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds, for the discussion of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore I say that when a simple wound occurs on the head and there is no fracture, then consider: if it is from a stone throw or the like, and it has only grazed the scalp and is a large wound, and you fear the occurrence of a hot abscess in that patient, then you should immediately consider and phlebotomize the cephalic vein, and do not delay this; and extract blood from him according to the quantity and his strength, unless some cause prohibits phlebotomy, especially if the blood that has come from the wound is little.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side from the wounded part; and guard the patient from repletion and from wine and coarse foods.
But if some cause prohibits bloodletting, then let his diet be reduced and he should not approach meat or wine.
And place on the wound, if a hot abscess occurs in him, a linen cloth soaked in plain rose oil or in wine in which a styptic has been dissolved.
But if it is certain that a hot abscess will form, then place on the wound, if it is recent, its own blood and the un-altered powder that I will shortly recommend.
And if the air has already altered it, then apply some ointment until it is healed.
But if the wound is large or is caused by a sword cut or similar, and its edges are not joined together, then join them with sutures according to the method of suturing I shall recommend.
But if the wound has exposed the bone and hangs, and there is no retention of it but a slight suspension, then incise it and then treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh in place of the bone, and order the patient to be fed foods which are thick: heads and  fowl and things similar, until it is healed.
And if you find putrefaction in the bone and it does not adhere to the bone, then you incise all that which is rotten and remove it, and then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which a flow of blood can occur, and it is not stopped by medication, then you search for the artery, and if you find it uncut, then you incise it with a spatula, or tie it; and if necessity compels you that what we have said is not sufficient, then you cauterize until the bleeding is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then their cause is light; thus you dust these with this powder, for it consolidates them before the air alters them.
And the method of the powder is this: take oil of roses one part, dragon's blood two parts, unquenched lime three parts; let all be well crushed and sifted, and with it let the wound be filled, and let it be bound with a good bandage until it adheres well to it, and is coagulated with the blood.
And then bind it above with powders and leave it, and do not unbind it while it is thus, that it is not festered, for the medicine is not removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes it does this alone with heat, when you do not have roses and dragon's blood at hand.
And do this same operation on large wounds when it is necessary that it be placed on them and they are tight, and they are recent wounds with their blood, which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, and especially when it is in summer time, then this powder is not suitable, but rather treat them with what we have said, so that you place on them some digestive ointments, or that you place on them powder of barley flour with water and honey until they discharge pus; and then treat them with the other cure until they are healed.
But if a fracture in the bone happens with the wound, and it is small, then you extract it with tweezers.
And I will describe the treatment of head fractures in what is poisonous.
Of the wounds of the neck.
But if the wound happens in the neck, then there is no difference in medication between it and a wound of the head when it is simple.
But if the nerves of the neck or an artery are cut, then if it is a nerve, there is nothing to do but to avoid treating it with something that causes strong astringency, such as vinegar and vitriol and things similar to them, because they are harmful to the nerves and contract them. And do not apply anything cold to the wound, because the substance of the nerves is cold and their continuation is with the brain, which is the highest of all the members.
And if it is cured with light medicines, such as being washed with sweet water, sometimes infused with rose oil or plain oil, and then, when it is washed with sweet water and other minor remedies according to this manner, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures, or draw the lips of it together, and then treat it with powders until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep or there is pus at its lowest part, then perforate it at the most depressed point.
But if an artery is incised in the wound and a flow of blood results, then separate or cut it, or tie it, or cauterize it if necessity requires it.
But if the vertebrae of the neck have been incised, or any part, and the veins of the neck have been saved, then you should adjust the edges of the wound with sutures on the neck's bone and do not touch that; first even it out and return it to its natural shape, and then tighten it with a suitable bandage, and leave it for a few days; and then treat it with ointments which have styptic and healing properties until it is healed.
But if the wound has caused damage to the bone, then  cleanse it, and if there are bone fragments in it, extract all those that you can; and what you cannot reach, leave it until the wound suppurates, and is separated and the damage is relieved.
And if the wound is recent, then sprinkle on it the powder which has been recommended to you. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with that of which the remedy will be found in its chapter.
If it is a wound from a lance or knife, and you see its depth, then consider further if there is a puffing out from the wound when the patient breathes; then know that the wound is mortal.
And if it is not deep and the wound is fresh with its own blood, then you should apply powder to it in the first hour, and do not bind it tightly so that the blood is not retained in its depth and returns to the heart and kills the patient, but apply an attractive ointment, and unbind it twice a day.
And if you do not have ointment at hand, then place old linen on the opening of the wound so that it draws out the moisture; and make the patient sleep on the wound so that what is collected there drains away.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound and the patient has not suffered spasms, tremor, heart problems, or stricture of the throat, and you see his other conditions are good, then know that the wound is safe; and then treat it with cauterization and other treatments until it is healed.
But if its healing is delayed, and it remains open for a long time, then know that a fistula has formed; therefore treat that according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from a sword or knife cut, and is simple on the surface of the foot or back, then treat it as previously described for sutures, if it is large, or with powder, if it is small.
But if damage has been done to the bone, and pieces have been cut from it, then you should clean the wound and try to extract those pieces if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then leave them until the wound suppurates, so that their extraction is easier.
And for other accidental wounds in other limbs, the treatment is similar to what we have said.
Of wounds of the abdomen.
The indisposition which happens in the abdomen is either great, small, or moderate.
And a small rupture is made difficult in another way, and it happens that their reduction is more difficult if you do not take care of their replacement to their place immediately, because it opens and is swollen, wherefore their entry is made difficult.
And therefore the moderate rupture is best, because in that one the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is difficult in the two species previously mentioned.
And know that the suture of the abdomen is according to four methods, but two sutures are common which are suitable for sutures of other wounds of the body.
And two other sutures proper to the abdomen are also used for other wounds, but however they are more proper in the suture of the belly.
And I recount to you each one with explanation and declaration.
Therefore I say that when the wound is small and some part of the intestine has emerged through it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then this is in one of two ways: either because of the smallness of the rupture as we have said, or when the intestine has become inflamed by the coldness of the air.
And therefore when this is so, then it is necessary that you warm it in such a way that you submerge a sponge or cloth moistened in lukewarm water alone, or that you boil in water squinant, cyperus, and spikenard, and bathe the intestine with this until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes this is done with a substance that has astringent properties, and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflammation.
And then, when you see the inflammation resolved, then you moisten the intestine with water in which mallow and marshmallow have been boiled, for by this the return of the intestine is aided with little difficulty.
But if the return of the intestine is hindered despite this treatment, then incise the rupture a little with an instrument with which hemorrhoids are incised.
And the first treatment is better, when it is appropriate that there is no incision, and there is no need to dry due to necessity and debility.
Regarding the instrument, this is its form:
One part of it is sharply curved, and the other part is not sharp, and the end is thin, and it should not be as thin as a spatula, but rather somewhat blunt.
And so, when the wound is enlarged and the intestine protrudes, then its reduction should follow its natural form, to its proper place, if possible in this way, for it is better.
But when the rupture is large, and is in the lower part of the abdomen, then you should make the patient lie on his back, and raise his legs higher than his head; and if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then you should raise his head and feet higher than the lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two sides of the abdomen, then always direct your attention and consideration to ensuring that the side where the wound is located is higher than the other.
And thus you should administer this in large wounds and in medium-sized wounds.
And in small wounds, apply what you have prepared, and then have an assistant, whose hands are clean, hold the entire rupture with his hand and adjust its lips, and then, from there to there, let him sew up any other necessary cause.  (The final phrase is slightly ambiguous, possibly implying sewing up any other openings or adjusting the edges further.)
And this is the common method of suturing: first, take a needle, or several needles according to the size of the wound; then, starting from the edge of the rupture, about a finger's breadth from the end, pass the needle through, making sure not to include any sinew in the two ends of the suture, using two ends of the thread which is under the suture, from the inside parts, until you have passed it through that area;  and when you have joined the two ends of the suture material and two ends of the thread, four ends are created. (The phrasing here is challenging;  "ciphac" seems to refer to an additional thread or support, perhaps used to reinforce the suture).
Then tighten with a double thread the loop of the needle, and sometimes from two sides simultaneously, and then bring together the two edges of the wound with a neat approximation; and then leave a finger's breadth, and insert another needle there, and then tie this one with thread as you did with the first needle; and continue doing this with the needles as needed until you have completed the entire closure of the wound.
And let the needles be of moderate thickness, neither too thick nor too thin, for thin needles cut the flesh too easily; and thick ones are difficult to pass through the suture, therefore they should be of moderate thickness.
And the needle should not be inserted too deeply into the two ends of the suture, so that the flesh is not unnecessarily cut and the wound does not open before it heals.
And the sutures should not be too long, lest the wound be hindered from healing.
Then blunt the ends of the needles so that they do not injure the patient during sleep; and place over the suture powdered substances on all sides, which will hold the ends of the needles; and leave those in place until you know that the wound is healed.
And this type of suture with needles is most suitable for small wounds, because for their suturing one or two needles, or thereabouts, are sufficient.
But the second suture is common: and it is that you suture the four extremities of the wound and two ends of the lip, at once, with a needle around which thread is wrapped, appropriate in both thinness and thickness.
And then, when with the needle you have penetrated these four extremities, return the needle to the same place from which you began, so that the retracted thread falls from the highest part into the wound, so that the suture is like a cloth suture with which cloths are tightened.
And place between each suture the width of the little finger.
And this suture is known to men.
And with such a suture I sewed up the wound which had befallen a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the disruption of the wound was greater than a palm, and two palms of intestine had come out from the middle of the wound, and the disruption was in the middle of the member; and then I reduced it after the intestine had been outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
And the wound was consolidated in the space of fifteen days or thereabouts, and I cured him and he is healed; and afterwards he lived for many years, and is preserved in his faculties.
And the other physicians had judged that he would not be healed; and it was by a miracle that he was, for I did not cure him with ointment, for it was in a place where ointment was not found nor any medicine, but I placed old linen on the wound twice a day and washed it continually with honey water until it was healed.
But I shall describe two kinds of sutures proper according to the account of Galen (or a similar authoritative source, as "Paraula" is likely a variant spelling or corruption of a name like Galen).
And it is that you begin with the suture of the intestine (or possibly "cord"), and you insert the needle from the outside to the inside.
And so, as the needle penetrates the intestine (or cord) and into that same laceration, following its width and length across the whole body, leave the end of the thread into which you have inserted the needle; and make the needle penetrate at the other end, from the inside to the outside, at the other extremity of the wound.
And when you have made that penetration, then you make it penetrate again at that same extremity of the wound, from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the thread that is in this part, and make the needle penetrate at the other extremity, from the inside to the outside.
And make your penetration with that [needle] with which you made that penetration into the thread at the extremity of the wound which is on the patient's side until all of it penetrates.
And then begin from that same side and sew that [wound] with the end of the thread on the other side, and extract the needle from the intestine (or cord) which it penetrated; and then return it to that intestine (or cord) and sew the end of the thread that is on the other side with that extremity of the wound; and extract it from the intestine (or cord) which is on the patient's side.
And do this once and again, and do this one time then another time, until you sew everything that is outside according to that example.
And the sum of this manner of suturing is that you sew with a running stitch, with which you sew skins, in such a way that you sew the thread once from the side of the extremity of the intestine (or cord), and that you leave it another time until you have completed it.
But the second proper type of suture, which I call *Gualia*, and this is according to what I have recommended to you, is that you sew it according to a similarity with which some physicians sew it, such that you approximate each part to its corresponding part in the appropriate manner, and apply the end of one part to the end of the wound, and the end of the wound to the other end of the part, and this is done as I have recommended to you.
So that you place the needle in the extremity of the wound closest to the outside part, and make it penetrate to the inside part in that same place, and leave the two ends of the thread; and then turn the needle and make it penetrate from outside to inside through the two ends of the thread.
And then turn them each, and make each penetrate from the inside part to the outside part through two other extremities of the wound which are on the opposite side.
And this manner of suturing is better than the common, light suture, and it is the suture of which I previously made mention.
This is the method of Gualia.
And more, some say, the suturing of the belly is rectified with two needles, that is, that you insert into each of them a thread, and begin with the introduction of the needle from the side that is nearest to you, and make it penetrate to the other side according to the suture of those who sew leather similarly.
And know that when the disruption is in the middle of the abdomen, then its suture is more difficult than in other places of the abdomen.
But the judgment of the treatment of a wound is the judgment of other wounds.
The reason is that when you treat the fresh wound with its blood before the air alters it, and you return the intestine and sew it and prepare it decently, then place on it incarnative powder.
And if the air has already altered the wound, then place on it some ointments that are present to you until they form a hard skin and the threads fall out, and they are consolidated with cyprus and myrrh; and then treat it as is the treatment of other wounds until it is healed.
But if you do not have the medicines present, then place on it immediately, as soon as it begins to discharge pus, old linen, and change it twice a day, as has been taught to you, until it is healed, for you will not need other medication in many cases when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound will spread to the principal members, then it is necessary that you soak wool in good quality olive oil, or in rose oil, and place it in the area around the places that are between the armpits and under the armpits.
And if you feel pain or putrefaction in the intestines, for many times this happens, then cauterize it with black, warm, styptic wine, and especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and has made a penetrating exit into its cavity.
And know that that which is in the large intestine is of easier healing than that which is in the small intestines.
And it is of difficult healing because the intestine which is called "jejunum" does not receive healing from a wound that falls upon it at all; and this is because of the great multitude of veins that are in it and because of the magnitude of those veins, and because of the subtlety of the body of that intestine, and because of its proximity to the nature of the nerves.
But if that which proceeds from the wound is the web [likely referring to a membrane or tissue], and you obtain that recently, reduce it according to your skill with which you reduce the intestines similarly.
But if it has passed into the space and has already become green or black, then you should bind it with gall over the place that is blackened, so that a flow of blood comes, for in the web are veins and arteries.
And then incise that which is beyond that ligament, and place the two ends of the gall hanging from the part below the wound outside it, so that the attraction of it towards the falling of the web and the emission of the arm of the wound may be relieved for you.
A reminder of wounds when they happen in the intestine.
When a disruption occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it is possible that it may be restored in some men, for this reason because I saw one who had been wounded in his belly with a lance blow, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old and had become a fistula, and from it issued a discharge and flatulence.
Then I pursued his cure, although I was not confident in his healing, but I continued to subtly treat it until it was healed and the place was consolidated.
And I, after I saw the place already consolidated, feared for the patient that an accident of illness might befall him in his belly; and no such ill accident befell him, and he remained in his usual disposition, eating and drinking, and then entered a bath and exercised in his ways.
And some experienced ones have said that when a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it should be tied according to this manner:  that you take ants with large heads; and then bring together the two lips of the wound and place one of those ants, which has its mouth open, upon the two lips of the wound; then when it bites upon those and clenches its mouth, cut off its head, for it will adhere and not be dissolved.
And then place another ant near the first, and do not cease doing this with ants, one near another, according to the extent of the whole wound.
And then reduce [the intestines] and sew the wound; and let those attached heads remain in the intestine until the intestine is healed; and let no harm befall the patient.
And it is also possible that the intestine be tied with fine thread which is made from the intestine of an animal, since it is better for a needle; and it is that you take the end of these intestinal threads, and it should be very clean, and then tie its end with the thread, wrapped, and then insert that thread into needles and sew with it the intestine, and then reduce it into the disruption [the rupture].
And this method of suturing with ants is only a matter of trust and hope.
But if the wound is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, because then it is only a reduction [in size], and not a complete healing of it.
Concerning the treatment of fistulas and fistulae.
