Know that every wound and abscess, when it ages and becomes old, and becomes an ulcer, and is not consolidated and discharges pus constantly and is not excised, then, in whatever member it may be, is universally called a fistula, and we call that a zukem.  (Note: "Zukem" appears to be a variant spelling or a word from a related dialect meaning fistula).
And a fistula, truthfully, is a hardened, white, knotted formation, which is painless and has a concavity like the concavity of a bird's claw; and therefore it is called a knotted ulcer, that is, claw.
And in some men it is moist, which discharges pus; and very quickly the moisture is excised [or removed] in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is thick, and sometimes it is thin, and sometimes it is coarse, and sometimes it is subtle.
I have already shown all those causes, and their signs, and their cure with medicines, in the division [of the text/treatise].
And it is necessary that I make known to you the cure of fistula, which is done by means of an operation with the hand.
And know that fistulas occur in all the members of the body; for there are fistulas which reach the large veins and arteries, or the nerves, or the diaphragm, or the intestine, or the bladder, or to one of the ribs, or to one of the vertebrae of the back, or to one of the joints composed by composition, such as those of the hand and foot; and those which damage the proximity of a principal member and similar.
Therefore, that one of these fistulas which is according to this manner is among the illnesses of difficult healing, and does not receive a cure, and occupation with it is labor and ignorance.
And if it is one of the fistulas whose depth does not reach those places that I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its cure, and wish to arrive at knowledge of it, then take a probe of iron or brass, and if the fistula goes straight, and search for it with it.
But if the fistula is tortuous, then investigate it with a slender lead probe, for lead, with the lightness of its body, is easily bent around the tortuous path.
And if the fistula has many horrific windings, it is not possible for you to ascertain its course with a probe.
Then, inject a cleansing fluid through the opening, for the moisture by which you irrigate it will go through the other openings and clean them.
And then inquire carefully, in whatever way is possible for you to know if there are bones or nerves there, and if the depth of the fistula is far or near, or if it is a fistula which has many openings, and attend to all this with the sum of your strength, with your questioning, with which you question the patient, and the discovery of the pain through your compression with the hand over the area, and the signs and accidental causes of the abscess.
And when you have considered all those things with true judgment, then proceed to the treatment with confidence:  and it is that you consider then if the fistula is nearby and apparent, or in a safe place, far from a joint, or from a nerve, or from an artery, or from a vein, or from one of the places which I have told you about.
Then you dry the fistula as was said before in its description, and remove what is in that filtration and of corrupted flesh, and what has grown in the opening of the flesh, or of skin and such things, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the fistula is of great depth, and is straight, then it is necessary that you incise it in the front as far as is possible for you, and then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh; and then apply cauteries wrapped in sharp medicines, and introduce them into the depth of the fistula, which you have grasped with an instrument (or iron).
And do this several times until the sharp medicine corrodes all that is rotten in the depth of the fistula from the corruption.
And then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you cauterize it as we have recounted.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone, and you are certain of this, then you should investigate it and dry it as we have recounted, if you do not prohibit any cause from a vein or nerve or principal member as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then you scrape it until all the corruption is removed; and then treat it with something that will cause it to grow flesh until it is healed.
And if it is not healed and discharges pus as it did before, then know that you have not achieved the total eradication of the corruption; and then uncover it again and meticulously perform the scraping and cleansing with your utmost strength, and then restore it.
And so if it is healed, good; but if not, then know that that corruption is in the deep recesses of the body, which you have not reached, and then for you in that case there is nothing to do but leave it.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or it is possible for you to pull it out, then you should pull it out with fine forceps which are suitable for this.
And if there are many bones, then meticulously perform the extraction of all of them, and of these leave nothing behind with your strength.
But if any of the humidities cause you difficulty, and in their incision there is no fear or mistake, then incise those ligaments or flesh if they are there, and free the bones.
But if you are not ready to remove the bones at this time, then apply something to the wound that will cause it to suppurate, and leave it until that reaches the area around those bones of flesh and ligaments, and keep the wound from closing, and keep the incision that you made in your operation constricted.
Then, if you fear, place on it a cloth soaked in crushed rue with oil, or a cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment, or in any of the green ointments, because when those ligaments suppurate, and the bones are separated from them, their removal and extraction is facilitated.
But if it is a large bone, such as a leg bone or thigh bone or similar to those, and only its surface is corrupted, then scrape that with a rasp only superficially until that blackness and corruption is removed, and then restore the wound.
But if the corrupted part is large, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to close that and completely incise to the place where the corruption has reached, and then treat that until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his thigh, so that you may use that as an example and help in your operation.
There was a man young in age, around 30 years old, who experienced pain in his thigh, and from the inner parts, pus spread through the matter within the body until the matter reached the thigh, and it was matter of an abscess with a large abscess, and there was no external cause for it.
And then time passed with that due to the error of the physicians until the abscess was opened, and much matter flowed from it, and its treatment was delayed until the leg became fistulated, and many orifices were made in it, all of which discharged pus and the humors of the body.
And then many physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no wisdom in those men in the art, until I was called.
And I saw his leg and the matter flowing from those orifices in great quantity; and already the body of that man was entirely weakened and his color was jaundiced (citrine).
And then I inserted a probe into one of those orifices and the probe reached the bone.
And then I quickly examined all the orifices and found that they reached from all parts of the leg.
And then I considered and incised one of those orifices until I uncovered a part of the bone, and I found it corrupt, and it was already rotten, corrupt, and blackened; and a part of the leg was already corrupted where it had penetrated to the marrow.
And then I sawed away what was uncovered and prepared of the exposed bone to me.
And I believed that in the bone there was nothing but that corruption which I cut away and sawed, and I had already eradicated it.
And then I applied the restoration of the wound with consolidating medicine for a long time, and it is not consolidated (healed).
And then I again iterated and uncovered the bone a second time upon the first uncovering, and I found continued corruption in the bone.
And I sawed away what appeared to me of that corruption, and then I was thwarted in making its cure and restoration, and it is not restored.
And then I uncovered it another time and did not cease to incise the bone little by little, piece by piece, and forcefully attempted its restoration, and it was not restored until I had incised about a palm's breadth of it, and that I extracted with its marrow, and then I restored that with medicines, and it is slightly consolidated and healed.
And further iteration in the operation and sectioning of it is not necessary except for the disposition of its fragility and for the scantiness of its tolerance and fear of death, for it happened to him at all hours through the superfluity of evacuation, a bad syncope.
And therefore it is healed by a complete healing, and nothing is in place of the hard bone flesh; and its disposition in its body is made good and its strength returned; and it is converted to its dispositions and caused him no impediment in walking nor harm in any matter.
But if it is a prominent bone in a place already excised, then you must excise that according to this manner: and it is that you take a ligature and bind that to the extremity of that prominent bone, and command someone to extend it upwards; and place another ligature of thicker wool over the first ligature; and then tie that over the flesh that is below the bone, and extend the extremity of it so that you pull the flesh to the parts below, and you uncover the flesh over the place which you wish to saw so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
And place a piece of wood or a board decently under the bone, for when you do this, the saw is not prohibited from cutting the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be over the corrupted place little by little, for if there is corruption in the concavity of the bone and it does not appear in its outward appearance to the senses, then force the sawing of it another time.
And if the bone is corrupted and not prominent,  but one part is continuous with another, and the corruption is in the middle or in a part of it, then you uncover the flesh from all parts completely; and then place wood underneath, and then saw it from the first part where the corruption is until the sawing is continuous with the other part.
And let the sawing be according to the place of the corruption, little by little, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, incise that corrupted joint, and saw the bone where it is continuous with the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuity of two joints, then there is no reason but ingenuity [or skill/cleverness] in it.
And if the corruption is in the chest [presumably meaning the palm] of the hand or foot, it is very difficult; however, it is necessary that you investigate the corruption in whatever way appears to you, and that you open it, and clean it according to each disposition which is possible for you, and with every tool [or ingenuity/skill] be rectified to you, when it does not oppose you vein or nerve.
And know that the incisors and saws to incise these bones are many according to the location of the bones, and the preparation of them, and the thickness of them, and the subtlety and the size and the smallness of them, and the hardness and their rarity; therefore it is necessary that you prepare for every kind of operation an instrument convenient to that operation.
And know that this operation signifies to you the kind of instrument of which you have need; because with you is great study and long and knowledge of the ways of this art, and testimony of ways of illnesses, because then you already have for yourself what is convenient in the matter of instruments for every illness.
And I am forming for you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments which you will place as simple ones, with which you will proceed to your prohibition which you will experience upon the others. Of the incision of extremities and the sawing of bones.
Extremities may be affected either by an intrinsic or extrinsic cause; or if you have treated that corruption with those medicines and the treatment is not effective, or you see the corruption spreading through the limb without any apparent cause, then you must incise that limb to the point where the corruption has reached, so that the patient escapes death or the worse affliction of limb loss.
And the sign of this happening is that the limb turns black as if it were burned by fire, or the blackness spreads until the putrefaction reaches the limb and threatens to spread throughout the body; and therefore, treat it with an incision.
Similarly, if the cause of the corruption is the sting of some venomous creature, such as a sea scorpion, a viper, or a spider, and similar things.
And if the corruption or sting is in the extremity of a finger, then you incise the finger and do not give space to the corruption so that it spreads to the hand.
And similarly, if it happens in the hand, then you incise it from the root of the palm, and do not give space to the corruption to spread to the forearm.
And if it affects the arm, then incise the arm up to the elbow joint.
And if the corruption passes beyond and you see it around the elbow, then in that case the patient's death is certain; and to administer anything other than this in treatment is a waste of effort.
And similarly, if corruption affects a toe, then you incise it up to one of the ankle bones.
And if it takes hold in the heel, then you incise the whole foot. But if it ascends into the knee, then you incise the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the corruption has already reached the knee, then there is no remedy for him but dismissal, and to leave the patient to die.
And the manner of incising the limb or sawing it is that you tighten a ligature on the lower part which you wish to incise, and tighten another ligature above the location; and let the assistant extend a ligature to the lower parts, and let the assistant extend another ligature above; and you separate the flesh which is between the two ligatures with a spatula until all the flesh is uncovered; and then let it be incised or sawn.
And it is necessary that linen cloth be placed on all parts so that it does not touch the healthy place, and that the patient's injury and hot abscess be properly dressed.
But if you encounter a flow of blood when you operate, then you carefully cauterize the affected area, or place on it some powders that staunch the blood; and then return to your treatment until it is completed.
And then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage, and treat it until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his foot, this same accident which I have recounted to you, which is that a blackness occurred in his foot with a burn that resembled a fire burn.
And that corruption occurred first in his toe before it spread to the whole foot.
And then that man prepared himself, because he saw the cure proceeding to the limb with such vehemence that he suffered from pain and burning, and he incised that foot up to the joint, and thus it healed.
A long time later, the same corruption occurred in the finger of his index hand.
And then he came to me, and I tried to repel that superfluity with what I applied to his hand in the way of medicine, to cleanse his body; and the superfluity was not repelled and began to spread to another finger until it began in the hand.
And he asked me to incise his hand, but I did not want to do so, hoping for the repulsion of that superfluity, and moreover fearing lest the incision of his hand prove fatal, because the patient's strength was failing.
And after he departed from me, he returned to his land, and it was reported to me that that man prepared and incised his entire hand, and he was healed.
And this narrative is added only to help in the case of this disease, and to indicate a method by which it may be understood and according to which it may be treated.
Concerning the treatment of sinuses and the nature of clyster administration with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in fleshy limbs and the space of the abscess is extended until it forms a collection of pus, and then it is broken and perforated, and the pus has flowed out, leaving the place empty as if it were a vessel, and the skin that is over it is like thin cloth, and it has not reached further corruption, and it does not involve the bone or nerve or ligament, it is called a sinus, and not a fistula.
But however, if the space is extended to the point that the corruption affects some member, then it is called a fistula and not a sinus.
And its treatment is to incise that skin entirely as it is involved, especially when it is thinned and is like cloth, and to ensure that it does not adhere to the location due to its corruption.
But if you suspect that the skin adheres and that the corruption has not spread further, and there is swelling of the skin, then you treat that with a clyster.
And consider the sinus, and then if it is large, and the arm that is affected has a foul odor, then clysterize it with an Egyptian medicine which is made of oil, sour wine, and resin, taking equal parts, and let them be mixed in a vessel, and let them be cooked over the fire until the medicine becomes thick and reaches the consistency of honey.
Then take what is necessary from it and dissolve it with water and honey; and clysterize the sinus with this, and constrict its orifice, and leave the medicine in it for the amount of two hours.
And then extract that with a sponge; and do this for as many days until the sinus is cleansed and the foul odor is removed.
And sometimes it is clysterized with lye water, when this medicine is not present to you; and you take the ashes of vines, and put water over it; and then filter that and clysterize with it until you are confident that the sinus is then clean.
And if the patient does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the lye water, then clysterize with water and honey in which crushed resin is mixed, and clysterize with mixed wine and honey, for it is a property of honey to cleanse and wash, and wine makes it adhere to the sinus, especially when the wine has an excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and are confident that the sinus is now clean, then clysterize it with something that causes flesh to grow there.
And you should take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or clysterize with some other medicines and ointments that possess the properties which I have already written about in the treatise on ointments.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow, so that the clyster cannula cannot be inserted, then widen it little by little with a small iron instrument, and place a small, rolled-up cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment or basilicon ointment inside until it is widened.
And similarly, if the opening is wide, then approximate its lips with a suture and leave enough space for the clyster to enter without difficulty.
And if the opening from which the pus flows is situated higher up, then dry the lowest point of the area so that the pus will flow downwards, for when the pus is confined within the depth of the sinus, flesh will not grow there.
And if it is not possible for you to dry the sinus to the lowest point of the bone as desired, then make sure to prepare the limb with a preparation which allows the pus to flow easily as you see fit, and that nothing obstructs the drainage of the pus.
But the cloth that is over the opening of the sinus, you should change that frequently so that whatever pus collects there drains away. And so that you may know if the pus that flows from the wound is much or little, or is digested, or is undigested.
And by this you will ascertain the state of the sinus, whether the patient feels pain there or not, and whether there is an abscess there.
And as your treatment continues in this manner, and you see that the material that was placed over the opening of the sinus is now adhering, or is about to adhere, then on the second or third day loosen it, and when you loosen the dressing, then change the cloth for another and renew the ointment.
But if near the sinus there is a subtle poison, then do not be discouraged by its adhesion, for its healing is imminent. For there are some men in whom the generation of flesh in their wounds is slow, and there are those in whom the opposite is true.
And if undigested pus has flowed from the sinus for many days, then know that the sinus will not adhere; and if the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and the cause is prolonged, then you should adjust the drying quality of the medicines. And if the medicines are moist in their substance and in their virtue, they are dry, such as diafinicon ointment, when excess is added to it as a  treatment.
And the best remedy with which it is cured is that you take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it in rose oil, and add old, good wine to its substance, and mix that with it, and use that.
And it acts similarly in that case, when there is no pus: diafinicon ointment, honey cooked until thickened, and use that; or take myrrh, aloes, and incense, grind it all and pulverize it over honey while it is on the fire, then spread this on a cloth and bind it over the sinus; and spread that cooked honey (until thickened) and pulverize it on a cloth and bind it.
And sometimes aristolochia longa and lily of the valley roots (and it is lily of the valley) and chickpea flour and centaury are administered with it; and use those simples or compounded, grind them and sift them and powder them over the honey that has been mentioned; and use it.
And if the sinus is prolonged, and is not healed by this treatment, then know that the corruption is in the bone of it, and it already makes inquiry into the bones or other hard parts or ligaments; then you cure it with a fistular cure, as is said above.
Of the cure of paronychia and worn nails and added finger incision and section.
Paronychia is an abscess which arises under the nail of the finger or toe of the hand or foot, and quickly spreads to other fingers, and as it is prolonged and its cure is less valued, or it is abscessed by a hot abscess, or it is corrupt, and throws out pus until it corrodes the root of the nail, and quickly corrupts the whole nail, and quickly its corruption reaches the bone until there is a foul odor to it, and the extremity of the finger becomes swollen, and its color becomes dark.
And therefore when you have that with what we have said in the division and you do not succeed with your treatment, then it is necessary that you incise with iron all the superfluity that remains of the nail and then cauterize the wound near that, because a hot cautery is very helpful in this.
But if the bone is sound and the nail is healthy, and the additions of the nail, now empty, compress the flesh at the underlying parts, and begin to prick and injure it, then it is necessary that you place a thin blade under the angle of the nail which pricks the flesh, and push it up and incise that flesh easily, and place on what remains of the flesh corrosive, cauterizing medicines until it is all removed; and then cure that with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already reached the bone, then it is necessary that you incise that bone and extract it, for a bone that is corrupt will never heal.
And if you see the corrosion and corruption spreading in the finger, then you should incise it at one of the joints, according to the advice previously given, and then cure it until it is healed.
But if the nail suffers a blow or friction, and experiences severe pain, then you should first bleed the patient, and then dry the nail with a sharp spatula with a transverse cut from above downwards, and be careful that the cut does not reach the flesh beneath the nail, for you will cause the patient severe pain, and it will be the cause of the generation of superfluous flesh in the area; and then treat that area with something that relieves pain.
But a finger that is born in the hands of some men is entirely fleshy, or has very little bone, or very little nail.
And some of them are born at the root of a joint, or their growth is in some of the joints of the fingers. But that which is born in some joint of the finger does not hurt, and that which is born in the joints is easily injured.
And for those that are fleshy, the incision is easy, and you should incise that below its root with a broad spatula.
But for that which is born at the root of a joint, the cure is difficult; and therefore beware of its excision.
But for that which is born on a finger between the joints, you should first incise its flesh with a round incision down to the bone, and then saw it with one of those saws suitable for this, and then treat it until it is healed.
But consolidation which happens to the fingers between their knuckles, either by the birth of a man thus formed, or by the consolidation of a wound, or by burning with fire, or the like; then it is necessary that you separate that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form.
And then place between them cloths soaked in rose oil, so that they are not lightly consolidated, and separate them, or place between them a thin sheet of lead until it is consolidated as needed.
And similarly, if consolidation occurs in some fingers with the palm, then you should separate that consolidation according to how it is situated and suitable to the shape of the limb.
Concerning the incision of vices, they are various.
Vices are various, and they are twisted, thick veins full of melancholic superfluities which appear in many parts of the body, and their greatest occurrence is in the legs, and especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and porters.
Then it is necessary that you administer a purging of the body of black bile several times with strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilic vein.
But the cure of them with iron is according to two methods: one is that they be incised and that black blood be drawn out, and the other method is that the vein be raised and entirely extracted.
The incision is done in this manner: first bathe the leg with very hot water until the thick, foul blood is resolved from the leg. Then bind the patient's leg from the highest part of the thigh to below the knee with a bandage, and then cut the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision. Then raise the black blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg upwards and from above downwards, until the quantity of blood you wish and deem sufficient has been extracted, which will remove the patient's ailment.
Then bind it up, and instruct the patient to abstain from foods that engender black bile.
And repeat the treatment by phlebotomy when the veins are full and this harms the patient.
But their elevation is according to this method: you who raise the patient's leg, if there is much hair on it, then put it in a bath and bathe the leg with hot water until it reddens and the veins are prominent; or, if a bath is not available, let it be exercised strenuously until the limb is heated.
Then incise the vein on the straight part of the vein by a longitudinal incision behind the knee, or at the lowest part behind the ankle.
Then open the vein with an incision and scarify the vein on all sides until it appears sensitive, and it is from this appearance that you see that redness turning to blackness.
And then, when you have opened the vein, you will see that white thing which is like a cord.
Then you place a thread under it so that it is elevated and is drawn away from the vein, and suspend it with a golden or lead weight.
Then make another incision near that incision for three fingers' breadth, and then scarify the vein from the part above the vein until it appears.
Then elevate it and incise it in the second incision towards the heel, and then extract it and elevate it until it is pulled out through the second incision, and then extract it from the incision that is above it.
And do this until you extract it from the incision that is highest or most superior of all the incisions, until it is all removed; and then incise it.
And if it does not obey you in being extracted and elevated, then introduce a needle with strong double thread and tie it and extract it, or introduce a thread under it and rotate your hand in every direction until it is ejected. And be careful that it is not incised; for if it is broken, it becomes difficult for you to elevate it much and it becomes a danger for the patient because of that injury.
And when it is all removed, then place on the wound site wool soaked in rose wine and olive oil, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the vein is constricted, having tortuosity to which there is a twisting in parts, and it is not straight as we have said, then it is necessary that you make incisions upon it in every part of its tortuosity and in the places of its twisting, and then suspend it with hooks until you elevate it entirely.
And be careful in your incision that you do not cut the vein and that you do not injure it, for it will be difficult for you to elevate it; and use your prudence in this.
The form of the incision with which the vein is cut:
It is not made pointed like hooks, nor is it of a sharp extremity so that it does not injure the vein; and it is of thick flexibility, like lion, for if it were thin, it would cut the vein by its thinness; it has superfluous thickness as we have said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, as in Al-Ategi in the land of the Arabs and in the hot and arid lands of India, of little humidity, and in which neither grass nor trees grow.
And very easily it is generated in other places of the body besides the legs.
And its gestation is from putrefaction that comes from under the skin, just as serpents, worms, and scabies, and worms engendering between the skin and the flesh, come from within the body.
And a sign of the susceptibility to the coming of it is that in the leg a vehement inflammation occurs; and nearby the place is blistered; and nearby the vein begins to emerge from the place of that blister, which resembles the root of a plant.
And when its extremity appears, then you should wrap around it a piece of lead weighing one drachm to two drachms, and tie it and leave the lead suspended from the leg.
And as many times as you see something of it coming to the outside parts, wrap that in lead and tie it. And if the growth is prolonged, then you incise a part of it and wrap the remainder; and do not incise its root until it has come out, for if you incise it, it is retracted and enters the flesh and causes an abscess and putrefaction in the place and a bad ulceration.
Therefore it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is expelled from the body and no part of it remains in the body.
And from this vein it has issued in some men to a length of five palms and fingers, and it has already come to my attention that one of twenty palms has issued.
And if it is incised at the time of its treatment, then introduce a probe into its opening and perforate it with a long passage through the body, until all that is in the matter is evacuated, and treat the putrefaction of the place for some days with medicines; and then cure it according to the treatment of abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, and especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or in the foot, and many orifices come to it, and a branch issues from each orifice.
And therefore cure it in the way we have said in the division and with what has been said above.
Concerning the incision of worms generated under the skin, and it is called bovine illness.
This illness is called in some lands near us bovine illness because it often happens to cattle and is a small worm which is born between the skin and the flesh, and goes throughout the body rising and falling, and is shown by the feeling of its movement from limb to limb until it breaks the skin and comes out.
And its generation is by the putrefaction of certain humors, just as worms and serpents and earthworms and ascarids happen in the belly.
And it is feared for its malice, for when it goes into the body and is elevated to the head, and has reached the eye, then it quickly enters it and destroys the eye, and this happens many times.
Therefore, when you wish to cure this and extract it, then it is done only by observing its movement and appearance to the touch, and then it is necessary that you tightly bind that which is above it and that which is below it with a strong ligature and constriction, and then cauterize it and extract it.
And if it is deeply embedded in the flesh and you do not find it, then you apply a hot cautery to the place until it dies, and the greatest cause that is feared is its corruption, by which it corrupts the place as we have said.
But if you see it has reached the head near the eye, then bind above the eyebrow with a strong ligature, and then cauterize it, and extract it; and it is necessary that the patient use purification of his body with medicines evacuating bad putrid humors, and guard himself from foods generating putrefaction.
Concerning the incision for the illness nafir.
This illness is called nafir in our land, and it is a pain which comes in some limbs, and then is moved from limb to limb.
And I have already seen that, as I will tell you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I uncovered her arm and saw a small inflammation in the basilic vein of the arm, and after it had remained for an hour, I saw that inflammation moving like a worm climbing upwards, but more lightly than a snake, as if it were quicksilver when it runs from place to place.
And then the pain left that place and fixed itself in the muscle, and remained there for about an hour.
And then it ran to the other side until it reached the other arm.
And then I remembered that it was moving throughout her body, as I saw; and I was very surprised by its movement and the speed from one limb to another. And I had seen this before with my own eyes in that manner, however I have seen many who find pain shifting from limb to limb. But I did not see it with my eyes as clearly as in the woman.
And I considered that this was so because the woman was old, with a dry body and exposed veins, and in this way, that windy affliction was made apparent.
And it is necessary that it does not appear according to this retro-cure in those who are delicate and with moist bodies and hidden veins.
And if you want his cure, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not apparent to your eye as we have said, then you press lightly on it and dry it until that trapped wind is expelled, and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it with your eyes, then you treat it with rubbing of the body and with that which expels winds, and drive them away with foul-smelling pills and with pills of serapium and the like.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their types, and according to the places in the body where they fall, for their diversity is that some have large points, and small ones, and concave ones, and blunt ones, that is solid ones; and some that have three angles, and some that have barbs; and some that have feathers.
And of the signs of the brain, it is when an arrow falls into it and pierces the bone, and emerges from the skull which is over the brain, for there happens a sudden violent headache, and dimness of vision (scotoma), and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and inflammation and redness of the tongue, and spasm and confusion of mind, and vomiting of bile, and speech is quickly cut off and the voice is destroyed, and there issues from the place of the wound a white moisture resembling pus, and a discharge resembling watery flesh.
And therefore if these signs appear to you, then abstain from the cure and the extraction of the arrow, unless it is already outside.
But the signs of the arrow when it falls into the heart, are that it is near the left nipple, and feels as if it were stuck in something hard, and not in something empty; and very quickly the movement of the arrow is similar to the movement of the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and there is coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and syncope; and therefore know that death approaches without any doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung are thus:  frothy blood issues from the wound, and the vessels near the neck are inflamed, and the color of the patient is changed, and he breathes with great difficulty, and desires the intake of cool air.
And if the arrow falls into the diaphragm which is in the chest, the pain is then near the ribs, and the breath is drawn with violent pain and with sighing, and not all the muscles of the limbs are paralyzed.
And if the arrow falls into the stomach, then very quickly some undigested food comes from the wound.
And if the arrow falls into the abdomen, and is fixed there, there will be some discharge of pus from the wound, or the intestine, or bowel which is ruptured, and there is no hope of cure without extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow falls into the bladder, and urine flows out, and some matter proceeds from it to the external parts, and very quickly pain comes upon the patient, then know that it is mortal.
But the other members, such as the face, the neck, and the throat, and the shoulders and the armpits, and the vertebrae of the back, and the clavicle, and the hip, and the leg and similar members, are saved according to the best dispositions when the arrow does not strike a vein or artery and is not a poisoned arrow.
And I recount this to you that I have seen of the nature of these arrows, so that you may thereby signify your own cure.
Which is the reason why, in a man, an arrow fell into the lacrimal duct of his eye at the root of his nose, and I extracted it from him on the other side, below his ear, and he was healed, and no harm befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew which fell into him in the corner of his eye, below the lower eyelid, and the arrow was already hidden and I found nothing of it but a small point with which it adhered to its shaft, and it was a large arrow of the sharp arrows of the Turks, with an iron head in which there were no barbs.
And the Jew was healed, and no ill accident befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man which had fallen into his throat, and it was an Arabic arrow, and this is one which has barbs; and then I incised upon it between two guides, and it was already submerged in his throat; and then I worked subtly with it, and then I extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen into his belly, and I thought that he would die from it; and therefore, since thirty days had passed and no change had come upon him in his condition, I incised over the arrow and extracted it, and he was healed, and no ill accident befell him.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow fell into his back, and the wound had healed over him, and since it was almost seven years, the arrow emerged at the root of the thigh.
And I saw a woman in whom an arrow fell into her belly, and the wound is healed within her, and she is not changed in any of her conditions, and the woman finds no impediment in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man into whom an arrow fell in his face, and the wound is healed and the arrow broken, finding no great pain, and many similar to these.
And I extracted the arrow from the prince of the king's man that had fallen into the middle of his nose and had already declined a little to the right side, and the whole arrow was hidden.
And I was called to his cure about three days after the arrow's impact.
And then I found the wound from the arrow very narrow; and then I explored it with a slender probe, and did not feel it; but he felt a pricking under his right ear, and then I suspected that that pricking was from the end of the arrow.
And I plastered the place with a plaster which has attractive and digestive virtue, trusting that the place would suppurate so that the location of the arrow would appear to me, so that I could cut it out.
And no significant accident occurred in the place that the arrow had reached; and I continued with the plaster on him for many days and no accident occurred; and the wound consolidated meanwhile, and the patient rested from its extraction for many days until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and reported this to me.
And then I applied a sharp corrosive medicine to the wound for many days until it was opened, and with a probe I tested it, and found the subtle point of the arrow, which adhered to the shaft.
And then I adjusted the application of the sharp medicine to the wound until the point of the arrow appeared visibly to me, and I spent time with this for a period of up to four months.
And then, since the wound was enlarged, and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it, and remove it, and I did not succeed in this way, then I did not cease to refine and devise ways and means to do this, until I managed one day, using ingenious instruments, to grasp it with carefully-made forceps, as the form of these will appear at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it; and then I restored that wound.
And some physicians judged that the cartilage of the nose would not be restored, and I restored it, and the wound is consolidated, and the patient is healed with complete health, and he suffered no harm from it in any way.
And I recount to you the manner of extraction of certain arrows so that you may establish a rule and understanding of that which I have not mentioned, for the parts of this art and its definitions are not comprehended in a narrative, nor do the books contain them.
And skillful artisans compare the little with the much; and what is present with what is absent; and they find new operations and new instruments because of the difficulty of removing foreign bodies from the art when it happens.
Therefore I say that arrows are not removed from the limbs in which they are fixed except in two ways: either by traction from the place where they enter; or by counter-traction from the opposite side.
And that which is removed from the place where it entered, is an arrow embedded in fleshy tissue, and is pulled and removed.
And if it does not yield to extraction at the time of injury, then you must leave it for a few days until the flesh around it softens, and the extraction and its removal are eased.
And similarly, if it is embedded in bone and does not yield to extraction, then leave it for a few days and repeat the extraction and movement each day, and it will eventually be removed.
And if it still does not yield after a few days, then you must perforate the bone around the arrow on all sides with a thin drill until the area around the arrow is widened, and then extract it.
But if the arrow is hidden within the body and is beyond feeling, then you should probe for it with a probe, and if you feel it, then draw it out with instruments suitable for its extraction.
And if you cannot reach it because of the tightness of the wound and the length of the projectile, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in the way, then you should incise the wound to widen it so that it is possible to extract the arrow.
And if it is held fast by barbs, then you must free the flesh in which it is embedded from all sides with all the skill you can muster; and work on it, if you cannot manage its removal, by fracturing the barbs and twisting them until they yield.
And when you undertake the extraction of the arrow, wherever it may be, then use twisting with your hand and forceps on all sides until it yields; and proceed with great subtlety so that the arrow is not broken and that its extraction is not made difficult for you.
And if you cannot manage it at that time, then leave it for a few days until the parts surrounding it soften, and then try again, for then it will be easier. But if you encounter excessive bleeding, then apply the remedies we have described in the appropriate chapter.
And be careful to avoid cutting a vein, nerve, or tendon, and use all possible skill in removing the arrow; and do this with ease, calmness, slowness, and firmness, as I have recommended to you.
And therefore, position the patient so that the arrow is aligned with the wound, for this is more helpful. But if that is not possible, then apply what you can, according to the circumstances.
But if the arrows are protruding from the opposite side, or if any part of it protrudes from the wound, or if the arrowhead is bent by pressure from the part above the deep wound and sometimes protrudes, then cut it there; and let the incision be the same size as the forceps grasp; and then pull it out, for it will come out easily in this way.
But if it is lodged in the bone, then twist the blade against the arrow until it is embedded in the bone, and widen the opening, and then pull it out.
And if not, leave it for a few days, and then try again until it comes out.
And if the shaft of the arrow is still in the wound, then push it in further.
But if the shaft has fallen out and you want to apply pressure, then insert, if it is blunt, a concave instrument so that the concavity fits over the arrow's tail and then push it in.
But if the arrowhead is concave, then push it in with a strong instrument entering into it, for the arrow will be loosened by this.
And if the arrow is poisoned, then you must cut away all the flesh in which the poison is, if it is possible for you to do so, and then treat it with what is appropriate.
And if the arrow penetrates the foot, or the belly, or the bladder, or the side, and is close enough that you can touch it with a probe, and it is possible for you to cut it out, then cut it out and beware of cutting a vein or nerve, and extract it, and then sew the wound if necessary, and treat it until it is healed.
The form of forceps with which arrows are extracted:
Let the ends of them be like a bird's beak, which are made like a saw when they grip the arrow so that they do not let go of it.
And of these, many types are made, large, small, and medium-sized, according to the size of the arrow and its smallness, and the size of the wound, and the tightness of it.
like a ray, so that its entry into the concave arrow is eased and it is pushed with that.
Of phlebotomy of the veins.
The veins for phlebotomy, of which the customary number is thirty, of which sixteen are in the head; that is, two pulsating veins which are near the ears, called  *alhasisain* [likely a mis-spelling or corruption of a term needing further research]; two veins that are in the two apparent temples; and two veins which are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, which are called *aspicientz* [likely a misspelling or corruption]; and the vein prepared in the middle of the forehead, and the vein which is at the end of the nose; and two *guidegi* [unclear, needs further investigation] which are at the neck; and two veins which are in the upper lip; and two veins which are in the lower lip; and — these are the veins which are called *algelic* [likely a misspelling or corruption] —; and there are two veins which are under the tongue. But the veins which are phlebotomized in the arms are five; for of those there is one cephalic, and it is beside the wild vein; and the people call it “the vein of the head”, and it is a thick vein, and it is composed of a branch of the basilic, and of another branch of the cephalic; and the people name that one “vein of the whole body”, and the basilic is the vein which is located in the inner arm, and is named “citillar”, and the common people name that one “vein of the belly”; and the *funis* of the arm, and it is located above the *focil* [likely referring to a specific anatomical location, needs further investigation], and it is that in which phlebotomy is performed, and it is that which appears above the pulse by manifest appearance; and *salvatella*, and it is the vein which is between the index finger and the little finger, and there are two branches in it.
And in the foot and the leg there are three veins; one of those is that which is below the curvature of the knee on the outer side of the leg; and the second is the saphena, and its location is below the leg on the inner side of the leg; and the sciatic vein, and its location is below the leg on the outer side of the leg; and in the leg there are other veins similar to those.
The opening of the veins which are near the ears is for chronic cataracts and migraine and *sahafati* [unclear, needs further investigation] and old, chronic ulcers of the head.
And the manner of bleeding those is as I have recounted to you: it is that the head of the patient should be shaved, and the head of the patient should be rubbed at the occiput in the location of both veins with a wide cloth with good friction; then the patient should tighten his neck with a bandage until the two veins and their locations appear near the ears in two depressed areas of the head; and seek those with your finger, and if you feel the pulse of those with your finger, then bleed with a lancet.
Then you take the lancet knife, and it is that which is incisive, and then you introduce that under the vein in the elbow until the blood comes out. Then raise your hand with the vein and the elbow to the upper parts and incise the vein with an incisive incision. And let the length of the incision be the amount of two fingers joined, or thereabouts; and let blood come out as much as you wish.
Then bind it with powders and leave it until it is healed.
And already and previously in the beginning of the book, the incision of these and their cauterization.  (A more natural English phrasing might be: "The incision and cauterization of these were already described at the beginning of this book.")
But from the two arteries that are at the temples, the benefit of phlebotomy is for chronic migraine and sudden severe pain, and persistent ophthalmia, and a flow of acute superfluities effused into the eyes.
And the manner of phlebotomizing these is as I have recounted to you: constrict the patient's neck very tightly until the two veins appear to the touch by manifest appearance and their pulse is demonstrated beneath your finger; and then you incise with a lancet.
Then you raise the skin above the vein in the upper parts with your index finger, and insert the phlebotomy incision underneath, and raise the vein upwards; and incise it just as you have done in the other two veins; and let blood flow according to your necessity.
And then loosen the constriction of the patient, and place your finger on the vein for an hour, and then place cotton and powder on it and bind it above with strong compression, and leave it until it is healed.
And now the remembrance of them, and their incision, and the drawing [of blood] has been preceded in the beginning of the book.
Therefore, the benefit of phlebotomy of the frontal vein is for phlebotomy of the cephalic vein in ancient diseases of the face and for migraine, rigidity, and foul ulcers.
And the manner of his phlebotomy is as I recount to you; that is, that the patient constrict his neck with a bandage until the vein appears; and then take the instrument which is named a lancet, of which this is the form:
Place the pointed end, which is at the head of the lancet, upon that vein and strike it with a hammer or other similar thing in its manner, and let the blood flow according to the quantity you wish; and then loosen the constriction of the patient and bind it until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a lateral phlebotomy, however it is not fitting that the phlebotomy be of an acute extremity like the other phlebotomies, rather it should be of a broad extremity, and phlebotomize it according to the transverse, because the bone is near and would easily break the phlebotomy when it is subtle [thin].
But the benefit of the two veins that are at the lacrimal ducts of the two eyes is for scabies of the eyes, for redness and swelling in the eyes, and for diseases of the face.
And the manner of the phlebotomy is that the patient constricts his neck with a bandage, and then phlebotomize those veins, and you should be standing over his head.
And let the phlebotomy be made transversely, a little lengthwise with a small phlebotomy instrument, because in that place there is no flesh; and if the phlebotomy instrument is thin, its point is easily broken.
And then make the blood flow as much as is necessary for you, and place cotton on those [wounds], and bind them for a night, and then unbind them.
But phlebotomy of the vein of the nose is beneficial for acute fever and violent headache, and for diseases of the face and migraine and redness that occurs in the face, especially when it is chronic.

And the veins that are phlebotomized in the neck are those which run customarily in all men, and their phlebotomy is done in two ways: either they are opened with a myrtle or olive-shaped phlebotomy instrument of thinning design; or they are cut with a lancet-shaped phlebotomy instrument, and it is called an alnessil, and this is its form:
Let its width be thus as you see, and it is suitable for opening concave, full, prominent, thick veins, and those that contain thick, turbid blood.
And this phlebotomy instrument is of lesser width and of a more subtle extremity, and is suitable for phlebotomy of thin veins that contain thin, clear blood.
But the benefit of phlebotomy of the basilic vein, which is one of three veins: because it draws out the blood of illnesses that are below the throat and neck, and of that which affects the feet and abdomen.

(The last part is a bit ambiguous and might mean "before the vein is cut open with a lancet.")

And if you see the blood flowing from the phlebotomy like the urine of a child, and the blood is thin and red, then know that it is from the artery; and then you should quickly place your finger over it for a long time; and then remove your finger, and if the blood flow is stopped, for many times it is stopped, then you loosen the arm and leave it.

And then if the blood is stopped, it is good; and if not, then treat it with that which has been previously mentioned in the recollection of the place of the powders that stop the flow of blood.

And this nerve often appears to the senses; and if it is hidden from some men and is subtle and is not shown, then it behooves you to perform the phlebotomy with which you perform phlebotomy with a slight incision, and avoid the nerve with your skill.

The first of these is that you should know that phlebotomy is administered only for the preservation of health and for its hope and caution against the onset of illnesses, and that phlebotomy should be performed in one of the veins that are in the elbow, that is: cephalic, median cubital, and basilic; and that phlebotomy should be performed at the beginning of spring, on an empty stomach in spring and autumn, and in winter near mealtime, when signs of repletion appear.
And let phlebotomy be performed on the third day or on the first, after three hours have passed since daybreak.
But phlebotomy, which is administered when it provokes suitability and necessity for it, may be done at night or day, at any hour and at any time.
And it is necessary that a child should not be phlebotomized until he is fourteen years old, and a pregnant woman should not be phlebotomized except from the fourth month until the seventh, and old women who have passed sixty years should not be phlebotomized.
And if someone wishes to be phlebotomized in any way, it is necessary that he cleanse his bowels before phlebotomy with a cleansing enema, if he has much retained waste in them, so that the veins do not draw the superfluities from the bowels into the phlebotomy, which are putrid, and that they may not harm the principal members.
And let not patients with nausea and diarrhea be phlebotomized, nor those recovering from illness or fever until this is resolved, nor those with dropsy; and moreover, phlebotomy should be avoided near a colic attack, near vomiting, and near intestinal problems, and near excessive intercourse and labor and exercise, fasting, or vigils, and every cause that dissolves strength, be it bodily or vital.
And consider beforehand the thinning of humors if the blood is thick, with foods and drinks, and with medicines if it is possible for him.
And then enter a bath if no cause prohibits it, or be exercised by some activity so that the blood may be thinned.
And perform his phlebotomy in the middle of the day, that is, around the third hour, as we have said.
And ensure that the patient's feet are protected that day from all harmful accidents, both physical, such as anguish, anger, fear, and all bodily accidents, such as excessive labor and exertion, sexual intercourse, and things similar.
And let there be present at his bedside things whose use is customary among men, with aromatics and perfumes, and with balms and similar things, according to what is possible.

And let the constriction be moderate, for if the constriction is uneven, or if the constriction is excessive, then it prevents the flow of blood; or if it is too loose, then it prevents the free flow of blood.
And before the constriction, it is necessary that the one who is to be phlebotomized rub his hands together until the veins are swollen and clearly visible.
Then the phlebotomist should anoint his fleam with a little old oil; and then he should place the index finger of his left hand on the vein, below the spot where he wants to perform the phlebotomy, so that the vein moves slightly, and the percussion should cease because those veins which are found thus like a fleeing string should be avoided for phlebotomy, and those that are full of air, when you place the fleam on them, contract from the fleam, and the phlebotomist is deceived; and the fleam does not open the vein, and if it opens, it is tightly closed.
Therefore, the phlebotomist must be skilled and attentive to all these things.
Then apply the fleam, but if the vein is opened at once, it is good; and if not, repeat the process in another place a little below or above, with speed, if the area is not inflamed.

And then repeat the phlebotomy if necessary.
And if the opening is small and the blood flows slowly, and you fear that the desired quantity of blood will not be drawn, then repeat the phlebotomy in that opening, following its straightness and widening the opening slightly, and do this quickly before the area becomes inflamed, because in many men the area becomes inflamed from a small opening.
But if you see that it is already inflamed, then do not repeat the procedure there, for it will not help; and place on it some olive oil, for it relieves the body of blood. And olive oil is better in this instance than other oils.
Similarly, administer olive oil in all phlebotomies with which you bleed veins to prevent blood clots, and they make this with theriac and sagapenum when one of these is placed on the area, because the blood is thinned and is resolved when it is thick.
And if a large abscess is generated at the site of the phlebotomy, which happens frequently, and especially in one who has been bled only once, or through a small opening in the vein, then take care and place on the area a sponge soaked in slightly warmed water and salt, and compress it for an hour, for it will resolve it.
And it is necessary to do this immediately after the bleeding from that vein, or from another vein.
And if some blackness or greenness remains in the place for some days, it does not matter; and if you wish, place on it some aloe and myrrh dissolved, or some mint juice or calamint or something similar.
And abscesses and swellings often occur after phlebotomy of the basilica vein.
And then place your hand on it, and if you then find softness under your compression, then that swelling is malignant and bad; and then be careful to place on it some of what we have said, for blood will very quickly flow from the artery.
And make a plaster with something that has styptic properties, so that the place is hardened, and then treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
And it is necessary to extract blood from everyone according to the quantity of their strength and according to the color predominating in the blood as it appears.
For if the blood is black, then you let it flow until it is red; and similarly, if you see it thick, then you let it flow until it is thinned; and similarly, if it is sharp (or acrid), until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for one who is full and strong and needs bloodletting at once, that the phlebotomy of the vein be widened and the phlebotomy be wide.
And in one who is weak, the opposite should be done, and it is necessary to extract [blood] in several times, and the opening should be narrow.
And the best way to administer phlebotomy is that the section be transverse, not a puncture.
And this manner of phlebotomy is safe from excessive bleeding and injury to nerves, and is more praiseworthy and safer than phlebotomy according to latitude and longitude (i.e., a lengthwise incision).
And to the one who is accustomed to fainting during phlebotomy, you should treat them before the phlebotomy with a little bread infused in pomegranate wine or warmed simple oxymel; and extract the blood from them in three or four times.
And if there is coldness of complexion, then before phlebotomy take bread infused in syrup of myrrh or in honey syrup flavored with spices such as pepper, cinnamon, ginger, and the like, or in a fragrant and very aromatic syrup.
And if syncope occurs during phlebotomy, and it is caused by the loss of much blood, then they should drink meat broth and a very subtly fragrant syrup; and use aromatherapy with rosewater, and let their feet be bathed with it.
And use the other remedies that we have mentioned in the section on syncope due to evacuation (in the chapter on syncope).
And whoever wants to bleed their arm and dissolve their blood a second time, it is necessary for that person, for whom phlebotomy is for the purpose of evacuation and whose strength is weak, that they evacuate blood little by little, that is, according to the amount of strength they have, on continuous days.
But whoever wants to bleed their arm and shed their blood a second time, and their body is strong, then let them do so about seven or nine hours after the first phlebotomy.
But whoever wants to draw blood from their body, contrary to the side to which the illness inclines, should be bled on the second or third day.
But that body in which there is much already heated blood, and it is acute and causes fever, should have blood drawn from it at once, and a large quantity should be drawn, and the opening should be widened until syncope occurs, provided you have observed all the conditions of phlebotomy; and that you place your hand on their pulse below the heart, so that death does not result instead of syncope, for this often happens when the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligent.
And it is not advisable, when you want to untie the arm and evacuate blood a second time, that the opening of the vein is already closed and the exit of blood is difficult, that you press on it with vehemence and the arm is bent slightly, because that is very bad.
Having left it until it is bled a second time; or remove it with the sharpness of the phlebotomy tool that is coagulated with blood in the opening of the vein; or place on it theriac, alfarach, or sage, and compress it with light pressure until the blood has flowed out.
And if the vein is inflamed, then leave it and do not touch it until the inflammation has subsided.
And if necessity compels you to evacuate blood a second time, and there is no excuse, then you bleed it in that place or in the other arm in another vein.
And the vein called "rope of the arm" is phlebotomized instead of the cephalic or basilic, when they are not found or are hidden, because it is composed of those.
And the quality of his phlebotomy is that the patient immerses his hand in warm water until the vein reddens and appears clearly.
Then it should be tightened with a bandage with moderate constriction; and then phlebotomize according to the small transversal, not latitudinally nor longitudinally.
And let the opening be wide, and your phlebotomy with which you phlebotomize it should be a little above the joint of the hand.
And if the flow of blood is impeded, then immerse the hand again in warm water and let the blood flow into the water until you achieve your need.
But if you are in the summer days, then you are excused from re-immersion of the hand in warm water, and the measure is to place the bowl of blood in warm water in winter time.
And phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all other veins, because beneath it there is no pulsating vein nor nerve.

Therefore, phlebotomize the largest branch below the leg, below the ankle, because it is better and healthier.

And it is often very hidden in many men; and if you find it and it doesn't appear to the touch at all, then phlebotomize one of its branches, and that is the one that appears on the back of the foot between the little toe and the ring finger.
And beware of the nerves, and let the amount of blood flow that you wish.
And then loosen the bandage and place cotton over the phlebotomy site and bind the place, for it heals easily.
Concerning cupping and the qualities of using them.
Cups are made of horn, or of brass, or of glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one of them is with scarification and blood extraction, and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping which is without scarification is done in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And for cups that are administered with scarification and blood extraction, there are fourteen places on the body, of which one in which cups are placed is the nape of the neck, and it is the back part of the head; and the middle place of the two sides of the neck together; and cups on the chin below the jaw, below the mouth; and cups on the two shoulder blades; and cups on the loins, and this is above the end of the hip bone; and cups on the two flanks, and these are on the two sides; and cups on the two hips; and cups on the two thighs.
And cupping does not extract blood except from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore it does not deplete the strength through them, as is the case with phlebotomy; nor should cupping be done with fire or without fire in any disease caused by repletion until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity calls for cupping because of illness or a chronic condition, administer it at any time, at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, in the middle of it, and at any time.
Therefore, when you see any of these conditions, especially if it is in the third or middle part of the month, then we command that cupping be done after two or three hours of the day have passed.
And the benefit of cupping the nape of the neck is that when it is applied, it relieves the heaviness in the head and that which is settled in the eyes; but it is necessary that this be in addition to the evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in the place of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.
And it is not permitted to use it on one who is of cold complexion, or in whom there is catarrh, for it causes great harm to such a person.
And for this reason, it is not fitting to use it on the elderly, and on those whose heads suffer from cold illnesses.
But to the one on whom this is continuously applied, oblivion (fainting) is caused; and for that reason, you should command the cupper to place his hand a little with the cupping glass towards the lower parts, to prevent the production of oblivion (fainting).
But cupping of the elbow is in the place of phlebotomy of the vena fusca and the basilica vein: and for that reason it benefits asthma, and the constriction of the throat, and the splitting of the throat's instrument (possibly referring to a split or damaged uvula/vocal chords), and cough, and repletion (congestion).
And it is necessary that the cupping glass at the elbow be slightly raised, for if it is placed lower down, it produces weakness in the body and in the stomach.
And cupping of the temple benefits accidental pains in the head and ophthalmia, and migraine, and suffocation, and pains in the roots of the teeth; and it is in place of phlebotomy of the basilica vein.
And it is necessary that you command the cupper that he not press his hand with scarification lest he incise the artery and cause a flow of blood.
And cupping below the chin benefits ulcers in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar to those diseases that are in the mouth, and it stands in place of phlebotomy of the veins that are in the two lips.
But cupping that is done on the two shoulder blades benefits catarrh, which is caused by an excess of heat.
And cupping of the two inner parts of the ankles benefits in the same way as phlebotomy of three veins, that is, of the basilica, mediana (or possibly 'fusca', which could mean dark or obscure), and cephalic veins, because it draws blood from all those smaller veins that are in the flesh, and those small veins from the other larger veins until the drawing reaches the three named veins.
And command the cupper that he not proceed excessively in scarification, because the place is devoid of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
And there is a cupping-glass with which one cups on the hips and it benefits haemorrhoids of the rectum and deeper ulcers.
And it is necessary that you command the cupper that it be a large cupping-glass, and that it be of brass, because the place requires strong suction; and quickly are thin cupping-glasses broken and it should be scarified with scarification.
But cupping of the legs diminishes pain by evident lessening, because it draws blood from the whole body, and it benefits old pains of the joints and of the womb, and of the bladder; and it provokes menstruation, and benefits swelling and dropsy; and it stands in place of phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins, and of the two jugular veins (or possibly "cavilhas" refers to other veins in the leg).
However, they greatly weaken the whole body and cause syncope in many men.
And of the jugular cupping, the benefit is similar to the benefit of cupping of the two legs.
And the quality of the application of the cupping glasses is that you place the cupping glass initially empty and draw by moderate suction, and do not prolong the application of the cupping glasses, but apply them lightly, and remove them carefully, so that the humors come to the place by equal distribution. And do not cease to iterate this and continue until you see the place redden and swell and the drawing of blood appear.
And then you scarify and iterate the suction little by little, and consider the condition of the body.
Because that man who is of tender flesh and rare pores, it is fitting that you scarify him once and no more, lest the place be ulcerated.
And command the cupper to amplify the scarification and make a small cut, and moderate the suction with ease.
And if the blood is thick, then it is necessary to scarify twice, for in the first time the way of thinning is opened to the blood and its watery parts. But in the second, to the scrutiny acquired by the extraction of the thick blood.
And if the blood is very foul, then let scarification be done a third time so that it achieves the desired effect.
Finally, when we want to extract the blood little by little or a small amount, one scarification is enough for us.
And if we want extraction of much blood, we make many scarifications; and if we know that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that we scarify with deep scarifications.
And the term scarification refers only to the depth of the incision.
And if the skin of the one to be treated is thick, hard, dark, and its pores are constricted, the sites for cupping should be anointed with softening, opening, and resolving ointments; if it is summer, such as aloe oil, violet oil, sweet almond oil, or poppy seed oil; and if it is winter, then narcissus oil, lily oil, chamomile oil, or blood oil, and similar things.
And if the excess [humor] is thick and cold, then let the oil be amaranth oil, or balm oil, or aloe oil, or anise oil, and similar things.
But if the patient has large pores and tender flesh, then the use of oil should be avoided, and for such a person, the cupping sites should be washed with vinegar or cold water of nightshade, or with poppy water, or with bordelais water, and similar things.
And for one whose blood is excessively moist, wash the cupping sites with sharp vinegar, or with myrtle and sumac water, and similar things.
And for one whose humors are thick, wash the cupping sites with old wine, or with water of sansevieria (possibly a mis-identification, likely a plant with similar properties), which is marjoram, or with a decoction of anise, or of chamomile, and similar things.
And the cupping should be done before the bath; rather, it should be administered about an hour or two before the bath, and the patient should sleep after the cupping.
What is it that the person to be cupped or bled should observe before cupping?
It is necessary to consider at the beginning: if the person to be cupped or bled is choleric and has sharp, inflamed blood, then they should take cooling things, such as sour pomegranates, endive in sharp vinegar, lettuce, and dried borage, and julep, and things like them.
And give them foods such as chicken, and the meat of domestic birds, and foods seasoned with acids, and similar things.
And for someone whose complexion is cold, they should drink syrup of almonds, or syrup of dried borage seed, and sumac, and very fragrant wine (between vintage and Christmas); and they should be advised to eat and drink sparingly, and give them foods such as chicken, hempseed, peas, small birds, and doves.
And it is necessary that on the day of cupping and bloodletting, their drink should be greater than their food.
And it is necessary that at certain times, some men be given theriac alpharac, or medicines of musk, that is, nutmeg, or celadine before cupping or bloodletting, or afterwards, so that the principal members may be comforted and the blood thinned; and they should not take those things when they are heated.
But the cuppings that are without scarification should be applied to the liver, and over the spleen and gout, and over the navel, in the places of the knees, and the groin, because these members do not tolerate scarification on them.
And it is not desired for these (cuppings) except the drawing of blood from a limb, just as we place a cupping glass on the breast in the illness of a nasal hemorrhage; and we use this so that cold, viscous windiness of the limb may be dissolved, just as we place it on the abdomen and navel; for this rarefies the limb and warms it and reduces pain because it resolves windiness.
And it is placed on the tonsils when they suffer from obstruction or swelling, for by the virtue of strong attraction it quickly opens the obstruction and eradicates the swelling from its place.
And similarly it acts when it is placed on the liver and spleen because of windiness enclosed within them.
And these cuppings are administered empty with suction only; and sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of lukewarm water in illnesses of the spleen.
And this is so that the cupping glass is filled and is larger, that is, with hot water alone or water in which herbs suitable for this purpose have been boiled.
Method of cupping administered with fire: let the width of the opening be two fingers, according to how we have formed it; and the length of the handle be half a palm; and let it be on the side, in the place that is in the circumference, half of it, the hole should be small enough for a needle to enter it.
Let it be made of bronze, copper, or citrus wood, with thick edges, smooth, even, and polished so that it does not injure the limb upon its placement.
And in the middle of it, let there be a transverse bar of bronze or iron, where a candle with fire is placed.
And let this cupping glass be larger than the one we have described; and the smaller one smaller than that, according to the illness and the age of the one who uses it.
And the cupping glasses for infants and thin people are different from the cupping glasses for young people and those with large bodies.
And the way to place this cupping glass with fire on the limb is to extend the wick with a suitably sized wood fire in front of a small wax candle, and place this on the middle of the transverse bar in the middle of the cupping glass, so that the fire is lit around the lower part, so that the patient's body is not burned.
Then let it be placed on the patient's limb, and the finger on the hole we have mentioned, until, when we hold the cupping glasses and do not need to remove the finger and let the vapor escape through that hole, then the cupping glass will immediately be detached.
And then ignite the wick according to its method, and repeat if you need to.
But in cupping, which is administered in the illness of pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse bar or hole, and it is not administered unless it is filled with water, and it is placed on the limb alone.
And this is its form: And this cupping glass, the larger it is, the better, because it can receive a greater quantity of water.
Chapter 97.
Leeches are not administered to many limbs, but only to those limbs where the placement of cupping glasses is not possible, because of their small size, such as the ear and gums and similar; or on a member devoid of flesh, such as a finger and nose and similar.
And the quality of using those is that you should find leeches in clean, fresh waters of stagnant pools.
And then let them be left day and night in fresh water until they are hungry, and so that no substance remains in their bellies; and then let the body be evacuated at the beginning with phlebotomy and cupping; and then rub the patient's limb until it reddens; and then they should be placed upon it.
And when they are full and fall off.
And if it is possible to draw blood from the area with a cupping glass, it is more beneficial afterwards.
And if not, then wash the area with vinegar and then with warm water; and let it be rubbed and squeezed.
And if the flow of blood is prolonged near the application of the leeches, and it is oozing, then dip a cloth in cold water, and place that on top until the oozing stops.
And if the bleeding is profuse, then sprinkle on it crushed vitriol, or gallnuts and similar styptic substances, until the bleeding stops; or let halves of cooked fava beans be placed on the area, and left there until the beans stick to the place, and the bleeding is stopped.
And it is fitting, if necessary, to iterate the leeches, when it is possible to change those.
