And if the leeches are prevented from attaching, then rub the area with fresh blood or prick the area with a needle until blood comes out; and then let them be applied, because when they sense any blood they will immediately attach themselves.
And when you want them to fall off, sprinkle a little crushed aloes, or salt, or ashes, on them, and they will fall off immediately.
Thus begins the prologue of the third part: On Restoration. This chapter is very often necessary in the art of medicine; and it is the restoration of fractures and accidental dislocations in the bones.
Oh, son, know that foolish and vulgar physicians, and those who never looked at the books of the Ancients or read a single letter of them, boast of this chapter.
And for this reason, this skill of the learned in our land is kept private; and I have never forgotten this at all; and I have not added what I learned except through long reading of the books of the Ancients and study on their understanding, until I extracted the science from them.
And then I applied it to experiment and study throughout my life.
And I have already described in this chapter everything that my knowledge contains and on which I have experimented; since I have abbreviated it for you and freed it from lengthy occupation; and I have abbreviated it to the point of brevity; and I have shown it clearly; and I have illustrated it with many forms of instruments that are used in it, as is done by way of explanation, as I did in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter one.
Summaries and collections on the subject of bone fractures, those which are necessary to be stated before we begin the discussion of broken limbs and their various types. Therefore, I will state in the prologue of this chapter the summaries of the discourse; and the chapters in understanding which, at the beginning, the artisan and practitioner must focus on their truth; that is, you and those who are quick to learn or teach this noble art, different from you.
Therefore I say that when a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall happens to someone, then you should initially consider phlebotomy or bloodletting, or both together, unless some cause prohibits it, such as weakness, or the person injured is a child, or an elderly person in decrepitude, or it is a time of extreme heat or extreme cold.
And furthermore, let the patient be satisfied with a diet of cold herbs and the flesh of birds and rabbits; and we prohibit fatty meats and overfilling with food and any food that fills the veins with blood, until you are certain of a hot abscess, and if you do not fear effusion of matter into the limb.
And then return the patient to their former regimen, to which they are accustomed.
And so, as the broken bone begins to be restored, then the patient should be fed nutritious foods, substantial and heavy foods with viscosity, such as foods made from wheat cooked in water, and rice, and heads [possibly referring to specific cuts of meat], and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish, and thick wine and similar things; and with this regimen, the consolidation of the fracture is made more easily and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in the very young and in the elderly, cannot be rejoined and consolidated according to their nature because of the dryness of their limbs.
However, nature adapts and applies to the broken bone from all sides some substance similar to glue, in which there is thickness that adheres to and binds it until it is completely conjoined and bound together, until it attains strength and firmness, like fire at the beginning, until no cause prevents it from its work.
And for this reason it is necessary that you give the patient foods in which there is weight, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the bone of the foot is different from a fracture of the bone of the arm; and similarly all the other limbs are different from one another.
And I come to the remembrance of every type of fracture, explained in its own chapter distinct from the others.
There is great diversity of fractures in themselves. And the types of bone fractures are diversified in themselves; for there is an arched fracture, different from that which happens in the stars; and sometimes a fracture is made according to the length of the bone: and these are the fractures of the shaft and splinters and additions, proceeding and not-proceeding, as is done in the quill when it is prepared for writing, and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption at the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And for each type there is a proper device or treatment in its restoration, as will be remembered distinctly in its place.
Many are the signs of bone fracture. And thus a bone fracture is known: by its crookedness, and by the prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and by it when we compress it with the hand.
And therefore, when there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor is it felt by your touch when you touch the bone with agitation, and the patient does not find great pain, then there is no fracture here.
Perhaps there is a crookedness or small fissure; then you should not move it with your hand and with total compression; rather, place on it the medicines of which remembrance will be found near that moment, which is appropriate for the location; and then let it be tightened by a subtle bandage.
And know that if the place is curved and not split in two, or if nothing else happens to it that is noticeable, as if the entire part were removed from its comparison, then you must begin your work at the rectification of it before an abscess happens to it.
And if a hot abscess happens to it, then leave it for some days until the abscess ceases; and then dress it according to whatever method you can, lightly and skillfully.
And know that the restoration and healing of it is easier than of a bone in which a noticeable injury has occurred.
And then tighten it as the healing progresses.
But if there is injury to the bone, then extension of that broken limb is necessary in two parts, if it is the hand or foot; or with your hand if the limb is small; or with two cords or with one cord and your hand.
And be in a position where you place the limb in a dressing according to its natural shape, while the broken parts of the limb are extended; then you must force those additions back into place with skill and in whatever way you can.
And take care, according to your strength, that you do not cause pain or injury to the patient through your operation; and force yourself, to the best of your ability, to apply one bone to its fellow, according to their best alignment.
And it is necessary that at that time you profitably touch that with your hand, and if you see any abnormality there, rectify it and adjust it to the extent of your ability.
And avoid vehement extension and compression, as many foolish physicians do, who often cause hot abscesses through their operation, or cause contraction in the limb, as we have seen them do many times.
And the quality of the binding of the calmed limb is that which I recommend; so that you know that broken limbs are diversified in their smallness and in their largeness and in their form.
And for those that are small, such as the arm and the finger and the wrist and similar to them, then the bandage should be of very fine soft cloth.
But for those that are large, such as the thigh or the leg, the bandage should be strong and firm, because a firm bandage connects the large limb and binds it on all sides with an even binding, so that no blemish enters it.
Then begin, after your reduction, to place a bandage suitable for this, over the place of the fracture, with three or four layers according to what the limb requires; and tighten your hand a little with the bandage.
And then come with that to the highest part of the fracture site, and tighten less than you did your binding with which you bound the fracture site. And then extend the bandage from the fracture site a little, and soften the binding little by little, until you have covered a large part of the area.
And then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken place with a bandage, and then bring the bandages to the lower part of the fracture; and let your action in the tightening of the bandage and in the softening of it be as we have said in the first bandage above.
And then place between the layers of bandage some lion's mane tow or cloth, which will equalize the crookedness of the fracture, if there is crookedness; and if not, do not put anything there.
And then place another bandage over it, and then apply properly made splints over these bandages immediately, if there is no inflammation or hot abscess in the area, and then apply something that dries the abscess and reduces the inflammation; and leave that for a few days; and then you tighten the splints over it.
And let the splints be half the width of a shaved cane, and well and wisely shaped; or let them be of pine wood; or of palm branches; and let the width of each splint be according to this form and this figure:
However, it is necessary that the splint that is placed over the fracture be somewhat thicker and wider than the other splints, and the length of the splint according to the length of the limb in size and smallness; and then tighten over the splints with another bandage according to your first tightening; and then bind over it with a suitable thread according to what we have said about tightening, and that is that your tightening should be greater in the area of the fracture, and the farther away from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And it is necessary that the threads be moderate in thickness and fineness, and be of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen in the practice of some fools putting their threads of twisted rope, it is a great error, when the tightening loses its evenness.
And similarly, very fine threads are not suitable, because you will not achieve with them what you want by tightening, and there should not be less than one finger's width between splint and splint.
And if the patient is irritated by the ends of the splints near the binding in those places, then place under those splints some carded flax or wool so that it does not cause him injury.
Sometimes a fracture is accompanied by a wound, and a disruption occurs in the bone, and I will return to that in the following chapter according to its specific nature.
And know that not every broken limb is splinted on the first day; the reason is that, when the limb is large, you should not apply splints until about five or seven days, or more, depending on your assessment of the risk of a hot abscess developing.
And this is the manner of the plasters with which the Ancients made restorations; these are placed on fractures, dislocations, and sprains.
A method of common plaster, a shortened version for the restoration of fractures, suitable for many constitutions and especially for women and children, because it is something which neither heat nor cold affects:  it is to take the powder of the mill, which is the fine flour dust that settles on the walls of the mill from the movement of the millstones.
And prepare it thus, except that you sieve it with egg white, and make the resulting mixture neither thick nor thin; and then use it.
Another method of plaster for restoring fractures, dislocations, and sprains: take of asafoetida, of ladanum, of acacia, of elecampane, and of wild pomegranate root, of each one drachm; of myrrh and aloes, five drachms; of aloes, twenty drachms; all should be crushed and sifted and mixed with  aloes water or with egg white if the patient's constitution is hot; and then use this plaster, for it is of good composition and restores lightly broken bones, and it benefits many people.
Another method of plaster which benefits fractures and sprains: take of wild pomegranate root and of marshmallow root, ten drachms; of myrrh and of aloes, five drachms; of acacia, six drachms; of Armenian bole, twenty drachms.
All should be crushed in a mortar with great care, and sifted, and mixed with water or with egg white and be administered.
A method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places, and if pain occurs in those and in fissures and fractures: let soiled wool be taken and submerged in sour wine and boiled oil and be placed on the affected area.
But in this plaster there is no restorative virtue; it rather draws out hot abscesses and expels pain.
Another method of plaster that restores a broken bone: take leaves of the wild fig and leaves of wild poppy; pound everything and make a plaster of them.
A shortened method of plaster administered for the restoration of a bone fracture and when you desire resolution of the remainder of an abscess: take roots of marshmallow, of chamomile, and violet flowers, of barley flour, of all equal parts; let them all be crushed and mixed with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, then mix it with moist coriander water or with water and use it.
Another method of plaster, stronger in resolution than the first, when a hard abscess occurs during the restoration of a limb: take roots of marshmallow and linseed, and pomegranate, and melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, all in equal parts; let them all be crushed and mixed with willow water or with fresh water or with wine, all this according to the quality of the limb and the restoration of its color.
However, Hippocrates did not say in his book that it should be placed on the broken limb during its restoration except a cerate made of wax and oil and nothing else; and he recommended that it be of medium consistency between thick and thin.
But in Wales it is seen that they are placed on the broken limb during its restoration, and the things which have drying properties with a little heat are like myrrh, incense, and similar to those things that we have mentioned.
Method for a plaster that helps with injury and pain: take millet, wax, shaved human hair or bird down, and marshmallow, and salt, in equal parts; let them be crushed and sifted and mixed, and let a plaster be made.
But the amount of time the bandage should remain and then be loosened is that you should consider whether pain or itching has occurred, or if the bone is broken or dislocated; then you should not loosen it for many days. And if vehement pain, itching, or inflammation occurs at the site, then you should carefully loosen or remove it at that time, and remove the plaster from it.
And take linen cloth and a moist sea sponge, and immerse it in lukewarm water, and with that wash the area until that itching ceases and the pain is calmed; and then let the limb rest for an hour; and then place on it clean wool soaked in sour wine and oil or rose oil, and bind it on for a night until you are sure of a hot abscess, and that the inflammation of the limb has subsided, and its pain is lessened.
And then repeat that subtle binding and small plaster and do not tighten it as much as your first binding, and refine it with that until it is healed.
And if you see that the abscess, redness, pain, inflammation, and all the symptoms have subsided completely, and there is no need for plaster and binding, then you should repeat it as you did at the beginning, equally.
And if none of the conditions we have mentioned occur in the limb, then do not unbind it before three or four or five or seven days; and sometimes all this is left for twenty days, depending on what appears to you from unbinding the limb, as we have said, until the fracture adheres, and approaches the coagulation of the flesh upon it.
And then adjust the binding more than in your first binding, and adjust it to the thickening of the patient's flesh, as his recovery progresses.
But if you see the fracture site drying and thinning more than is appropriate, then you should know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it; and therefore, bathe it with lukewarm water every time you unbind it; and let this be done every three days; and loosen the binding a little, and by this operation, nourishment will reach the limb, and it will heal easily.
But what foolish restorers of fractured limbs do, when it is not set correctly at the beginning, and is set crookedly, is an error according to their practice, and a great danger; and if it were correct, the Ancients would have mentioned it in their books and would have worked with it.
And I have not seen anyone do this, wherefore it is more reasonable not to do it.
Chapter Two: On fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their forms are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are caused by a blow from a sword, and the blow shatters the entire bone into pieces, which are below the bone, just as happens in wood, and because of this, this type is called *ascial* (or axial); or the sword cuts a part of the bone, and cuts the flesh but the cut does not go through to the back; and this type of fracture is called *levatio absoluta* (complete elevation or perhaps a clean cut).
And the wound of these two fractures is either large or small; and there is a fracture that is a crushing or grinding; and its cause is a blow with a blunt object or a fall on a rock, and similar things.
And this fracture is either penetrating, almost reaching the membrane which is below the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the wound of these two fractures is either large or small.
And there is a fracture that is hidden in the bone, as small as the thickness of a hair, and because of this, this type is called a hairline fracture.
And there is a fracture that is caused by a fall or by a blow from a stone and the like, which causes some lamina of the bone to enter the inner parts, and it is made in a concave place, just as happens to the rims of helmets when they receive a blow and a part of them is driven inwards.
And this happens mostly in the bodies of moist bones, such as the heads of infants.
And in all these types of comminuted fractures, the pieces are like separated stars. And I will return everything to its place with repositioning.
And we recognize all these types of fractures by probing them and by investigating them with probes, and by the presence of corrupted flesh which is over them.
And a hairline fracture is known because it is uncovered on the bone, and is wiped clean, and then examined with a probe, because the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of a fracture, consider first the patient's symptoms.  Then, if you see symptoms that clearly indicate danger, such as vomiting of bile, convulsions, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, syncope, acute fever, swelling of the eyes and redness of them, and similar symptoms indicating serious complications, without a doubt, it is not easily healed.
And if you see no unusual symptoms, and you expect recovery, then you begin its treatment.
The reason is that if the injured person comes to you at the beginning of the injury, and this is during the winter days, then you must study the condition of the bone before the fourteenth day, according to all circumstances.
And if it is in the summer days, then you must be careful to examine the bone before the seventh day, before what is under the bone is corrupted by the panniculus (tissue), and before the complications we have mentioned occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the pannicle covering the brain, and is with small and comminuted fragments, then you must incise the comminuted and crushed part, as I will explain to you.
And so you shave the head of the injured patient, and expose the bone according to custom and manner as best you can, according to the nature of the wound, and that it be light on the patient.
And if you encounter during the examination of the bone, a flow of blood or a hot abscess, then you must oppose it with what is necessary, and that is to fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil, and let the wound rest until the abscess is sedated and you are sure of the blood flow; and then begin with the surrounding of the bone and its removal.
And this should be according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you incise the bone with a narrow incisor, and this is its shape:  And then apply another incisor, this one, a little wider than that one; and this is its shape:
And then apply yet wider incisors than the second.
And it is necessary that you have several different incisors, some of which are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others and are sharpened at the ends, and are made of Indian iron or steel.
And use them carefully, striking the incisor so that you do not break the head and damage it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then you must perforate its circumference before you apply the incisors with the drills, which are called shallow drills.
And they are not called thus except because those do not reach the bone of the skull, because the drill has a round end or edge below its sharp head, similar to a small edge or circle, which prevents it from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take a large number of these drills, each of which is suitable for the thickness of each bone, so that you have one for every part of the skull, the tip of which is sharp, its length and shortness being according to the thickness of that part of the skull.
And this is the form of three types of drills: The method of perforating the circumference of the broken bone is to place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone is penetrated; then move the drill to another place, and place them so that the distance between each hole is the width of a finger or thereabouts.
And then incise with incisors that which is between each hole in the bone, and make it as thin as possible, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with some other instrument that you have prepared for this purpose, such as thin tongs and forceps.
And it is necessary that you take great care that the incisor does not touch any part of the brain.
And so, when you have encircled the bone by perforation, and it is separated from the brain if it is connected to it, and you have extracted it, you must scrape and smooth its roughness, both what is on the bone and what is within the bone, with another instrument similar to an incisor, but it must be thinner and finer than the other incisors.
And if some small and jagged bone fragments remain, then remove them easily with one of the instruments; and then treat the wound with liniments and ointments that we have mentioned.
And another method of operation is easier and less frightening, which Gualia described and praised greatly; and this is his account: it is necessary for you at the beginning to begin by uncovering the part of the bone at the location where the fracture is most severe and most evident, until, when you have uncovered that part and placed under it the end of that slow and gentle incisor of which this is the form:
And it is so that the lenticular part is smooth and does not cut anything, and the sharp part is divided into two edges lengthwise, like a chisel; so that the lenticular part is fitted to the brain, with its sharp edge facing the bone.
And then strike the incisor on one side with a small mallet until you have incised all the bone easily as it is curved, and you are sure of the panniculus [a layer of the body] that no accident occurs to it in any way, even if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, even if he were sleeping.
Then, if anything remains attached to the bone of the panniculus in some parts of the bone, incise it with the lenticular end of that incisor, and remove it easily, for it is removed without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the panniculus, and what is cut away or removed from the bone is its surface, and there remain roughness and small splinters, then it is necessary to scrape away that roughness and remove those splinters with fine rasps, of which you have prepared a number of different sizes, so that it is possible for you to use in each place those most suitable and those most appropriate according to the way in which you conduct your operation on that broken bone and its form.
And it is necessary that you proceed in your scraping, with which you scrape the bone, beginning with the broadest of those rasps, and then use progressively the finer ones, and do not cease doing this continuously until you come to using the finest and most subtle of all those.
But in all other small, capillary fractures and subtle fractures, it is necessary to administer treatment according to what you apply to their rectification.
And it is a matter that is not hidden from all those who have studied this art. And I stand by what we have shown written above concerning large fractures.
And if the panniculus (muscle layer) is retracted when you scrape the bone, then it is necessary that you take a cloth according to the size of the wound, and submerge it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound; and then take another doubled or tripled cloth and submerge it in wine and rose oil and place it over the thin cloth and do this as lightly as you can so that you do not injure the tissue; and then administer a broad bandage over that, and do not tighten it except to the extent that it holds the wound alone.
And leave it with this dressing for one or two days until you are sure of a hot abscess; and then unbind it and then administer medicines which draw out superfluous moisture, such as flaxseed, barley flour,  powder of lentils and birthwort, and similar things.
And it is so that you make a powder of these medicines that is sprinkled on the wound, once they are dried; and universally, administer to it any medicine whose property is to cleanse and not to bite (cause further irritation).
And take care, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment is clean; and that there is no dirt or anything else in it; and do not allow virulence to increase in it; for when it is applied to the pannicle (membrane) of the brain, it corrupts it and rots it, and from this a malignant illness befalls the patient.
And it happens in the tissue of the head, as the bone is cleaned of it, and especially when its treatment is neglected, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore consider then, if the accidents that we have mentioned befall the patient, know that it is perilous without a doubt.
And if blackness does not occur except from the medicine that is placed on top, and it is in the virtue of that medicine to produce that blackness, then it is necessary that you take one part of honey and three parts of rose oil, and with these moisten a cloth, and then place it over the area; and then treat it with the remedies that are suitable until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Concerning the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except in two superior parts together, or one of those sometimes, because in it are two bones, because the lower part is cartilaginous, and it is not broken and nothing happens to it but abrasion and crookedness and sinuosity.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then it is necessary that you insert your little finger into his nose, and that you adjust that fracture inside with your index finger, and with your thumb outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And let this be done by you easily, and be careful that you do not cause the patient pain through your operation.
And if the fracture is in the uppermost part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it is necessary that it be set with the end of a thin rod.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the same for both.
And take care to restore it on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then around the seventh or tenth day you should treat the resulting abscess with cautery.
And then insert into the end of the nose a small roll of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part, or two small wedges if the fracture is in two parts, and let the wedge be of sufficient size to fill the opening in the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers say that the wedges should be soaked in a decoction and changed every day, but that is not my practice; rather it is necessary that the roll be soaked in egg white mixed with powdered barley; and then leave the wedge in place until the bone is set and the cartilage is hardened.
And sometimes, instead of a wedge, a goose feather, wrapped in linen cloth, is inserted into the nose; and this is done when the nasal fracture is severe and the patient is not prevented from breathing.
And this method is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do this, and if you wish, use wedges.
And if another hot abscess occurs, plaster the nose with cerate or with cotton soaked in sour wine and rose oil, or with diachylon.
But if a hot abscess does not occur, then it is necessary to plaster the outside with sesame flour and cooked lentil powder mixed with egg white; and then place linen over it, and do not bind the nose with anything.
But if the nasal bones are broken with a small fracture or are comminuted, then it is necessary to make an incision over them, and extract them with instruments suitable for this; and then close the incision, and treat it with what causes granulation and consolidation, and with plasters suitable for this.
And if a wound occurs inside the nose, then it is necessary to treat it with wedges and administer lead cannulae until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
On the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, observe then if the fracture is only external and is not broken in two and is bent inwards, then its diagnosis is easy.
And it is necessary, therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, that you insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert the index finger of the right hand; and push with it the convexity of the inward fracture towards the outer parts, with your other hand on the outside of the bone; thus properly equalizing it.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary that you administer extension from two sides according to straightness until its equalization is possible.
And if concussion of teeth occurs with disruption, then bind those which you hope will remain with gold or silver wire or silk; and then place a plaster over the broken jaw, and then place a doubled cloth over it, and place over the cloth a well-made large splint, or a piece of sole leather equal to the length of the jaw; and then bind that above according to how the binding appears to you and as the application suits, until it is healed.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and give him food of sorbitive leons. (Note: "sorbitive leons" requires further research to determine the precise meaning.  It likely refers to a type of easily digestible food.)
And if you estimate that the shape is already altered in some way, then you should consider and unbind it on the third day; and then rectify that which is already altered and plaster it with powdered barley, with egg white, or with bean flour, after you have removed the plaster, and place wool over the plaster.
And then, when this plaster is attached and fitted over it, and the position of the bone is not altered, leave it, do not unbind it until it is healed and the fracture is firm; for many times this fracture is fixed in three weeks.
And if another time a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times in its sedation until it is healed, and let that abscess be softened/matured. (The meaning of "remaugut" is inferred based on context; it implies a process of allowing the abscess to mature or break down before draining.)
And if the fracture is with a wound, then consider if a piece of the bone is separated or splintered; then you should carefully remove those splinters with whatever is suitable for that in the removal of them from the instruments. (The tools used are implied by "sturmentz".)
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you should widen it with a fleam (or lancet) according to your needs.
And then, when you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains of them, then stitch the opening of the wound if it is wide; and if not, place on it one of the plasters which is suitable for this and it will fill in that area, until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
Concerning the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Often the clavicle is broken in the front part above the muscle, and this fracture is done in one of three ways.
Because it is either broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without splinters; and it is easy to restore it; or it is such that splinters occur in it, and it is difficult to restore it; or it is such that the fracture is with a wound.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds the splint and sets the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck towards that part.
Then bind the fracture with your fingers until its shape is made as it should be, and there is neither small prominence nor concavity in it.
And if you need more extension, then it is necessary that you place under the patient's armpit a roll of cloth or wool, and let its size be according to the amount of your need; and extend and elevate the clavicle and compress the roll with your hand until you bind the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot pull the end of the clavicle to the outer parts, because it has reached the acromion, then it is necessary that the patient be supine on his neck, and you place on his muscle a moderately sized cushion.
And the assistant presses the muscle towards the lower parts until the bone of the clavicle that is on the acromion above is elevated, and then you rectify the fracture and bind it with your fingers.
But if you feel splinters of the clavicle and they are bothersome, then it is necessary that you make an incision over it and extract those splinters easily.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you carefully make an incision with one of the scalpels that you have prepared for this, after you have placed under the clavicle the instrument that supports the chin; and this instrument is of wood or iron, of which this is the form:
And it is similar to a spoon of which this is the concavity; and let its width be according to your need, according to the size of the bone or its smallness.
But its length should be as possible for the operation; that it has two ends like you see, and one end is wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound which you incised when you extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and there is a risk of a hot abscess, then you join the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a hot abscess, then you fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's opening.
And if a hot abscess occurs, then dip a cloth in sour wine and rose oil and wine, and place it on that.
And ensure that the splint does not become dislodged from the broken bone; and for that it is necessary that you see the patient each day, because when the ligament is softened or you see the splint is becoming dislodged, then you correct that and tighten the bandage.
Then place the patient's arm along their neck, and place a small cushion under their ankle at night, under their arm; so that with this, their injured limb is elevated, and therefore the fracture of the collarbone is elevated from the muscle; or let their arm be tied to their neck.
And do not untie the bandage unless there is a significant swelling or abscess in the area, before twelve days have passed.
And then renew the plaster if you see the need, and adjust the binding, and restore it and leave it until the fracture of the collarbone is restored and coagulated.
And it is strengthened and healed in about 28 days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The shoulder blade is broken on its side and not shattered, only the edges [are broken].
And so when the location of the break — and it is broken in the middle of the shoulder blade — is unknown except by touch, then according to the nature of the fracture, prepare it and reduce it to its natural form in every way possible to you.
Then place on the area powdered finely ground albumen with lion's wool; and then place a powder of folded cloth; and then place over it a thin linen bandage according to the size of the whole shoulder blade or a little more.
But if the bandage is concave in any part of the shoulder blade, then you fill that concavity with lion's wool, and then tighten the bandage evenly.
And then bind it above with a long bandage with decent constriction and firmness of the bandage until it is not moved from its place.
And examine the bandage each day; and every time that it is loosened, bind it again and tighten it, and adjust the bandage if it has moved from its place.
And lay the patient on his/her healthy side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in 20 days or in 25.
And so when this number is complete, then you dissolve the bandage, and you should know for certain that this is of the bones whose beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if it proceeds from the location of the collarbone and extends below the clavicle, then you incise over it, and remove it, and proceed as we have said in the fracture of the clavicle, with cauterization of the abscess if it occurs there.
Chapter 7.
On the restoration of a fractured foot.
The foot is sometimes broken in its middle part, and that heals slowly; but its extremities heal much faster because they are splintered and comminuted.
And among the accidents when the middle of the foot is broken is that it inclines towards the inner parts, and great pain occurs, and difficulty in breathing, and cough, and strong fever and vomiting of blood; and the cavity in the broken bone is evident and not hidden from feeling.
And its restoration is that the patient should be supine on his back, and a cushion placed between his shoulders; and then press on his muscles, and adjust his bones with the hands from both sides, and be skillful in the operation and in the setting, in every way that is possible and appropriate, until the shape of the bone is restored as it should be.
Then apply a plaster and lint, and place over it a splint of thin willow or lime wood, or similar in lightness, after you wrap it in cloth; and be careful in binding this over the broken bone so that it is not loosened; and use a bandage in a circular manner around the dorsum sometimes, and tighten it with a decent constriction; and observe the bandage at all times, and when it is loosened, tighten it.
And if necessity demands loosening of the bandage because of corrosion or itching, or pain, or an abscess that occurs in the area, then you should carefully untie it and remove the plaster and rectify what has happened by means of treatment; and then reapply the plaster, if it seems appropriate; and adjust the constriction until it is healed.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs only happens in the thicker parts, which are two; and in their extremities, on the front part,  damage does not occur because they are cartilaginous. And its diagnosis is not hidden from the sense of touch, through investigation with the fingers.
And the restoration should be thus: equalize the fracture with the fingers as much as possible, until it is aligned as needed, and then plaster and bind the broken bone with a bandage, if that is necessary.
And if the rib fracture is inclined towards the inner parts, then the patient experiences vehement pain and a stabbing sensation, like the stabbing pain that occurs in someone with pleurisy, because the bone pierces the membrane; and he experiences difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting much blood; and this is difficult to cure.
And already the Ancients were ingenious in that matter with many inventions.
And of them it is said: it is necessary that you give the patient foods that produce inflation and flatulence, so that the belly is inflated and distended, and the fracture is pushed outwards.
But we would avoid this so that it does not lightly cause a hot abscess, if it does not already exist; and if it already exists, then it helps in that.
And some of them said that a cupping glass should be placed on top and that it should be forcefully sucked; and this is similar to reasoning, but nevertheless it is feared that the cupping glass will draw superfluities to the place because of the weakness.
And some of them said: the place must be covered with wool that has been submerged in hot oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is filled, so that the bandage is equal to the circumference it encloses; and further, the patient should be treated with a treatment for pleurisy, with diet and medicine.
And if a violent cause constricts the patient, which he cannot bear, and the bone pierces the skin with a lacerating puncture, we fear for the patient, then it is necessary to cut open the place, uncover the broken rib, and then place on top of it an instrument that preserves the [bone] splinter (the mention of which has preceded), and that we incise the bone easily, and extract it.
And then we should approximate the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with stitching, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an internal abscess occurs, then you should take and mix powders in rose oil and place [the mixture] on the area, and the patient should be treated with what is appropriate for an internal abscess.
And let him lie supine on the side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the spine and neck.
Sometimes a fracture occurs in the bones of the neck, and then it leads to their attrition, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then consider this: for if you see his hands already softened and numb and dead, and he cannot move them, nor extend nor clench them, and when you constrain them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and he feels no pain in them, then know that he is not healed, and in most dispositions, it is mortal.
And if he moves them and feels constriction in them with the fingers and puncture, then know that the marrow of the bone is saved and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if the same thing happens to the vertebrae of the neck and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then observe his feet.
Then, if you see that they are already softened, the same thing has happened to them as to the hands, and then when he lies on his back, he experiences flatulence and excretion without his will, and sometimes he lies on his back and wants to urinate and cannot, then know that it is mortal, and then do not work on his cure.
But if none of those things occur, the condition is better and milder.
And the treatment is that you should perforate the inflamed boil, then place on the affected, rubbed vertebra plain rose oil, or with boiled egg yolks; apply this several times a day until the inflamed boil is reduced.
Then apply one of the comforting and stimulating plasters, and bind it tightly; and instruct the patient to rest and remain calm and not to sleep on the side where he feels pain until he is healed.
And if, due to the attrition of the bone, a splinter or something similar has separated from it, then you must clean the wound and remove that bone; and then bring together the two edges of the wound with sutures if it is large; and then treat it with some incarnative ointment until it is healed.
But if the posterior part of the hip bone is broken, and it is the end of the coccyx, then you should insert the little finger of your left hand into the anus, and with your other hand adjust the broken bone as much as possible according to the appropriate technique; and then apply a plaster and bandage, if you deem it necessary, and then bind it tightly.
And if you feel splinters broken inside, then remove them and treat the wound with the previously described treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
On the restoration of the hip.
Hip bones are rarely broken, and if they are, the fracture is usually only a separation at their extremities, and they are split lengthwise and incline inwards; and the patient experiences pain and throbbing at the site, and the leg is numb when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until it is over the fracture, which is its form.
And if the fracture is only at its extremity, then you bind that according to what is prepared for you by equalization, binding until it resembles its natural shape.
But if the fracture is lengthwise or inclines downwards, then you make the patient lie on their stomach until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. And then, when you have bound it, place a plaster over it, and then place a splint of wood or reed over that, and tighten it with a bandage, over which you do not fear puncture of the fracture nor displacement of the splint.
And bind the cavity with something that fills it until it receives the bandage equally, and command the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you cease the extension of it and its restoration until the hot abscess is sedated, and place on it that which sedates it as is prescribed; and then return to its restoration, and bind it as is necessary.
And if it happens in the bones that they are pierced or comminuted, then you extract them.
Chapter 11.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the humerus.
The humerus is that which is between the hip and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a wooden splint of moderate thickness according to this form:
And let two bandages be tied at the two extremities, and then it should be suspended from a high place.
And seat the patient on a stool; and then place his broken arm on the splint until it reaches his wrist, in the middle of which the curvature of the splint should be attached.
And then suspend some heavy weight from it, or the assistant should extend it to the lower parts; and then the physician should set the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture is reset as it should be.
And the other method is that the patient be lying on his back on his neck and suspend the hand from his neck with a bandage; and then command two assistants that one of them hold that which is above the fracture with his hands; and the other hold the lower part of it; and each of them should extend to his part.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you tighten above the fracture with a bandage and above that with another bandage, and each of the two assistants should extend to their respective parts.
And so if the fracture is close to the extremity of the muscle, then it is necessary that half of the bandage be placed on the wrist and the other below the fracture near the elbow.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the elbow, then it is necessary that the bandage be placed on that place and on that elbow.
Equalize that with ease and without trouble, and then when it is equalized as necessary and is applied with a good application, then tighten that unless a hot abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then leave the constriction until the seventh day, and place on it soiled wool soaked in sour wine and rose oil until the abscess subsides, and then you tighten that.
And the way to tighten that is to place a plaster over the fracture, and then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm over that support, and place your open hand on its muscle.
And place the cloth and tighten the arm over the support so that the arm is in a fixed position, unless some cause of the broken bone prohibits this.
And then use splints.
And it is necessary to place over the fracture that splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on each side.
And then tighten over the splints with the bandage which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is necessary that your bandage over the fracture site be tighter. And the longer the fracture, the less tight the bandage should be.
And so if you see the placement of the splints and the bandage as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its proper place, then you have done it correctly.
And if you fear a hot abscess, then you leave the bandage and splints in place until the seventh day, as we have said.
And then examine the bandage in all parts for three days so that neither itching nor inflammation occurs at the site; or it is forbidden for food to reach the limb due to the pressure of the bandage; and then rectify all this as we have said.
But if you are confident that none of this will happen, then do not untie the bandage until three days or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on their side, and their hands should be on their stomach.
And place over the splint a poultice full of wool evenly, and check every hour of the night and day that the shape of the broken limb is not loosened or dissolved, or that the bandage is not softened; then rectify all this with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order which we have previously mentioned, so that the food is light at the beginning until the healing has begun; and then you should thicken their food.
For it is customary for the splint and his leg to be bound for forty days; and then they should be unbound and a bath should be administered, and it should be medicated with ointments which rectify that.
And if the fracture is badly crushed, then you should not unbind the bandage and splints from it until 15 days or until two months.
Chapter 12.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two focils; of which one is small, and it is that which is next to the thumb, and the other is large and it is that which is placed under the little finger, on the lower side.
And very easily the larger focil is broken alone, or the smaller, and very easily they are broken together.
And when the small focil is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is quicker.
And when the focil is broken below, the fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller focil above, then it is convenient for the physician, during its restoration, to apply slight extension easily until it is set.
But if the large focil is what is broken, then it is necessary to apply more forceful extension.
And if both focils are broken together, then you must apply the extension much more strongly.
And it is necessary that you place the figure of the hand during its restoration and its extension stretched out on a cushion, and that the pulse of the hand be in the upper parts, higher than all the other fingers, and the auricular finger be lower than the other fingers, and the patient be lying on a flat couch.
And let there be a cushion in front of him at his height or level, so that the patient does not suffer strain.
Then let the assistant extend the arm from the lower part with his hand or with a bandage, and another assistant extend it above similarly.
And then the physician should manipulate the bone until he reduces it to the best possible position.
And if in the fracture of the bone there are splinters, then you must forcefully return all pieces to their place with your skill.
And if a separated splinter appears in him that protrudes and is not within hope of restoration, then saw it off and remove it according to the method which we have described above.
But if the fracture is with a wound, I have already dealt with this in a separate chapter, and therefore take the treatment from there.
And if an abscess occurs near the beginning of your treatment, then you apply a cloth with a cerate made of rose oil and white wax, and let it be of a medium consistency between thick and thin, and bind the cloth over it with a subtle bandage until the abscess is reduced; and then remove the cerate and apply a plaster prepared from crushed mullen with egg white, and then apply a splint; and let the splint which is placed over the fracture be more padded and firmer.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is varied depending on the major disposition, whether the fracture is in one bone or in two bones together.
Then apply your bandage over the fracture site more firmly and tightly, and wherever the bandage is more tightly applied to the parts above the fracture or to the parts below it, apply the bandage a little more loosely as was advised at the beginning of this chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and damp cloths, and not hard or rough, and the bandage with which it is bound should be of a medium thickness, neither too thin nor too thick, as we have already recommended.
And examine the limb and the bandage after a few days. And if any circumstance occurs which requires rectification, such as itching which occurs in the limb, then you should bathe the limb with warm water until that itching ceases.
And leave the limb unconstrained until it rests, and then reapply the bandage.
And if the fracture is now softened and the bone is reset and resembles the others, then rectify all this with your skill.
And further consider if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb by excessive tightness of the bandage, for then it is necessary that you loosen it a little and leave it so for a few days until nourishment flows to it, and then tighten it again.
But if the patient experiences none of the conditions we have mentioned, then the bandage should not be loosened for about twenty days or thereabouts.
And then suspend the patient's arm by its own weight for its agitating movement, and make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is healed in thirty days or in thirty-two days, or very quickly in twenty-eight days, and all this depends on the constitution and the disposition of the body's strength/healing power.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the restoration/healing of a fracture of the wrist and fingers.
Know that in the wrist and the knuckles of the fingers, fractures happen slowly, and they are not often injured, but rather suffer attrition (wear and tear).
And when a fracture or attrition occurs in the hand, then the patient should lie down and place a cushion in front of him, according to the alignment/level, and then place his hand on top of the extended cushion.
Then the assistant extends the broken bones, and the physician aligns them until they are joined with a good union/setting.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and lint, unless a hot abscess is present; and then apply a bandage on top according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is in the inner parts of the palm of the hand, then make a kind of ball of cloth, and instruct the patient to hold it with his fractured palm; then it should be secured with a long cloth.
And let the bandage be of leather with cloth in which it is softened, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and tighten it as needed.
But if the fracture is in the outer parts, then it is necessary that you place a bandage on top and another bandage on the underside of the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply the splint, in such a way that the hand is bound to the splint between the fingers with bandages.
And if a fracture occurs in any of the knuckles of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be straightened as needed and then secured with the palm; and if you wish, apply a small, straight splint so that the fracture is elevated and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger, or the index finger, or the ring finger, or the little finger, then it should be straightened and bound to the finger next to it; or all should be bound together continuously, which is better; or place a small splint on it, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and near to that, the disposition of a hot abscess and oppose—with your operation—what is necessary when any of these things happen, according to the repeated narration of the patient.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken and this is evident to the senses depending on whether it is broken at the front or the more superior part.
And its restoration is done thus: a bandage is tightened above the fracture and another below the fracture, and the patient is laid prostrate on their stomach and face.
Then the physician extends the entire bandage to its proper place according to the nature of the fracture, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is close to the hip joint, then the bandage should be close to the knee on the side of the kneecap, so that the extension pulls upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the knee, then the bandage should be placed near the knee, and the extension should be in the lower parts.
Then the physician aligns the bone with both hands until he reduces it to its natural form and the bone is joined by good conjunction; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, unless an abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess occurs there, then you leave it for a few days until the abscess has matured and subsided, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be made like this:  You should wrap a firm bandage around the fracture, and fold it two or three times, removing any excess; then wrap the leg until it reaches the ankle and the hip joint.
And insert a long piece of felt between the thigh and the leg near the knee on the lower parts, and let the ends of the felt come from the upper part of both sides, and then wrap around the leg and thigh what is superfluous of the bandage.
And then place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture, and place one splint over the leg bone.
And then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the bandage is tight.
Then begin with a firm bandage in the middle over the fracture site with three or four wraps, and the farther you get from the fracture site, the looser and softer your bandage should be.
And then extend the two ends of the felt to the lower parts until they reach the ankle, and tighten both ends of the splints on the other side so that the bandage is not moved from its place.
And then leave the bandage on, ensuring that no gangrene, hot abscess, swelling, or similar conditions occur in the limb; and if any of these conditions occur, then loosen it, and correct the problem promptly, as we have said many times before.
But if there is a piece of bone in the bone that protrudes, then you should reset it as best as you can; and if not, cover it and keep it in place, and treat it with the methods previously mentioned until it heals.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is tightened in addition to the splints being applied, as we have described for the treatment of the arm.
However, in the restoration done in this manner, the patient does not experience lameness; and if it is restored alone, without the other leg being used for support, then it is necessary for him to limp, as he always has.
And know that the thigh is held firmly for forty days, or a little more or a little less, and all this depends on the variety of constitutions and other conditions.
Chapter 15.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the kneecap.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap heals slowly, but it often causes considerable attrition (or wearing away).
But if a fracture occurs, then it is only a fissure or hole in its parts, and this is with or without a wound, and on this everything depends with regard to sensation (or feeling).
And its restoration is thus: that which is broken of its parts should be aligned with splints until it is rejoined and united as much as possible by setting, easing (or manipulation), and fabrication (or splinting).
Then apply a plaster and place a round splint over it, if you need one; and bind it above with a bandage appropriate to it.
And then consider all the measures which we have recommended in other fractures, such as a hot poultice and similar things, so that every accident is countered with what is suitable for him until he is healed.
Chapter 16.
Of the restoration of a leg fracture.
Know that in the leg there are two bones, of which one is large, and is called by the name of the tibia (shinbone); and the other is slender, and is called the fibula.
And the same types of fractures occur to each of these bones as occur in the two bones of the arm, and therefore its restoration is done in the same way as the restoration of the arm, and its operation is the same.
And if the two bones are broken together, the leg is deformed in all parts.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is deformed in the front parts.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it below, and it is among those things that are not easily seen/revealed to you.
Then administer extension, setting (reduction), and bandaging of the splints according to that same manner/method.
However, if the leg fracture is large, having many splinters, the extension should be less and gentler, and relieve it with your skill.
And in the leg, there is a certain method of operation similar to that on the arm. When you align the splints and complete your operation, you will then take pieces of pine wood, which are placed under the bandages that are applied in layers, and are usually made of palm branches; choose those of small thickness, and not the thin ones, and let their length be according to the length of the leg, from the knee to the very bottom.
Then wrap each one in two cloths, according to its length, and place one on one side of the leg and the other on the other side, from the knee to the lowest part of the foot.
Then bind the two splints in three places: at both ends and in the middle. For with this binding, the leg is prevented from bending to the right or left, and remains in a good position.
And now a small wooden splint, according to the length of the leg, has been applied; and let the leg be placed on it so that it conserves its position.
And especially you should do this in a fracture, and when there is an open wound.
Then examine the leg each day and study it carefully, and watch it for abscesses, inflammation, or other similar problems; and if any of these problems occur, then treat it as necessary until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the fracture of the talus and the bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture of the whole talus does not often occur; but fractures of the bones of the foot do occur sometimes; and fractures of the toes rarely occur, and only attrition (wear) occurs to them in cases of ill-disposition (i.e., poor alignment or healing).
And if a fracture occurs to the bones of the foot and sometimes those bones are elevated above each other, then have the patient place their foot on the ground prepared as before.
Then place one foot on what is elevated among those bones, and then press down on those bones, and squeeze them until they return to their places.
Then apply a plaster and tow over it, and place under the sole of the foot a small board to which two flat pieces are attached.
Then bind that tightly to the foot with a descending bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths and other necessary things.
And when three or four days have passed, then untie the bandage, for you will find the bones joined, whether they were broken or dislocated, and the operation on them is as you know.
But if some say they are broken, then restore those, and join them as I have recounted to you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then apply a splint to the broken finger according to its length, and let it be a little longer than the finger; and place under the foot that board which I have mentioned to you, and bind it with a proper bandage.
But if what is broken of the bones is two or three or more, then you place on each finger a splint of its size wrapped in linen cloth, and bind the board to the bottom of the foot, and let it rest on one of the ends of the board beyond the belly of the foot so that it holds it with good retention.
And it is convenient for you that you do not forget to consider all the things which I have told you in the other fractures of the accidents that we have mentioned; and oppose yourself to every accident with what is suitable.
Chapter 18.
Of the fracture of the woman's hip and of the pubic bone and of the man's penis.
When the hip of the woman is broken, then make her lie down on her back, and gently incline her towards one side, and let her be held from behind.
And then the midwife fills the hip with cotton, and it should be made into a ball in her hip; and then the woman should be held, and her buttocks raised little by little, because all this is useless until the broken part of the hip is positioned as a ball and the broken bone returns to place.
And then place powders on her buttocks, and when she wants to urinate, remove the cotton easily and let her urinate, and reduce and reposition it according to the manner in which you initially placed it, and then return it to its first position.
And do this for seven days or thereabouts, for then it will be healed.
And if you wish, take a pig's bladder and attach a reed tube to its opening; and introduce the entire bladder into her hip, and then inflate it through the tube until the bladder is inflated inside the hip, for this will help the fracture to set.
And then it should be filled with cotton and remain in place for several days, as we have recommended, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the pubis of a man or woman is fractured, then administer for its restoration and healing what we have recommended for a hip bone fracture.
And that which is right is not hidden in these nearby fractures which happen infrequently, especially to one who studies and uses and understands this my book well; for that fracture indicates to you the best methods in its restoration and setting, and therefore understand well.
But in the case of a fractured penis, take the neck of a goose, and place the penis within it; and then place a cloth bandage over it, and let it be wrapped, and then let it remain for three days or thereabouts, and it will be healed.
Chapter 19.
Concerning the restoration of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
It is advisable for one who suffers a fracture with a wound, and especially if the bone is large, such as the thigh bone or the humerus, or similar, that you carefully attend to and bleed them immediately if the conditions for phlebotomy indicate it as we have said above.
And if the wound flows with blood, then it is necessary that you attend to stopping it so that you sprinkle crushed egg whites on it, if no other cause presents itself.
And then begin to set the fracture on that same day, and do not delay this, unless an abscess forms.
But if a hot abscess forms, then you should delay setting it until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides, and do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you will cause bad accidents.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin, uncovered, then it is necessary that you carefully reduce it and align it with your hands easily and with little extension.
But if extension is not prepared for you and the alignment of it with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands using this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, the length of which is seven or eight fingers, and the width of which is according to the size of the wound; and for this it is necessary for the physician to have three or four of these, according to the amount needed for this in the treatment of every kind of fracture.
And let it be round, and let it be of little thickness so that it is not bent by the pressure on it during the operation.
And let it have a sharp end which has a margin at its ends; and let the upper part of it be of increasing thickness, and from the middle of it to the parts (i.e., the handle).
And this is its form:
And it is named in Greek "labre volentz atelarem," that is, a small spatula.
Therefore it is necessary that the sharp end of it be placed raised above the end of the prominent bone.
And push that in at once until the bone returns, and it is held with some kind of bandage.
Then strive to bind the ends of the fracture together, and if the broken end of the bone is thin and does not accept the instrument well, then it is necessary that you incise the end of that bone until the instrument can grasp it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone completely with what we have recommended, then incise it with whatever is suitable from the incisors which we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in whichever way is possible for you.
And then scrape away what remains on the bone of roughness and small splinters.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient finds vehement and persistent pain, then know that the bone is not returned to its natural place; and then do this, because you will provide the patient great relief.
When your restoration of the bone is complete, then submerge a cloth in black styptic wine, especially if it is summer, and do not place on the wound a cerate or anything else containing oil, lest it cause putrefaction and corruption.
And then administer stitches at the time of your completion of the bone's restoration, and leave the wound uncovered so that you may incise the hole with forceps and use bandages according to the size of the wound.
And take great care not to constrict the wound with the fracture, for many times foolish physicians have done this, and it caused the death of their patients or a corrosive illness or suppuration, that is, gangrene. And if your constriction is light, soft, different from the constriction of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or a bad wound, and you fear some bad accidents which we have recounted, and you find pain in the area, then it is not fitting that you place stitches there, but apply a bandage of stiff cloths instead of stitches and bind it tightly with them.
And when after a day or two the wound begins to suppurate, then remove the cloth which you had placed over it with wine, and then apply poultices, ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds, such as tetrapharmacon ointment and similar ones.
And it is necessary for you to unbind the bandage and examine the wound each day, morning and evening, until it is consolidated and healed, and it is necessary that the limb be positioned in such a way that the pus flows down from it easily.
If many days pass and the wound is not healed nor is the pus discharged from it, then know that there are small pockets of pus, for which it is necessary to explore the wound with a probe. And of those pockets of pus, those that are separated, remove and extract them; those that are not separated and press on the limb and cause pain, you must incise them as far as is possible for you.
And if a swelling or ulcer occurs in the wound—that is, an abscess, or another type of corruption and putrefaction—then it is necessary to treat each one of these with what is appropriate from the treatment which is described in its chapter.
And this is what my discourse should be about and you should take note: when a large bone is fractured, elevate the limb, such as the bone of the thigh and hip, and similarly for those of the large members, and do not attempt traction or extraction, because many times death results from this; rather, leave it until it suppurates, for it will fall off by itself within about twenty or thirty days; and then treat the wound if it seems necessary to you, but if not, leave it alone.
Chapter 20.
Chapter 20: On the treatment of swelling that occurs in the aftermath of a fracture.
Often this swelling occurs after the healing of a fracture, and especially if it is near the joints, and it distorts the shape of the limb; and it quickly prevents the limb from its natural function.
Then consider if the swelling is recent and administer medicines that have a styptic effect, such as aloe, enses [likely a misspelling or variant of a similar herb], myrrh, cerecloth (or a similar binding material), and acacia, and similar things; so that you take some or all of these, and mix them with styptic wine, or with egg white, or with sour wine, and apply this to the swelling on a cloth, and bind it tightly.
And leave the bandage tight; do not loosen it for many days; then loosen it and apply another, until the swelling is reduced; or bind a thin lead plate over it, because lead has the property of reducing anything swollen in the limbs.
But if the swelling has already become hardened and constricted and necessity requires its removal carefully, then incise it in its most prominent part, and incise the excess and prominence, or scrape it with a sharp instrument until it is reduced; and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Chapter 21. Of the restoration of a fracture when it is set and afterwards the limb remains thin and weak, not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is set, and afterwards the limb remains thin and weak, this is caused by many things: one of which is excessive loosening of the ligaments and their binding not as it should be; or by excessive tightness of the bandages so that nourishment is prevented from flowing to the limb; or by excessive superfluous embrocation; or by excessive movement not at the proper time; or by a deficiency of blood in the patient's body and by weakness of the patient.
And the cure for this is to give the patient nourishing food and to make his heart cheerful so that his blood is increased; and administer baths; and introduce joy and gladness to him.
And then apply a poultice to the limb, so that the poultice draws much nourishment to it, or continue embrocation with warm water, until nourishment flows and returns to its natural form.
Chapter 22.
Chapter 22. Of the treatment of fractured bones when they are set crookedly and the proper operation is prevented.
When, in a limb already healed from a fracture, there is a crookedness and prominence at the fractured bone and swelling, and because of this a deformity of the limb, but nevertheless the limb is not prevented from its natural function, then you should not accept the word of someone who claims that limb is newly fractured.
But there have been many foolish physicians and bone-setters who have done this in our land; and this operation is very blameworthy, leading to great fear, the sign of which is mortal.
But if the lameness and swelling are recent, then they should be bathed with lukewarm water in which softening herbs are boiled, such as mallow leaves and roots, melilot, and similar things; and a softening plaster should be applied, such as well-made diachylon; or a mucilage of mallow root should be made and mixed with white clay or olive oil, and applied as a plaster; or ripe figs should be crushed with pigeon dung, and similar things from among the medicines named above for consolidation.
And sometimes it is resolved with frequent, gentle friction with moist hands, and movement of the limb in every direction should be administered at all times.
But if the crookedness has become established and is strong and has turned to bone, and it becomes necessary to treat it with iron, then the excess should be removed, and the continuity of the bone dissolved, and that which superfluously swells with the bone should be incised with fine scalpels, and relief should be provided with care and skill.
Chapter 23.
Concerning the treatment of dislocations.
A dislocation is the displacement of one of the joints from its place, whereby movement is prevented, the shape of the limb is distorted, and great pain and injury are caused to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation occurs in someone, then it is necessary to strive immediately that it be reduced, and not delay that reduction, because if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed and very quickly the reduction of the dislocation is made difficult.
And for this reason, it should not be moved or stretched at the time of inflammation, because many times it causes spasms and excruciating pains.
But when this happens, then you must attend to the phlebotomy of the patient, and then let it rest until the inflammation subsides; and then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil, and then it should be reduced with ease.
And every limb should be treated with that which will bring back its proper function.
And I have now ordered the chapters on dislocation according to what preceded in fracture; from the uppermost part of the body to the lowermost part.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
If the two jaws are dislocated, it will not be long; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are moved from their places by slight displacement, and are slightly softened; or they are dislocated by complete and total dislocation, until they are softened at the point of the joint, until the saliva of the patient flows and he cannot hold it nor close his mouth, and his tongue is impeded in its speech.
But when his dislocation is slight, then in most instances it returns by itself with little intervention.
But if the dislocation is complete and total, then it is necessary that reduction be administered to him lightly, and not delayed at all.
And it is so that the assistant holds the head of the patient, and the physician inserts the thumb of one hand into the root of the dislocated jaw inside his mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or that he inserts both thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hands are outside with which he pulls; and he commands the patient to relax his jaws and allow them to be moved from each side; and the physician pulls the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, and especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then administer evaporation with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both is made easier and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
