And then, when they are returned and pulled together, and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then place on them powders of cloth with cerate, and it should be made of wax and rose oil; and then it should be tied lightly with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and his head should be placed between two pillows, so that it does not lean to the right or left; and do not force him to chew anything; before his food should be softened, until when the pains are reduced and the jaws are knitted, then let him eat what he wants.
And this should be administered with ease, and he should not work on opening his mouth during eating and drinking and speaking, until the jaw is knitted and healed.
And if the reduction of the two jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at some time and are not returned to their places, then many times fevers and continuous thirst occur, and the patient's belly is easily bloated, and he easily vomits pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is perilous; and many times he for whom this happens dies within ten days.
Chapter 25.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and of the extremity of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated inwards because of its connection with the ribs; and sometimes it is dislocated outwards, and this is evident to the touch.
And its restoration is that the patient lie on his back and extend his arms. Then constrict the area with your palm with firm compression, for it will return.
Then place a plaster and powders on that and bind it tightly.
But the extremity of that which is the elbow and is continuous with it, is not dislocated in any way; then it should be reduced and bandaged according to what we have said and according to what is available to you.
Then place a plaster, powders, and a bandage on it, and instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until it is healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that a muscle is dislocated in only three ways, one of which is that it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated from the upper part of the muscle, and this is done slowly; and it is not dislocated in the back parts by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated in the front parts by the nerves.
And it is mostly dislocated and protrudes outwards in the lower parts below the armpit, and especially in those whose flesh is thin, it is easily dislocated; but in those in whom there is much flesh, it comes out with difficulty.
And it quickly happens to some men through a blow or fall, and their muscle is inflamed by a hot abscess, and it is believed to be dislocated because of this.
Then it is necessary that you examine this carefully before anything else, and then you proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation is known when it is in the lower parts below the armpit; so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, because you find a manifest difference between them; and you find in the head of the muscle a concavity and below the armpit, towards the touching of the head of the muscle, like an egg, and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it through all movements.
If it is dislocated below the feet or above, and you then find a clear protrusion, and it is not hidden.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easily done when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the minister raises the patient's hand to the upper parts; and then places his hands below the patient's armpit, and raises the joint firmly upwards to its place, and the minister raises the patient's hand and extends it upwards; and then places it below because it returns lightly.
But if by what we have said it does not return, and from the beginning that the dislocation happened, many days pass, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water and a poultice be applied that softens and lubricates, such as boiled roots of marshmallow, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and it should be applied.
And then the patient should be supine on his back and a wool pad be placed under his armpit, that is equally between softness and firmness, and then the physician places his heel on the pad, and raises the head of the muscle with force, and pulls the patient's hand so that it is not moved downwards, and another minister holds the patient's head, so that it is not moved downwards, for then it immediately returns to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this method: it is that you have present a man who is taller than the patient, and stands on that side, and inserts his shoulder under the patient's armpit and raises the patient's armpit to the upper parts until the patient is suspended in the air, and another minister pulls the patient's hand from the lower parts of his belly. But if the patient is light, then it is necessary that something else be suspended with him, so that it weighs him down, for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is reduced easily according to another method: and it is that a long wooden post be fixed in the earth, the head of which is round in shape, like a pestle of a mortar, that is neither thick nor thin, and then it is placed under the patient; after being placed on top of the post soft cloths, and the patient is standing according to the length of the post, and then his hand is extended downwards on the other side; and his body is stretched on the other side with strength, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
And if its reduction is difficult for all the reasons we have said, then use this treatment: and it is that you take a stick the length of which is two braccia, and its width is four digits, and its thickness is lessened at the end near the armpit according to this form:
And then soft cloths are tied around the round head so that the stick does not injure the patient.
And then it is placed under the armpit of the patient, and the hand or arm is extended over the stick down to the lower parts, and the stick is tied over the shoulder and arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then the arm is placed on a rung of a ladder according to its width, and the hand is extended to the lower parts, and the rest of the body is suspended on the other side, for the joint enters immediately.
And when its entry is complete in whatever way possible for you, then you should place under the armpit a ball of wool of equal size; and then apply a plaster prepared from powdered comfrey and egg white over the entire muscle, as it is rolled up, and then tighten it under the armpit with a decent bandage; and the bandage should be rolled over the plaster, and his hand tied to his neck, and left, and his hand should not be moved for seven days.
And it is necessary that the patient's food be limited until the limb is strengthened, because it is easier for its healing.
And then the food should be gradually increased to seven or five, and movement should be exercised; and then if it is firm and not softened, then it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times due to humidity affecting it or another cause, then cautery with three punctures should be administered to it, as previously stated in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done and after seven days the bandage is untied and the joint is not fixed, and the plaster and the bandage are applied to it several times, and it is not fixed and it falls and he cannot raise that arm to the upper parts, then know that his nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, are either cut or stretched or softened, and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
Regarding the dislocation that occurs between the foot and the ankle or the rear parts, the reduction is done with impulse and extension with the hands, until it returns [to its place].
And let another bandage be applied to that [area], and cure it until it is healed.
And if heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement occur near the healing, then the patient should use baths many times until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its former nature.
Chapter 27.
On the treatment of elbow dislocation.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, but mostly forward and backward.  The dislocation is not hidden from you, for it is evident to the sight or touch, depending on how it is dislocated. When you compare the dislocated elbow with the sound one, this is manifestly clear to you, for the joint is hollowed and cannot bend the arm, nor can it touch its muscle with it.
And it is necessary that you take care, and reduce the dislocation immediately before a hot abscess develops, for if a hot abscess develops, its reduction is difficult, and it is not easily healed completely; and especially when it is in the backward parts, for it is worse than all other types of dislocation, and more painful, and often causes death with it.
And its restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the patient's hand should be extended with two hands, and his arm expanded, and the doctor's hands should be on and under the elbow, and he should pull the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the root of his palm until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the forward parts, then return it thus: bend the hand with your hand, until you strike the elbow with the root of your palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer strong and very forceful extension.
And it is so that two assistants extend the arm, and two other assistants hold that extension so that it is not moved during the extension.
And then the arm should be rotated in every direction, after it is wrapped in a long doubled cloth over his hand, or a bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint that hand with some oil so that it may lubricate the joint more easily; and then push the joint with strong pressure until it returns [to its place].
And after the reduction, it is necessary that he be placed upon a plaster which has styptic and drying properties with egg white, and it should be bound tightly with a descending bandage, and the arm should be suspended from the neck, and left like that for some days.
And then it should be unbound; and then if the joint is formed in its place, then you untie his bandage and leave it so.
And if you see the joint not well bound, then you repeat the plaster and bandage, and leave it for some days until it is constricted; and then unbind it.
But if it happens that there is pain after the reduction of the joint, and his disposition is towards movement, then administer moistening in a bath and gentle friction, and compression, until he is relieved; or place on the joint an emollient, that is, something very greasy; and then it should be bandaged, and left on the bone day and night. And then remove that, and put it in a bath; and when it is dry, then you rub the joint with a temperate friction, and then again apply the emollient, that is, very greasy ointment, once, and a second time, and a third time. with the aid of a bath, until it is cleansed.
And if you wish, place on it a warm, moist cow's bladder, and bind it tightly; and do this several times, for it is moistening and reduces [the swelling] to its proper state.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated wrist, that is, of the joint that is between the arm and the hand.
A dislocated wrist is sometimes easily reduced, unlike other joints; however, speed is necessary in its reduction at the time it is dislocated, before the area becomes inflamed or a hot abscess forms there.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the patient's wrist joint on a table, and the assistant extends the hand, and the physician places his hand or palm on the prominence of the joint and manipulates it until it returns to its place.
However, it must be considered whether the palm is already facing the belly of the hand; then the patient places the back of his hand on a table during extension and reduction. And if the dislocation is toward the back of the hand, then let the position of the hand be face down on the table so that the physician's hand can fall upon that prominence of the joint.
And so if it returns immediately, it is good; but if not, then compress it with a poultice to reduce the inflammation and leave it; and do not repeat this, for otherwise, you will not be able to manage or achieve its reduction once several days have passed.
However, if the joint remains twisted and causes the patient no pain, only that the hand is weakened and cannot grasp anything, then know that the nerves are pinched or bruised, and then there is no remedy except to cauterize near the injury and quickly apply a bandage.
Therefore, when the arm is reset, then apply the poultice we have described, and let it be applied and held for five days; then let it be unwrapped and left, and let the patient exercise their hand.
But if its movement is impeded and some heaviness occurs, then soothe it with warm water and friction several times, until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
On the treatment of finger dislocations.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in every part.
And when one of those fingers is dislocated towards the back of the palm or the belly of the hand, then stretch the finger and gently push the dislocation back with your fingers until it returns to its place.
Then support the head of the finger and hang it downwards from the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it like that for two days.
And then suspend it and extend it until it is straightened on that same day.
And when night comes, bind it according to that description, and do not cease to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for several days until it is tight [or strong/firm].
And do similarly if it is dislocated on the palm of the hand, and bind it to that part and perform this your first operation until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated in other parts.
Chapter 30.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation happens to one of the vertebrae of the back or neck, and many vertebrae are dislodged from their places, then in that case there is no cure, for death threatens the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's excrement is expelled involuntarily, and he cannot retain it; and many times some of his limbs are paralyzed, either both legs or both arms, or one of them; or a vertebra is dislocated, for many times it is dislocated and many times the dislocation occurs in four parts.
Therefore, that is dislocated in the posterior parts and is called paralysis.
But the treatment for him is that you then consider if the paralysis comes from a fever, because then in that case there is neither cure nor complete healing of it.
But in that which comes from a fall or from a blow or similar causes, the Ancients already described the methods of cure with lengthy discourses, of which the most returns with profit; but I have already abbreviated that, of which the little excuses the excess of it, for my nearness to my intended explanation, with which I have expounded and formed this from diverse instruments of that with which I will show this and expound that.
And of those, only that which occurs in the two properly is cured, with what I have recounted.
And it is that the patient be extended on his face on a soft, even surface near a wall, and a soft, thick mattress be placed beneath him so that his feet are not hurt.
And then a straight pole should be placed, fixed in the earth at the head end of the bed near the patient's head, and another pole near his feet at the other end of the bed.
And the assistant should hold the pole, and it should not be fixed in a pit; and another assistant should hold the other one in the same manner.
Then wrap a bandage around the patient's head and below the armpits, firmly; and extend the end of the bandage to the pole which is near his head, and tie it there; and then tighten another bandage around his hips, his buttocks, and his legs; and then adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other pole which is near his feet.
Then each assistant should extend the bandages to the part of the pole, and the two poles and the bandages should not be removed from the places in which they are fixed, but nevertheless they should not be fixed as we have said before. (The last phrase likely refers to a previous instruction not to rigidly fasten the poles, implying some degree of adjustability)
And the physician should place his palms on the spine with strength until it returns to its place, or place a board on it, and then adjust his feet on the board until it returns to its place. (The "it" refers to the spine.)
But if it does not return by this method, then take a board whose width or length is about three cubits, and make a hole in the wall which we have said is near the patient, into which the end of the board will fit. Then place half of the board on the spine, and the physician should place his feet on the other end, and press firmly until the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
And if you wish, use a drill that is turned by hand; and you should place in the ground below the patient's head at the back of the buttocks two poles, whose length is one cubit, and below their head at the back of the buttocks, similarly two poles; and the distance between each pole should be one palm, and there should be a hole in each pole in which the drill fits, and each should be firmly fixed in the ground so that it is not moved.
And there should be another round pole in which the drill is, and it should be revolved in the two holes of the two poles.
And at its end is a hole into which the pole is fitted, the length of which is one palm together with that which is revolved; and in the two other poles it is similar to that.
Then tighten the ligatures that you have tied to the patient's feet in the hole that is under their head, and the ligatures that you have tied to their thighs in the drill that is under their feet.
And then, beside each drill, let a minister stand who turns the drill with his hand, and the physician sets the vertebrae as we have said.
Then, once the vertebra is returned and the location is set, you should apply a drying plaster with egg white, and then lint; and then place over the plaster a piece of linen cloth whose width is three fingers or thereabouts, and its length is sufficient to cover the swollen area and the part of the healthy vertebra.
Then it should be bound with the ligature we have mentioned, and the patient should be given light nourishment until healed.
And if any swelling remains at the end of the healing, then medicines should be administered that soften and smooth, with the application of the linen cloth for a long time, which we have recommended; and a lead plate is then applied to that area.
And sometimes swelling occurs in the area behind the spinal vertebrae, and is caused by that dislocation; and that is a bone which already swells, so that no attempt should be made to treat it with this medicine, for it will very quickly cause death.
Chapter 31.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the muscle joint suffer only dislocation, and not what happens to other joints of small movement and concavity.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inwards, outwards, forwards, or backwards.
And it is mostly dislocated inwards, and rarely forwards or backwards.
And a sign of its dislocation inwards is that, when you compare the patient's healthy leg with the affected one, the affected leg is longer and the knee more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend their foot without pain.  And it is in this location that a hot abscess usually appears, because the head of the femur has already arrived there.
And the sign of that which happens in dislocation outwards is that its symptoms are contrary to these symptoms [described above].
And the sign of dislocation forwards is that when he extends his leg fully, nevertheless he cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he forces himself to walk, he cannot do so forward; and his urine is retained, and his groin is swollen, and he walks on his heel.
And a sign that dislocation has occurred backwards is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he bend it forward before bending the hip, and his leg is shorter than the other, and the groin is softened, and the head of the femur is behind that location.
Furthermore, regarding the reduction of these types of dislocation, you should consider that if the dislocation is old and chronic, and has already become fixed, and you attempt reduction and it remains in its displaced position, then it is not curable completely; and therefore, no attempt should be made in such a case.
But in a case where the dislocation is recent, and is one of the four types of dislocation, then take care to return the joint and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left, for it will return very easily and will require no other treatment.
But, if it does not return in this manner, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong assistant and extend his leg from the lower part, either with his hands or with a bandage which is tied above the knee; and another assistant should extend the thigh from above such that he places his hands under the patient's testicles; and then tighten a strong bandage around the thigh bone, and let another assistant hold the end of the bandage taut.
And let the extension be from the inside of the groin to the furcula (presumably the perineum), or from the back to the buttocks.
And let the extension of all those [assistants] be at once, until the patient is raised with his body from the ground and hangs suspended.
And this type of extension is common to all four [types of dislocation].
And therefore, if the dislocation is reduced with what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then it is necessary for each type [of dislocation] that which we will say concerning its own cure.
In the reduction of the hip itself, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is towards the inside, it is necessary that the patient be supine on his sound side; and then place the ligaments on the root of the thigh in that place which is between the head of the thigh and the place below the groin; and then extend the ligament upwards from the groin to the highest part of the furcula (perineum).
And then another strong assistant grasps his arms, and raises the thick part of the affected thigh and extends it outwards with a vehement extension, for in that way it returns to its place.
And this type is of easier cure, with which this member is reduced, than the other types.
But if it is dislocated upwards and does not yield to treatment with this type of cure at all, then it is necessary that the patient's two feet be tied together with a strong, light bandage, above both ankles and both heels.
And let the length of each (bandage) be the quantity of four digits, and let the affected leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two digits.
And then the patient should be suspended by the head from a pole, and let him be inside the house; and let his height from the ground be the quantity of two arms' lengths.
And then command a strong young man to gently lower the head of the thigh, and let another young man be suspended with the patient, and the other young man, gently but firmly pulling, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But in the proper reduction, when it is a dislocation to the outside, it is necessary that the patient lie on his back, as we have recommended in the case of coldness; and let the bandage be tightly applied to the affected leg and to his feet.
And then two poles should be placed, one at his feet and the other at his head; and then let adjusted poles be placed in the middle of the elevation (or hump/ridge) upon which wet cloths are wrapped, so that the patient is not harmed by the pole being between his thighs, lest the extension to the lower parts is pulled.
And then each assistant extends to their part, and the physician with his hand completes the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the anterior parts, then it is necessary that the leg be extended slightly, and it is according to this same narration on a bolster; and the physician places the palm of his right hand on the dislocated joint, and then compresses that with the other hand, and with that compression he places that which is extended to the lower parts to the part of the knee.
And if the dislocation is in the posterior parts, then it is not necessary that the patient be extended to the lower parts, and he is elevated above the ground; rather it is necessary that he be placed on something hard, just as it is necessary for the elevation if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we have said about his lying on two cushions; and it is upon his face, and the ligaments being extended, as we have said, there is not much difficulty.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a tablet (or splint) on the place where the joint is.
Therefore, when the reduction is accomplished as it should be — and the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you, which is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see them equal and the patient draws his leg and extends it without difficulty, then you know that the member is returned as it should be — and then you adjust the two thighs and apply a plaster and bind it so that the hip is not moved in any direction; and let the patient remain at rest for three or four days.
And then untie the bandage and the plaster, and compare one leg with the other.
Then, if you see them equal in appearance, then you know that the dislocation is now healed, and then allow the patient to go.
And if you see any cause for softening (or loosening), then you return and plaster that, and bind it according to your first binding, and leave it for three days.
And then untie it, and have him walk about gradually for some days, until he is strengthened.
Chapter 32.
Concerning the treatment of knee dislocation.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated outwards, and inwards, and downwards more towards the back; and it is not dislocated forwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the patient to apply his leg to his thigh; then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration for all its types is that you make the patient lie down straight, so that he extends his leg as much as possible on that; adhere your two hands to his face, and place his foot between your feet; then make his knee adhere to your palms, and hold those between your fingers on his knee with firmness, and another attendant extends his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its reduction is that his leg adheres to the thigh easily and without difficulty.
And then plaster that and make the leg adhere to the thigh, and then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then unbind them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for some days, until he is healed and strengthened.
But if the reduction is difficult for you, then administer strong extension with bandages, the use of which in the treatment of the hip was previously described.
Chapter 33.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the talus (ankle bone).
The talus is sometimes dislocated by a slight movement, and sometimes completely dislocated, and its dislocation is either inwards or outwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that sometimes the talus swells, protruding towards the side to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its slight dislocation is easy reduction; it is that it should be extended easily with the hands and manipulated until it returns (to its place).
But in treating it when it is completely dislocated, it is necessary that you make the patient lie down straight, and that a strong and capable assistant hold him from behind at the middle of his back.
Then you, with your right hand, take the foot from the uppermost part, and with your left hand from the lower part of the foot at the ankle.
And then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with your left, twice, and then extend it with the left; and gently push the toes of the foot with your right hand towards the leg without effort.
And do this twice, as we have described.
And then, a third time, gently push the toes of the foot towards the leg, and you will be at the ankle.
Then it will return in one or two times according to this method, and if you see the foot straightened, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation on it, for it will return.
But if the reduction by the method we have described is forbidden to you, then have the patient lie on their back on the ground, and firmly tie a strong cloth to the ground, so that it falls between their thighs, and you have already wrapped a cloth around them so that it does not hurt the patient.
And then let the patient's attendant hold their thighs, and let another attendant extend the foot with their hands or with a bandage tied around the ankle; and then let each attendant extend against the extension of their partner, and let the cloth remain between the patient's thighs, restraining it, so that their body is not pulled downwards against their extension.
And then the physician sets the dislocation with his hands, and another attendant holds the healthy thigh downwards, because the dislocation easily returns.
And so when the dislocation returns and the success of its reduction is clear to you, then apply a plaster and bind it firmly with bandages, and bind the foot to the leg with a bandage.
And you must be careful or wary of the nerves which are behind the heel, so that the bandage over it is not too tight and severely damages them.
And then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage is loosened, tighten it.
And then unbind it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the patient from walking for forty days. But if he is forced to walk before that time, there is no certainty that the dislocation will not be loosened and corrupted, and then it will not receive a cure.
And if a hot abscess happens to him, then you must administer to its reduction that which we described earlier, in the place of a cure and embrocation, until it is healed.
Chapter 34.
On the treatment of dislocations of the toes.
It is necessary that the dislocated [toe] be bandaged with slight extension without effort; and this is not difficult, rather it is easy.
But if the dislocation is in one of the bones of the foot, then you must make the patient place his foot on level ground or on a table, and he should be positioned as if walking.
And then you stand, and place your foot on what protrudes from the joint, and then press down on that with your foot firmly until it returns, and you see that it is now bandaged and no prominence appears in that place.
Then place under the patient's foot a board that supports the entire foot, to which two ends are attached, and then tighten it with a decent and firm bandage for three days; and then untie it and have the patient rest from walking for many days, until it is firm and its reduction is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocation that are made with a wound or with a fracture or with both together.
When any of those causes occur, and you exert yourself in its cure and in the restoration of the patient, death often ensues.
And therefore it is not fitting that anyone presume upon the cure except one who is wise in the art, of long experience, merciful and compassionate, slow, not rash nor presumptuous; and that he use in the beginning medicines that reduce hot abscesses only, and leave the patient to divine judgment, except for one in whom you hope for salvation from peril, with alleviation of the disease, and in whom some hope appears. And then exert yourself immediately to reduce it at the beginning of the cause, before a hot abscess develops.
And then if the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regimen which reduces hot abscesses and treat the wound with what is suitable for it of drying ointments.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and the jagged ends of the bone are separated, then you must force their alignment, and do in this what we have said in the simple illnesses that precede this in those locations.
And guard and avoid, according to your virtue, that entry into the paths of fear as my preaching has preceded you; for this is better for you before your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more salutary to your true body in your time. Thus end the words of Albucasis perfectly, for which may God be blessed. Amen.
